The Internal Evidence of Beginning and Decay
Maturidi presents two observations leading to knowledge of temporal origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses human reflection on one's own beginning and the universal experience of decay to prove that beings are temporal.

Core Idea
We can know our own beginning and see that everything decays, proving we came from non-existence.

Maturidi begins with a point that anyone can verify: we all recall our own beginning or our change from a smaller state. He then adds that everything undergoes weakness and perishes, which forces the rational person to realize that it existed after not existing. This lesson unpacks that twofold argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two observations
The passage presents two observations: first, one's own beginning and change; second, universal weakness and decay. Find each and note how they lead to the same conclusion.
3
Reflect on personal experience
Consider how you can recall your own beginning or growth. This internal evidence is accessible to every rational person.
4
Understand the logical necessity
The passage argues that if something undergoes decay and perishes, it must have had a beginning. Ask yourself: why is that necessary?
This is the heart of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكن أبين، إذ كل ذي عقل وبصر لعلّه يذكر ابتداءه أو تقلبه من أحوال تقدمت من الصغر واللطافة، مما إذا لم يجعل لتلك الجملة ابتداء يبطل كونه. ثم احتمال كل الوهن والضعف إلى أن يتلاشى ويبطل مما يضطره إلى العلم بكونه بعد أن لم يكن. وإذا كان ذا أمر من يملك التدبير ويعلم بالأحوال فالموات الذي هو تحت تدبير الأحياء ينتفعون به من حيث لا يشعر بذلك أحق بذلك. ثم دل كون الأموات على ما للأحياء بها الاستمتاع، على أن الذي دبرها هو الذي دبر الأحياء، إذ جعلها مستمتعا لهم، بها صلاحهم.
It is clearer, since every possessor of intellect and sight may recall its beginning or its change from prior states of smallness and delicacy, such that if that whole is not given a beginning, its existence is invalidated. Then its undergoing all weakness and frailty until it dissolves and perishes compels it to know that it exists after not having existed. And if it has a matter of one who possesses disposal and knows conditions, then that which is inanimate – which is under the disposal of the living who benefit from it without it being aware of that – is more deserving of that. Then the existence of inanimate things indicates the enjoyment of living things, that the one who disposed them is the one who disposed the living, since He made them a source of enjoyment for them, and through them their well-being.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof for the temporal origin of the world and the necessity of a creator. Maturidi begins by stating that any being with intellect and sight can recall its own beginning or its transformation from earlier states (e.g., from smallness to growth). If this entire sequence had no starting point, its existence would be inconceivable. Then he points out that everything undergoes weakness and decay until it perishes, which compels the rational person to know that it came into existence after non-existence. Next, he argues by analogy: if a living being has a disposer who manages its affairs, then inanimate objects that are used by the living without awareness are even more in need of a disposer. Finally, he concludes that the fact that inanimate things serve the enjoyment and well-being of living things proves that the same disposer (God) arranged both, as He made them beneficial for us.

Key Terms
ابتداء
وهن
ضعف
تلاشي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is an argument from design, focusing on complexity and purpose.
Correction: No, Maturidi's argument here is from temporality and change, not from design. He uses the fact that beings have a beginning and undergo decay to prove they are not eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say compels a person to know that something exists after non-existence?
A) Its complexity
B) Its undergoing weakness and decay
C) Its usefulness
D) Its beauty

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses two internal experiences—recalling one's own beginning and observing universal decay—to prove that beings are temporal and require a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to remember your earliest memory, do you sense that you had a beginning? How does that awareness affect your confidence that you are not self-sufficient?
?
Look around at the things you own—your phone, your clothes, your body. They are decaying. How does the fact that everything is perishing compel you to seek the One who never perishes?
?
If you truly internalized that you came from non-existence, would you be as attached to this temporary world? What would change today?

The Analogy from Living to Inanimate for a Single Disposer
Maturidi uses the relationship between living beings and their disposer to argue for a disposer of inanimate things, affirming divine unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues from the disposal of living beings to the need for a disposer of inanimate objects, concluding both are from one Creator.

Core Idea
If living things have a disposer, then inanimate things are even more in need of one, and the same God arranged both.

After establishing that beings are temporal, Maturidi draws an analogy. He notes that if living beings have a disposer who manages their affairs, then the inanimate objects that serve them must also have a disposer. In fact, they are more deserving because they are used without awareness. This leads to the conclusion that the same God disposed both the animate and the inanimate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from living to inanimate.
2
Understand the analogy
The passage draws an analogy: if a living being has a disposer, then inanimate objects (which are used by the living) are even more deserving of having a disposer.
The term 'أحق' (more deserving) is key.
3
Note the conclusion
The passage concludes that the one who disposed the inanimate things is the same as the one who disposed the living, because He made the inanimate beneficial for the living.
4
Reflect on divine unity
This argument not only proves a Creator but also that He is one. Ask yourself: why does the benefit of inanimate things point to a single Disposer?
Because the coordination between living and inanimate suggests a single plan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is clearer, since every possessor of intellect and sight may recall its beginning or its change from prior states of smallness and delicacy, such that if that whole is not given a beginning, its existence is invalidated. Then its undergoing all weakness and frailty until it dissolves and perishes compels it to know that it exists after not having existed. And if it has a matter of one who possesses disposal and knows conditions, then that which is inanimate – which is under the disposal of the living who benefit from it without it being aware of that – is more deserving of that. Then the existence of inanimate things indicates the enjoyment of living things, that the one who disposed them is the one who disposed the living, since He made them a source of enjoyment for them, and through them their well-being.

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof for the temporal origin of the world and the necessity of a creator. Maturidi begins by stating that any being with intellect and sight can recall its own beginning or its transformation from earlier states (e.g., from smallness to growth). If this entire sequence had no starting point, its existence would be inconceivable. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though every inanimate object you use has been placed under your disposal by a single Wise Disposer? Or do you take them for granted, as if they exist on their own? Reflect on your morning routine: list three things you used without gratitude, and then this day pause to thank the One who disposed them for you.
?
If inanimate objects are 'more deserving' of a disposer because they lack awareness, what does that imply about your own need for God? Are you aware of your complete dependence on Him in every breath? When have you acted as though you were self-sufficient, contrary to this lesson?
?
The passage says inanimate things exist for the enjoyment and well-being of the living. How often do you consider that your own well-being is a sign of a single Disposer? When you experience discomfort or lack, do you turn to the Disposer or simply complain about the circumstances? What would change if you saw every object as a gift from the same God who arranged your existence?

The Flaw of Basing Theology on Sensory Experience Alone
Maturidi states the second confusion: over-reliance on senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relying solely on sensory evidence can lead to false conclusions about the origin of the universe.

Core Idea
The seen is not always a valid model for the unseen.

People often assume that the unseen world must work like the visible world. Maturidi identifies this as the second major confusion leading to theological error. He shows how this assumption spawns multiple false sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the principle: 'the witnessed is the evidence for the unseen'. This is the core assumption.
2
Understand why it is mistaken
Reflect on why analogical reasoning from sensory experience cannot be applied to the question of creation ex nihilo. The unseen may operate under different laws.
Creatio ex nihilo is a unique event not subject to empirical observation.
3
Connect to the resulting sects
Recognize that this single error leads to multiple false doctrines. This sets the stage for the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني نظرهم إلى الوجود مما يقع تحت الحواس، فوجدوه يتقلب من حال إلى حال بالمواد والأغذية، وتولد بعض عن بعض، وظنوا أن كون الأشياء لا عن شيء والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده؛ لأنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك، والشاهد عندهم هو دليل الغائب. ثم تفرقوا. فمنهم من يقول: على هذا أمر العالم في الأزل؛ لكنهم اختلفوا فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا، من غير أن يكون له صنع. وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع: إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاضلها؛ وسماها قوم هيولى. والتفاوت في الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ: إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله. وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع، والدواب من اضطرابها. وعلى هذا كل شيء. ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع، ولكن لكل جوهر أصل، والطبائع دخيلة فيه. ومنهم من يجعله كذلك بالصانع ويقول: هو واحد، ويجعله علة لكون العالم؛ فيوجب قدمه بوجوده في الأزل. ويذهب في إثبات الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء وإتقانها: إن ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدبر عليم، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدرة، وبه صلاح الأشياء، فقالوا بالصانع. ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم في الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعللها. على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه ونعمه، وأنه قادر بذاته، فثبت جوده وكرمه بذاته. فيلزم كون الذي كرمه بذاته يوجبه، وقدرته توجده. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ومنهم من يقول: هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه. لكنهم اختلفوا؛ فمنهم من يجعل أصله طينة أحدث الباري منها هذا العالم والباري يجعله قوم واحداً، وقوم يجعلونه النجوم والشمس والقمر، بما كن يجرين دائبات، وبجريهن نشوء العالم. ويجعلون للجري ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له. ومنهم من يجعلها تعترض فيها الأعراض، فمن ذلك تولد العالم؛ يسمونه من قبل هيولى، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيره من حال إلى حال. ومنهم من يقول: أصله اثنان نور وظلمة؛ من النور كل خير ونفع، ومن الظلمة كل شر
And the second [doubt] is their observation of existence under the senses: they found it changing from one state to another through materials and nourishment, and one thing generating from another, and they thought that the existence of things from nothing and branches without an origin is impossible; because they have not witnessed that, and for them the witnessed is the evidence for the unseen. Then they divided. Some of them say: the world is eternal in this manner; but they differed: some make it so without a maker. On this basis comes the school of the naturalists: that difference and variation are due to the difference and superiority of natures; some call it 'hyle'. And the variation in what I mentioned is like pigments: they produce different colors due to variation and balance of temperament. Thus they made the essence of humans from balanced natures, and animals from their imbalance, and so on for everything. And some hold that the four natures are the origin, but each essence has an origin, and natures are incidental to it. And some make it so with a maker and say: He is one, and make Him the cause of the world's existence, thus necessitating its eternity by His existence in pre-eternity. They go in establishing the maker to the harmony and perfection of things: that this can only be from a knowing manager, since nature does not refer to power, and through Him things are set right; so they affirm the maker. Then since He was in pre-eternity, they necessitated the world's existence in pre-eternity in the manner of association of effects with their causes. Since the world contains His gifts and favors, and He is powerful by His essence, His generosity and kindness are established by His essence. Thus it is necessary that what He honors by His essence necessitates it, and His power brings it into existence. And there is no power except with God. And some say: this world originated from an origin in which making occurred. But they differed: some make its origin a clay from which the Creator brought forth this world; some make it the stars, the sun, and the moon, which perpetually revolve, and by their revolution the world arises. And they make the revolution have a beginning by deeming it impossible for one thing to come from another without a first. And some make it such that accidents intervene in it, and from that the world is generated; they call it 'hyle' beforehand, and describe it as the monotheists describe the Maker, then they invalidated that by its possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state. And some say: its origin is two: light and darkness; from light every good and benefit, and from darkness every evil.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage discusses the second major source of doubt: reliance on sensory experience alone. People observed that all existing things are generated from matter, change, and are produced from one another. They concluded that creation from nothing is impossible because they never saw it, applying the rule that the witnessed is the evidence for the unseen (analogical reasoning based on sensory data). This led them to various false doctrines. Maturidi then enumerates the resulting sects: 1) Those who believe the world is eternal without a maker (naturalists). They attribute all variation to the natures of things. 2) Those who believe in four primary natures but with each essence having its own origin. 3) Those who affirm a creator but make the world eternal as a necessary concomitant of God's eternal existence (necessitarians). They argue from the harmony and perfection of the world to a wise manager, but then they err by making the world co-eternal with God, reasoning that since God is eternal, His effect must be eternal (like a cause always implies its effect). Maturidi notes that they also argue from God's generosity and power: because He is generous, He must create, and because He is powerful, He must bring into existence; thus the world exists eternally. 4) Those who say the world originated from a pre-existing matter: some say it was clay; some say stars and planets; some say an eternal hyle (matter) that receives accidents; they describe this hyle as monotheists describe God, but Maturidi points out its contingency (it receives accidents and changes). 5) Dualists who posit two eternal principles: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). Throughout, Maturidi presents each view and critiques it implicitly or explicitly, showing that all these positions stem from the mistaken assumption that the unseen must be like the seen.

Key Terms
حواس
غائب
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation from nothing is impossible because we never see it.
Correction: The unseen can be governed by different rules; the sensory world is not the only reality.

Checkpoint
What principle do the people use to deny creation ex nihilo?
A) The witness of the senses is evidence for the unseen.
B) The world is eternal.
C) Everything has a cause.
D) Nature is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Reliance on sensory analogy alone leads to theological error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted Allah's ability to do something simply because you have never seen it happen before? This week, identify one area where you rely on sensory experience more than on Allah's promises, and replace that doubt with firm reliance on His power as revealed in the Quran.
?
When you think about the Day of Judgment, do your emotions and certainty depend on visible evidence around you? How can you train yourself to make the unseen (gha'ib) more real to you than the seen (shahid)? Commit to a daily practice of reflecting on one unseen reality until it shapes your actions.
?
Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that are actually based on your limited observations rather than revelation? Ask Allah to purify your heart from false analogies, and resolve to study His names and attributes from the sources, not from your own imagination.

Sects from the Sensory Fallacy: Naturalists and Necessitarians
Maturidi presents two sects: naturalists and necessitarians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sensory fallacy leads to naturalism and the necessitarian view of a creator who necessarily causes an eternal world.

Core Idea
Denying creation ex nihilo leads to either atheism or a distorted monotheism.

Once people reject creation from nothing, they propose alternative origins. Maturidi describes two main groups: those who believe the world is eternal without a creator (naturalists) and those who affirm a creator but claim the world is eternal because God is eternal. Both are errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the passage and find the two distinct positions: naturalists (no creator) and necessitarians (creator but eternal world).
2
Understand the naturalist position
Naturalists attribute all variation to natures and view the world as eternal without need for a maker. They call the underlying principle 'hyle'.
This is a form of materialism.
3
Understand the necessitarian position
Necessitarians affirm a wise creator but argue that the world must be eternal because God is eternal. They reason from design and God's generosity.
They treat creation as a necessary effect, not a free act.
4
Notice the flaws
Maturidi implicitly critiques both: naturalism ignores teleology; necessitarianism compromises divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني نظرهم إلى الوجود مما يقع تحت الحواس، فوجدوه يتقلب من حال إلى حال بالمواد والأغذية، وتولد بعض عن بعض، وظنوا أن كون الأشياء لا عن شيء والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده؛ لأنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك، والشاهد عندهم هو دليل الغائب. ثم تفرقوا. فمنهم من يقول: على هذا أمر العالم في الأزل؛ لكنهم اختلفوا فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا، من غير أن يكون له صنع. وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع: إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاضلها؛ وسماها قوم هيولى. والتفاوت في الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ: إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله. وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع، والدواب من اضطرابها. وعلى هذا كل شيء. ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع، ولكن لكل جوهر أصل، والطبائع دخيلة فيه. ومنهم من يجعله كذلك بالصانع ويقول: هو واحد، ويجعله علة لكون العالم؛ فيوجب قدمه بوجوده في الأزل. ويذهب في إثبات الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء وإتقانها: إن ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدبر عليم، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدرة، وبه صلاح الأشياء، فقالوا بالصانع. ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم في الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعللها. على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه ونعمه، وأنه قادر بذاته، فثبت جوده وكرمه بذاته. فيلزم كون الذي كرمه بذاته يوجبه، وقدرته توجده. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ومنهم من يقول: هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه. لكنهم اختلفوا؛ فمنهم من يجعل أصله طينة أحدث الباري منها هذا العالم والباري يجعله قوم واحداً، وقوم يجعلونه النجوم والشمس والقمر، بما كن يجرين دائبات، وبجريهن نشوء العالم. ويجعلون للجري ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له. ومنهم من يجعلها تعترض فيها الأعراض، فمن ذلك تولد العالم؛ يسمونه من قبل هيولى، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيره من حال إلى حال. ومنهم من يقول: أصله اثنان نور وظلمة؛ من النور كل خير ونفع، ومن الظلمة كل شر
And the second [doubt] is their observation of existence under the senses: they found it changing from one state to another through materials and nourishment, and one thing generating from another, and they thought that the existence of things from nothing and branches without an origin is impossible; because they have not witnessed that, and for them the witnessed is the evidence for the unseen. Then they divided. Some of them say: the world is eternal in this manner; but they differed: some make it so without a maker. On this basis comes the school of the naturalists: that difference and variation are due to the difference and superiority of natures; some call it 'hyle'. And the variation in what I mentioned is like pigments: they produce different colors due to variation and balance of temperament. Thus they made the essence of humans from balanced natures, and animals from their imbalance, and so on for everything. And some hold that the four natures are the origin, but each essence has an origin, and natures are incidental to it. And some make it so with a maker and say: He is one, and make Him the cause of the world's existence, thus necessitating its eternity by His existence in pre-eternity. They go in establishing the maker to the harmony and perfection of things: that this can only be from a knowing manager, since nature does not refer to power, and through Him things are set right; so they affirm the maker. Then since He was in pre-eternity, they necessitated the world's existence in pre-eternity in the manner of association of effects with their causes. Since the world contains His gifts and favors, and He is powerful by His essence, His generosity and kindness are established by His essence. Thus it is necessary that what He honors by His essence necessitates it, and His power brings it into existence. And there is no power except with God. And some say: this world originated from an origin in which making occurred. But they differed: some make its origin a clay from which the Creator brought forth this world; some make it the stars, the sun, and the moon, which perpetually revolve, and by their revolution the world arises. And they make the revolution have a beginning by deeming it impossible for one thing to come from another without a first. And some make it such that accidents intervene in it, and from that the world is generated; they call it 'hyle' beforehand, and describe it as the monotheists describe the Maker, then they invalidated that by its possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state. And some say: its origin is two: light and darkness; from light every good and benefit, and from darkness every evil.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage discusses the second major source of doubt: reliance on sensory experience alone. People observed that all existing things are generated from matter, change, and are produced from one another. They concluded that creation from nothing is impossible because they never saw it, applying the rule that the witnessed is the evidence for the unseen (analogical reasoning based on sensory data). This led them to various false doctrines. Maturidi then enumerates the resulting sects: 1) Those who believe the world is eternal without a maker (naturalists). They attribute all variation to the natures of things. 2) Those who believe in four primary natures but with each essence having its own origin. 3) Those who affirm a creator but make the world eternal as a necessary concomitant of God's eternal existence (necessitarians). They argue from the harmony and perfection of the world to a wise manager, but then they err by making the world co-eternal with God, reasoning that since God is eternal, His effect must be eternal (like a cause always implies its effect). Maturidi notes that they also argue from God's generosity and power: because He is generous, He must create, and because He is powerful, He must bring into existence; thus the world exists eternally. 4) Those who say the world originated from a pre-existing matter: some say it was clay; some say stars and planets; some say an eternal hyle (matter) that receives accidents; they describe this hyle as monotheists describe God, but Maturidi points out its contingency (it receives accidents and changes). 5) Dualists who posit two eternal principles: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). Throughout, Maturidi presents each view and critiques it implicitly or explicitly, showing that all these positions stem from the mistaken assumption that the unseen must be like the seen.

هيولى
صانع
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the flaw in the necessitarian argument?
A) They deny God's existence.
B) They treat God's power as necessitating creation.
C) They believe in multiple gods.
D) They reject causality.

Key Takeaway
Even those who believe in a creator can err by making creation a necessary effect rather than a free act of will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that some people believe God's generosity forces Him to create, as if He has no choice. When something good happens in your life, do you secretly think God 'had to' give it to you because you deserved it? Or do you truly recognize it as a free and undeserved gift from His will? How would your gratitude change if you saw it as entirely unforced?
?
Maturidi says 'there is no power except with God.' This affirms that God's power is not bound by any necessity outside Himself. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God's power is limited—because you think He 'must' act a certain way based on your understanding of His nature? Write down one such assumption you hold about God and face whether it rests on scripture or on your own reasoning.
?
The necessitarians argued from the world's perfection to a knowing manager. You likely believe in God as Creator. But do you ever treat God's existence and attributes as a logical conclusion that you can fully grasp, rather than a mystery that calls you to humble submission? When your theology becomes too neat, you risk making God small. Ask yourself: am I content with a God who fits into my arguments, or am I willing to worship a God whose ways are beyond my comprehension?

Other Sects: Pre-existing Matter and Dualism
Maturidi presents three more sects: pre-existing matter, hyle, and dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various theories that posit eternal matter or dual principles to explain the universe without creation ex nihilo.

Core Idea
Both material monism and dualism fail to account for the contingency of the world.

The sensory fallacy also leads people to propose pre-existing matter or two eternal principles. Maturidi lists theories involving clay, stars, hyle, and dualism (light and darkness). He shows that these theories are self-defeating because they attribute divine attributes to contingent things.

Guided Path
1
List the different origins proposed
Read the passage and identify the four views: creation from clay, from stars, from hyle, and dualism of light and darkness.
2
Notice the critique of the hyle theory
Maturidi points out that hyle is described as eternal yet receives accidents and changes, which implies contingency.
3
Reflect on dualism
Dualism posits two eternal principles. Maturidi does not elaborate a critique here, but the lesson implies it is also false.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If evil is not an independent principle but part of Allah's creation, how does that change the way you respond to suffering in your own life? Are you willing to see every trial as from Allah rather than from some dark force? What step can you take to embody this belief?
?
The dualists attribute good and evil to separate eternal sources. In your heart, do you sometimes feel that good comes from God but evil comes from elsewhere? How can you fight this subtle shirk in your daily reliance on Allah? Write down one specific prayer you will make to affirm that everything comes from Allah.

The Dye Analogy and Material Theories of Origin
Maturidi presents materialist theories using the dye analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dye analogy and how early thinkers explained the diversity of the world through balance and imbalance of natures.

Core Idea
The world's diversity arises from the mixing of fundamental natures.

This passage presents ancient theories that attempted to explain the origin and diversity of the world using natural principles. The author begins with an analogy of dyes to illustrate how different mixtures produce different effects. Then he reports two specific views: one that attributes human essence to balanced natures and animals to imbalanced ones, and another that sees the four elements as the origin but considers them accidental to each substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
The language is concise; focus on the main claims.
2
Understand the dye analogy
The analogy shows how different mixtures produce different colors, applied to the balance of natures.
This is a materialist explanation for diversity.
3
Identify the two theories
Notice the first theory: humans from balanced natures, animals from imbalanced. The second theory: origin is the four elements, with natures as accidental.
These are distinct views from different groups.
4
Reflect on the author's stance
Maturidi is reporting these views, not endorsing them. He will later critique them.
This sets the stage for the argument for a Maker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ : إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله. وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع، والدواب من اضطرابها . وعلى هذا كل شيء. ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع، ولكن لكل جوهر أصل ، والطبائع دخيلة فيه " . ومنهم من يجعله كذلك بالصانع ويقول : هو واحد، ويجعله علة لكون العالم؛ فيوجب قدمه بوجوده [ في الأزل" . و] يذهب في [ اثبات ] الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء وإتقانها : إن ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدبر عليم، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدر ، وبه صلاح الأشياء " ، فقالوا بالصانع . ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم في الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعللها.
على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه ونعمه، وأنه قادر بذاته، فثبت جوده وكرمه بذاته. فيلزم كون الذي كرمه [ بذاته] يُوجبه، وقدرته توجده " . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
That which I mentioned on the example of dyes: they emerge in different colors according to the variation of mixture and its balance. And upon that they made the essence of humans from the balance of natures, and beasts from their imbalance. And upon this everything. And among them is one who considers its origin to be the four from the natures, but for every essence there is an origin, and the natures are incidental in it. And among them is one who makes it likewise by the Maker and says: He is one, and makes Him a cause for the existence of the world; so he necessitates its pre-eternity by His existence [in pre-eternity]. And he goes in [proving] the Maker to the consistency of things and their perfection: that this cannot be except by an omniscient administrator, for nature does not return to power, and through Him is the well-being of things. So they said by the Maker. Then since He was in pre-eternity, they necessitated the existence of the world in pre-eternity on the pattern of the conjunction of things with their causes. And since the world is His gifts and blessings, and that He is powerful by His essence, so His generosity and nobility are established by His essence. So the existence of what He honored [by His essence] is necessitated by Him, and His power brings it into existence. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a report of various theories on the material origin of the world and an argument for the existence of a Maker based on design. The author first mentions the analogy of dyes: just as colors emerge from different mixtures, so the world arises from the balance or imbalance of natures. Some say the human essence comes from balanced natures, animals from imbalanced. Others hold the origin to be the four elements, but each substance has its own origin and the natures are accidental. Another group attributes everything to a single Maker, who is one and the cause of the world's existence. They argue that the consistency and perfection of things cannot exist without an omniscient administrator, because nature does not possess power and cannot return to it; through the Maker things are well-ordered. Since the Maker exists in pre-eternity, they conclude that the world must also be eternal in pre-eternity, as an effect that necessarily accompanies its cause. Furthermore, because the world is full of gifts and blessings, and the Maker is powerful by His essence, His generosity and nobility are essential. Therefore, the existence of what He honors is necessitated by His essence, and His power brings it into existence. The passage ends with a pious phrase: 'And there is no power except through Allah.' This section sets the stage for later discussions by presenting views that Maturidi will critique.

Key Terms
طبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the diversity of the world according to the first theory?
A) The four elements
B) The balance or imbalance of natures
C) The will of a maker
D) Accidental properties

Key Takeaway
Early thinkers explained the world's diversity through the balance or imbalance of fundamental natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the balance and diversity in nature—from the human body to the animal kingdom—do you ever find yourself unconsciously explaining it as mere 'mixture' or 'coincidence'? What would it take for you to see these as signs of a purposeful Creator, not just accidental balance?
?
The dye analogy sounds plausible at first glance. In your own life, have you ever been satisfied with a superficial explanation for something deep, ignoring the need to trace it back to its true origin? Will you commit today to not stopping at surface-level causes but to seeking the ultimate cause—Allah—in every affair?
?
These materialists differed among themselves. How carefully do you distinguish between essential truths of your faith and accidental opinions? Are there beliefs you hold that might be incidental rather than essential? Reflect on one such belief and test it against the source texts.

The Argument from Design for a Single Maker
Maturidi reports the argument from design and its implications for the pre-eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from design used to prove a single Maker, and the conclusion that the world is pre-eternal and the Maker is generous by essence.

Core Idea
The consistency and perfection of creation demand an omniscient Maker.

In contrast to material theories, some thinkers argued that the order and perfection of the world cannot be explained by impersonal nature but require an intelligent administrator. This passage presents that argument and then draws further conclusions about the pre-eternity of the world and the essential generosity of the Maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage and translation carefully. Notice the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
This is a compact argument; pay attention to each claim.
2
Understand the design argument
The key premise: consistency and perfection in things cannot come from nature alone, a blind force. They require an omniscient administrator.
Nature lacks power and wisdom.
3
Trace the corollary of pre-eternity
Since the Maker is eternal, the world as His effect must also be eternal, just as an effect necessarily accompanies its cause.
This is a controversial inference.
4
Note the conclusion about generosity
The world is full of gifts and blessings, and the Maker is powerful by essence, so His generosity is essential and necessitates the world's existence.
The final phrase 'There is no power except through Allah' is a pious closure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ : إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله. وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع، والدواب من اضطرابها . وعلى هذا كل شيء. ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع، ولكن لكل جوهر أصل ، والطبائع دخيلة فيه " . ومنهم من يجعله كذلك بالصانع ويقول : هو واحد، ويجعله علة لكون العالم؛ فيوجب قدمه بوجوده [ في الأزل" . و] يذهب في [ اثبات ] الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء وإتقانها : إن ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدبر عليم، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدر ، وبه صلاح الأشياء " ، فقالوا بالصانع . ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم في الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعللها.
على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه ونعمه، وأنه قادر بذاته، فثبت جوده وكرمه بذاته. فيلزم كون الذي كرمه [ بذاته] يُوجبه، وقدرته توجده " . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
That which I mentioned on the example of dyes: they emerge in different colors according to the variation of mixture and its balance. And upon that they made the essence of humans from the balance of natures, and beasts from their imbalance. And upon this everything. And among them is one who considers its origin to be the four from the natures, but for every essence there is an origin, and the natures are incidental in it. And among them is one who makes it likewise by the Maker and says: He is one, and makes Him a cause for the existence of the world; so he necessitates its pre-eternity by His existence [in pre-eternity]. And he goes in [proving] the Maker to the consistency of things and their perfection: that this cannot be except by an omniscient administrator, for nature does not return to power, and through Him is the well-being of things. So they said by the Maker. Then since He was in pre-eternity, they necessitated the existence of the world in pre-eternity on the pattern of the conjunction of things with their causes. And since the world is His gifts and blessings, and that He is powerful by His essence, so His generosity and nobility are established by His essence. So the existence of what He honored [by His essence] is necessitated by Him, and His power brings it into existence. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a report of various theories on the material origin of the world and an argument for the existence of a Maker based on design. The author first mentions the analogy of dyes: just as colors emerge from different mixtures, so the world arises from the balance or imbalance of natures. Some say the human essence comes from balanced natures, animals from imbalanced. Others hold the origin to be the four elements, but each substance has its own origin and the natures are accidental. Another group attributes everything to a single Maker, who is one and the cause of the world's existence. They argue that the consistency and perfection of things cannot exist without an omniscient administrator, because nature does not possess power and cannot return to it; through the Maker things are well-ordered. Since the Maker exists in pre-eternity, they conclude that the world must also be eternal in pre-eternity, as an effect that necessarily accompanies its cause. Furthermore, because the world is full of gifts and blessings, and the Maker is powerful by His essence, His generosity and nobility are essential. Therefore, the existence of what He honors is necessitated by His essence, and His power brings it into existence. The passage ends with a pious phrase: 'And there is no power except through Allah.' This section sets the stage for later discussions by presenting views that Maturidi will critique.

Refutation of the Eternity of Atoms
Maturidi refutes the eternity of atoms by noting they are not free from accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that atoms (substances) cannot be eternal because they are never free from originated accidents, which implies they themselves are originated.

Core Idea
Atoms cannot be eternal because they are always changing.

This lesson introduces a key argument from Imam Maturidi against the claim that atoms are eternal. The argument is simple yet powerful: since atoms are never without accidents, which are originated, the atoms themselves must also be originated. This reasoning is central to the Islamic theological position that all of creation is temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully to get the exact wording of the argument.
Notice the concise style.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the meaning of قدم الأعيان (eternity of substances) and الحوادث (accidents, originated things).
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument: a claim is stated, an observation is made, and a conclusion is drawn. See how each part connects.
This is a classic kalam proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is important for the doctrine of God's existence and creation.
It shows that everything other than God is originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يقول بقدم الأعيان - فوجدناها غير خالية عن الحوادث - نمنع القول بذلك.
And whoever says that substances are eternal, we find them not free from accidents, so we forbid the saying of that.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This brief passage refutes the claim that atoms (substances, أعيان) are eternal. The refutation is straightforward: we observe that atoms are never free from accidents (حوادث), which are originated things. According to the kalam principle, if something is never free from originated things, it must itself be originated. Therefore, atoms cannot be eternal. The author simply states this as a prohibition of the claim.

Key Terms
قدم الأعيان
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Atoms could be eternal even if they have accidents.
Correction: The constant accompaniment of originated accidents necessitates that the atoms themselves are originated, because if something is never free from temporal things, it must itself be temporal.

Checkpoint

A) Atoms are invisible.
B) Atoms are always associated with accidents, which are originated.
C) Atoms are too small to be eternal.
D) The Quran explicitly denies it.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of atoms is refuted by the fact that atoms are always associated with originated accidents, proving they themselves are originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the smallest particles of your body are constantly changing and dependent on God for their existence, where do you place your trust when you feel self-sufficient?
?
This argument shows that nothing in the universe is eternal except God. Is there any part of your life where you act as if something other than God is eternal or independent?
?
The constant change in atoms every moment reflects your need for Allah each instant. How often do you forget this and rely on your own strength? What will you do today to remind yourself of your utter dependence?

The First Proof: Pre-eternity is Emptiness
Maturidi states the first reason against pre-eternity: it is empty and contradicts observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first argument that pre-eternity is a form of emptiness that contradicts direct observation.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is empty; observation shows everything is temporal.

Maturidi begins his refutation of the pre-eternity of entities by stating a fundamental principle: anything that is pre-eternal is empty of originated events. However, we observe that all entities are subject to change and new events. This contradiction forms the basis of his first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Maturidi identifies pre-eternity with emptiness (خلاء) and observation (عيان) as the standard.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms 'قدم' (pre-eternity), 'خلاء' (emptiness), and 'عيان' (direct observation).
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why is pre-eternity considered emptiness? How does observation disprove it?
Consider that if something is pre-eternal, it cannot have any beginning or change, but we see that everything changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يقول بقدم الأعيان - فوجدناها غير خالية عن الحوادث - نمنع القول بذلك لوجوه: أحدها في القدم خلاء، وفي ذلك تكذيب شهادة العيان. والثاني وجود كثير من الأعيان وابتداؤها لمدد تعد، وهي من أجزاء الجملة تحتمل ما يحتمل الكل. لذلك لزم القول بالحدوث. ولم يجز أن يقال كان كامناً فظهر أو متفرقاً فاجتمع لما فيه إثبات غير حكم العيان. وإذا احتمل ذلك - وإن ارتفع عن الإحاطة به - احتمل كون العالم من لا شيء، وإن ارتفع وجوده عن توهم البشر بدليل. والكمون لا يحتمل، لإحالة كون شيء واحد مكاناً لعشرة مثله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث لما لا يخلو العين وصفته من صور ثم لا يخلو من مصور، كسائر ما يحس ذاته وصفته، وهو لا يقوم بنفسه ولكن بمقيم، فلا يحتمل القدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما كان كل شيء يُعلم من نفسه عجزه وجهله بأحواله وما فيه صلاحه، فيكون ذلك دليل الكلية؛ وغير ذلك من الأدلة التي تقدم ذكرها. ثم وصف الصانع بالقدرة في الأزل والجود، لازم، وكذلك عندنا بالصنع؛ ليكون كل على ما كان ويكون أبد الآبدين، على ارتفاع القدم عن كل كائن به؛ لأنه نوع الفناء وإحالة معنى التكوين عنه، إذ هو الكون نفسه؛ وعلى ما كان مما لا تخلو الأعيان من الحوادث التي طريقها القدرة والكرم، ثم رجعت إلى الحوادث على ما يحتمل ذلك. فمثله كل الأعيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And whoever says about the pre-eternity of entities – we find them not free from originated events – we forbid that statement due to reasons: First, pre-eternity is emptiness, and in that is a denial of the testimony of direct observation. Second, the existence of many entities and their beginnings for counted durations, while they are parts of the whole, admit what the whole admits. Therefore, the statement of temporality is necessary. And it is not permissible to say they were latent and then appeared, or separate and then gathered, because in that is an affirmation contrary to the judgment of observation. And if that is admitted – though it transcends encompassing it – then the existence of the world from nothing would be admitted, though its existence transcends human imagination without evidence. And latency is not admissible, because it is impossible for one thing to be a place for ten like it. And there is no power except with God. Third, because the entity and its attribute are never free from forms, and then never free from a form-giver, like all that is perceived of its essence and attribute, and it does not subsist by itself but by an establisher, so it does not admit pre-eternity. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, everything knows from itself its inability and ignorance of its states and what is its good, so that becomes evidence of the whole; and other evidence that has been mentioned previously. Then the description of the Maker with power in pre-eternity and generosity is necessary, likewise in our view with making; so that everything is as it was and will be forever, with the removal of pre-eternity from every thing that comes into being through Him, because that is a type of annihilation and the falsification of the meaning of coming into being from Him, since it is existence itself; and according to what was such that entities are not free from originated events whose path is power and generosity, then they return to originated events according to what they admit. So the same applies to all entities. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that entities (أعيان) are pre-eternal. Maturidi gives three reasons: (1) Pre-eternity is empty (خلاء) and contradicts direct observation (عيان). (2) Many entities have beginnings in time, and since they are parts of the whole, the whole must also be temporal. He also refutes the objection that they were latent (كامن) and then appeared, arguing that this would affirm what is contrary to perception and would lead to accepting creation from nothing. Latency is impossible. (3) Every entity and its attribute have forms and require a form-giver (مصور); they do not subsist by themselves but need an establisher (مقيم), so they cannot be pre-eternal. Then he argues that everything knows its own deficiency, indicating a dependence on a Creator. Finally, he states that the description of the Maker with power and generosity is necessary, and that everything is subject to originated events (حوادث), so all entities are temporal.

Key Terms
قدم الأعيان
حوادث
خلاء
عيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify pre-eternity with?
A) A state of permanence
B) Emptiness
C) Power
D) Observation

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that claiming pre-eternity for entities is to assert something empty that contradicts observable reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if something in your life—a habit, a relationship, a worldview—is permanent and unchanging, even though observation shows it is subject to change? How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate your assumptions about what is eternal?
?
In what area of your faith are you tempted to ignore clear observation or rational evidence? Will you commit to aligning your beliefs with what is clearly observed?
?
The testimony of direct observation is given weight in this argument. How often do you ignore the clear signs of Allah's existence and power in everyday life? What will you do today to pay more attention?

The Second Proof: Parts Imply the Whole
Maturidi gives the second reason: parts have beginnings, so the whole must be temporal, and refutes the latency objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that since many entities have beginnings, the whole must be temporal, and why the latency objection fails.

Core Idea
Parts have beginnings; the whole must follow, and latency is impossible.

Maturidi's second proof builds on the observation that many entities have definite beginnings. Since these entities are parts of a larger whole, the whole must also be temporal. He then refutes the objection that entities might have been latent (كامن) before appearing, showing that latency is logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the part-whole argument and the refutation of latency.
2
Understand the part-whole logic
Maturidi argues that if parts of the whole have beginnings, the whole must also have a beginning. This is a key principle.
Think: if every part of a house is built at a time, the house itself is built.
3
Examine the latency objection
The objection claims entities were latent (كامن) and then appeared. Maturidi rejects this because it contradicts observation and leads to absurdity.
Latency would mean one thing contains many, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The necessity of temporal origination (حدوث) is affirmed against the latency claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
Maturidi argues that latency is impossible because one thing cannot contain ten like it. ?

The Third Proof: Entities Need a Form-Giver
Maturidi presents the third proof (need for a form-giver) and concludes with arguments about the Maker and originated events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third proof that every entity and its attribute require a form-giver (مصور) and an establisher (مقيم), and therefore cannot be pre-eternal.

Core Idea
Entities depend on a form-giver; they are not self-subsisting, so they cannot be pre-eternal.

Maturidi's third argument focuses on the intrinsic need of every entity for a form-giver. Just as we perceive that things have forms and attributes, we recognize they require a maker who gives them form. Additionally, he argues that everything knows its own deficiency, indicating dependence on a Creator. He concludes with a reflection on the Maker's power and the necessity that all entities are subject to originated events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: the third proof, the argument from self-knowledge, and the concluding reflection.
2
Understand the third proof
Maturidi argues that every entity has a form and attribute, and thus requires a form-giver (مصور). It does not subsist by itself but by an establisher (مقيم).
Compare to a building needing a builder.
3
Consider the self-knowledge argument
Everything knows its own inability and ignorance, indicating it is not self-sufficient and points to a Creator.
This is an additional proof.
4
Analyze the conclusion
Maturidi states that the Maker's power and generosity are necessary, and that all entities return to originated events, affirming temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يقول بقدم الأعيان - فوجدناها غير خالية عن الحوادث - نمنع القول بذلك لوجوه: أحدها في القدم خلاء، وفي ذلك تكذيب شهادة العيان. والثاني وجود كثير من الأعيان وابتداؤها لمدد تعد، وهي من أجزاء الجملة تحتمل ما يحتمل الكل. لذلك لزم القول بالحدوث. ولم يجز أن يقال كان كامناً فظهر أو متفرقاً فاجتمع لما فيه إثبات غير حكم العيان. وإذا احتمل ذلك - وإن ارتفع عن الإحاطة به - احتمل كون العالم من لا شيء، وإن ارتفع وجوده عن توهم البشر بدليل. والكمون لا يحتمل، لإحالة كون شيء واحد مكاناً لعشرة مثله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث لما لا يخلو العين وصفته من صور ثم لا يخلو من مصور، كسائر ما يحس ذاته وصفته، وهو لا يقوم بنفسه ولكن بمقيم، فلا يحتمل القدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما كان كل شيء يُعلم من نفسه عجزه وجهله بأحواله وما فيه صلاحه، فيكون ذلك دليل الكلية؛ وغير ذلك من الأدلة التي تقدم ذكرها. ثم وصف الصانع بالقدرة في الأزل والجود، لازم، وكذلك عندنا بالصنع؛ ليكون كل على ما كان ويكون أبد الآبدين، على ارتفاع القدم عن كل كائن به؛ لأنه نوع الفناء وإحالة معنى التكوين عنه، إذ هو الكون نفسه؛ وعلى ما كان مما لا تخلو الأعيان من الحوادث التي طريقها القدرة والكرم، ثم رجعت إلى الحوادث على ما يحتمل ذلك. فمثله كل الأعيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And whoever says about the pre-eternity of entities – we find them not free from originated events – we forbid that statement due to reasons: First, pre-eternity is emptiness, and in that is a denial of the testimony of direct observation. Second, the existence of many entities and their beginnings for counted durations, while they are parts of the whole, admit what the whole admits. Therefore, the statement of temporality is necessary. And it is not permissible to say they were latent and then appeared, or separate and then gathered, because in that is an affirmation contrary to the judgment of observation. And if that is admitted – though it transcends encompassing it – then the existence of the world from nothing would be admitted, though its existence transcends human imagination without evidence. And latency is not admissible, because it is impossible for one thing to be a place for ten like it. And there is no power except with God. Third, because the entity and its attribute are never free from forms, and then never free from a form-giver, like all that is perceived of its essence and attribute, and it does not subsist by itself but by an establisher, so it does not admit pre-eternity. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, everything knows from itself its inability and ignorance of its states and what is its good, so that becomes evidence of the whole; and other evidence that has been mentioned previously. Then the description of the Maker with power in pre-eternity and generosity is necessary, likewise in our view with making; so that everything is as it was and will be forever, with the removal of pre-eternity from every thing that comes into being through Him, because that is a type of annihilation and the falsification of the meaning of coming into being from Him, since it is existence itself; and according to what was such that entities are not free from originated events whose path is power and generosity, then they return to originated events according to what they admit. So the same applies to all entities. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that entities (أعيان) are pre-eternal. Maturidi gives three reasons: (1) Pre-eternity is empty (خلاء) and contradicts direct observation (عيان). (2) Many entities have beginnings in time, and since they are parts of the whole, the whole must also be temporal. He also refutes the objection that they were latent (كامن) and then appeared, arguing that this would affirm what is contrary to perception and would lead to accepting creation from nothing. Latency is impossible. (3) Every entity and its attribute have forms and require a form-giver (مصور); they do not subsist by themselves but need an establisher (مقيم), so they cannot be pre-eternal. Then he argues that everything knows its own deficiency, indicating a dependence on a Creator. Finally, he states that the description of the Maker with power and generosity is necessary, and that everything is subject to originated events (حوادث), so all entities are temporal.

Key Terms
عين
صفة
صور
مصور
مقيم
قدم
صنع
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That entities can be self-subsisting and therefore pre-eternal.
Correction: Maturidi argues that every entity has a form and requires a form-giver and an establisher, so it cannot be self-subsisting or pre-eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why does Maturidi say entities cannot be pre-eternal?
A) Because they are made of parts
B) Because they have forms and need a form-giver
C) Because they are observed to change
D) Because they are composed of atoms

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that entities require an external form-giver and establisher, negating any possibility of self-subsistence or pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that every entity, including you, does not subsist by itself but only by an establisher. Consider one area of your life where you feel most self-sufficient—your career, your health, your relationships. In what specific way have you been acting as if you are the 'establisher' of that area? Before you sleep tonight, commit to one concrete action that acknowledges your dependence on the Form-Giver.
?
The source says that everything knows from itself its inability and ignorance. When was the last time you truly felt the weight of your ignorance about a crucial matter in your life—not just theoretical knowledge, but something that affects your relationship with your Creator? How does that feeling drive you to seek the One who knows all? Write down one question you have about yourself that only God can answer, and spend five minutes in sincere supplication about it.
?
If you truly believed that you cannot exist for a single moment without the Form-Giver, how would your attitude toward today's plans change? List one decision you are about to make—big or small—and examine whether your confidence lies in your own ability or in God's establishment. What would it look like to make that decision with complete reliance on the Maker?

First and Second Proofs for the World's Contingency
Maturidi presents the first two proofs for temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the absence of events in eternity and the part-whole argument to demonstrate the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
The world cannot be eternal because events exist and its parts begin in time.

Maturidi opens with a clear argument from sensory observation: eternity lacks events, yet events plainly exist. He then extends this with a part-whole reasoning: individual entities have temporal beginnings, so the whole world must share their contingency. This lesson examines these foundational proofs and their refutation of latency theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Notice how Maturidi starts with the contrast between eternity (lack of events) and sensory testimony (events exist). This is a direct challenge to those who claim the world is eternal.
The phrase 'تكذيب شهادة العيان' is key: denying sensory evidence is unacceptable.
2
Study the second proof
The second proof uses the existence of many entities with countable beginnings. Since parts are temporal, the whole must be temporal too. Consider why this is a logical necessity rather than just induction.
Maturidi refutes the objection that things could have been latent (كامنا) or scattered.
3
Reflect on the latency refutation
Why does Maturidi reject the idea that things were latent then appeared? He argues it would contradict sensory observation and is impossible (e.g., one thing cannot be a place for ten like it).
This shows that alternative explanations must be rationally evaluated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجوه أحدها في القدم خلاء، وفي ذلك تكذيب شهادة العيان. والثاني وجود كثير من الأعيان وابتداؤها لمدد تعد، وهي من أجزاء الجملة تحتمل ما يحتمل الكل. لذلك لزم القول بالحدوث. ولم يجز أن يقال كان كامنا فظهر أو متفرقا فاجتمع لما فيه إثبات غير حكم العيان. وإذا احتمل ذلك وإن ارتفع عن الإحاطة به احتمل كون العالم من لا شيء، وإن ارتفع وجوده عن توهم البشر بدليل. والكمون لا يحتمل، لإحالة كون شيء واحد مكانا لعشرة مثله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث لما لا يخلو العين وصفته من صور ثم لا يخلو من مصور، كسائر ما يحس ذاته وصفته، وهو لا يقوم بنفسه ولكن بمقيم، فلا يحتمل القدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما كان كل شيء يعلم من نفسه عجزه وجهله بأحواله وما فيه صلاحه، فيكون ذلك دليل الكلية؛ وغير ذلك من الأدلة التي تقدم ذكرها. ثم وصف الصانع بالقدرة في الأزل والجود لازم، وكذلك عندنا بالصنع؛ ليكون كل على ما كان ويكون أبد الآبدين، على ارتفاع القدم عن كل كائن به؛ لأنه نوع الفناء وإحالة معنى التكوين عنه، إذ هو الكون نفسه؛ وعلى ما كان مما لا تخلو الأعيان من الحوادث التي طريقها القدرة والكرم، ثم رجعت إلى الحوادث على ما يحتمل ذلك. فمثله كل الأعيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولو كان الكل قديما لكان وصف القدرة والفعل يزول عنه في الحادث. بل كان تكوينه أن يكون كل شيء على ما علم أن يكون؛ ويريد بتكوين لم يزل به موصوفا، إذ هو يتعالى عن اعتراض الحوادث فيه، بما يصير بمعنى العالم الذي دل إحاطة الأحداث به على حدثه. فمثله صنع الصانع. والله الموفق.
Of the aspects: one of them is that in eternity there is an absence of events, and in that is a denial of the testimony of sensory observation. The second is the existence of many entities and their beginning for countable durations, and they are parts of the whole, bearing what the whole bears. Therefore it is necessary to affirm temporal origination. And it is not permissible to say that it was latent and then appeared, or scattered and then gathered, because that would affirm something other than the judgment of sensory observation. And if that is possible—even if it is beyond comprehensive grasp—then it is possible that the world came into being from nothing, even if its existence is beyond human imagination, by evidence. And latency is not possible, because it is impossible for one thing to be a place for ten like it. And there is no power except with Allah. The third: because the entity and its attribute are never free of forms, then they are never free of a form-giver, like all that which is perceived in its essence and attribute, and it does not subsist by itself but by a sustainer, so it cannot be eternal. And there is no power except with Allah. Along with the fact that everything knows of itself its incapacity and ignorance of its states and what constitutes its well-being, so that becomes evidence of the whole; and other evidence that has been mentioned previously. Then describing the Maker with power in eternity and generosity is necessary, and likewise according to us with making; so that everything is as it was and will be forever and ever, on the basis of denying eternity to every thing that comes to be by Him; because it is a kind of annihilation and annulling the meaning of bringing into existence from Him, since it is existence itself; and on what was such that entities are never free of events whose path is power and generosity, then they returned to events in the way that permits that. So every entity is like that. And there is no power except with Allah. And if all were eternal, the description of power and action would disappear from it in the temporal. Rather, His bringing into existence is that everything is as He knew it would be; and He intends by a bringing into existence with which He is continuously described, since He is exalted above the occurrence of events in Him, such that it becomes in the sense of the world whose being encompassed by events indicates its temporal origination. So the making of the Maker is like that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a cumulative argument for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world. The author lists several proofs. First proof: In eternity (al-qidam), there is an absence of events (khalā' min al-ḥawādith), but sensory observation testifies to the existence of events; thus the world cannot be eternal. Second proof: Many individual entities (a‘yān) exist and have beginnings in time; they are parts of the whole, so the whole world must likewise be contingent. The author refutes the objection that these entities might have been latent (kāmin) or scattered, showing that such explanations conflict with observable reality. Third proof: Every substance and its attribute are never free of forms, requiring a form-giver (muṣawwir); they are not self-subsistent, so they cannot be eternal. Additional arguments: Everything knows its own incapacity and ignorance, which indicates its contingency. The description of the Maker as powerful and generous in eternity necessitates that He creates; otherwise, the concept of power would become meaningless. Finally, if all were eternal, the attributes of power and action would vanish when a temporal event occurs. Thus, the world must be temporally originated, and God’s creative action is eternal. The passage concludes with 'And Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
القدم (al-qidam)
الحدوث (al-ḥudūth)
العيان (al-‘iyān)
الكمون (al-kumūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world could be eternal yet have events, or that things might have been latent before appearing.
Correction: Maturidi shows that events are observed, so the world cannot be eternal; latency is impossible because it contradicts observation and reason.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the latency theory?
A) It is possible but unlikely.
B) It contradicts sensory observation and is rationally impossible.
C) It is the correct explanation for the origin of the world.
D) It is only applicable to some entities.

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs establish that the world cannot be eternal based on observation and part-whole reasoning, and refute alternative explanations like latency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on sensory observation every moment of your life. When was the last time you truly acknowledged that everything you see with your eyes is temporal and will pass away? How does that affect the way you value your possessions and ambitions?
?
Maturidi says the parts of the world have beginnings. You yourself have a beginning—your birth. But do you live as if you have no beginning, as if you will never end? What would it take for you to internalize that your existence is contingent and that you are entirely dependent on the One who brought you into being?
?
The second proof shows that if each part is contingent, the whole must be contingent. Your own body is made of parts that are temporary. If you are honest, are you more attached to the whole of your worldly life than to the Eternal? What would change in your daily routine if you truly believed that everything around you is originated and only Allah is Qadīm (Eternal)?

Third Proof: Form-Giver and Incapacity
Maturidi presents the third proof and additional evidence from self-awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third proof for contingency: every entity and its attribute have forms requiring a form-giver, and everything recognizes its own incapacity, thus cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Everything has a form and depends on a form-giver; nothing is self-sufficient.

Maturidi's third argument focuses on the nature of entities and their attributes: they are always found with forms, which necessitate a form-giver (muṣawwir). Additionally, everything is aware of its own incapacity and ignorance, indicating its contingency. This lesson unpacks these complementary proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the form-giver argument
Every substance (عين) and its attribute are never free from forms (صور). Since they require a form-giver, they cannot be eternal. Consider why this dependence implies temporality.
The argument parallels other kalam proofs: anything that has a cause for its form cannot be eternal.
2
Examine the self-awareness of incapacity
Everything knows of itself its incapacity and ignorance regarding its own states and well-being. This self-awareness is evidence that it is not self-sufficient, hence contingent.
This is a more internal, metaphysical proof compared to the earlier ones.
3
Connect to the overall case
These arguments are part of a cumulative case. How do they reinforce each other? They show that contingency is not just external but also intrinsic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the third proof, why can an entity not be eternal?
A) Because it has a form and thus needs a form-giver.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own body and mind, are you truly aware of your complete dependence on Allah for every form and every moment of existence? What would it mean for your daily life if you genuinely felt that you are being sustained at every instant?
?
The lesson says everything knows its own incapacity and ignorance. When was the last time you admitted to yourself that you are utterly powerless over your own life and destiny? Did that realization drive you to rely on Allah, or did you try to ignore it? What would change if you sat with that feeling for five minutes today?
?
If the entire universe and everything in it requires a form-giver, what does that say about the forms you chase—status, wealth, appearance? Are you seeking forms that will vanish, or are you seeking the Form-Giver Himself? What concrete step can you take today to shift your focus from the form to the Giver?

Allah's Existence
