The Eternal Maker and the Contingency of All Things
Maturidi argues for the necessity of an eternal Creator and the temporal origination of all things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that describing the Maker with eternal power and generosity necessitates that He creates temporally, and that if all were eternal, divine power would be meaningless.

Core Idea
The Creator's eternal power and generosity require Him to act; if all were eternal, His power would vanish.

After presenting proofs for the world's origination, Maturidi turns to the nature of the Maker. He argues that describing Allah with power (qudra) and generosity (jūd) in eternity necessitates that He creates. Conversely, if the world were eternal, the attributes of power and action would become meaningless when a temporal event occurs. This lesson examines these profound theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the necessity of describing the Maker with power and generosity
Maturidi states that it is necessary to describe the Maker with power in eternity and generosity. This implies that He must bring things into existence. Reflect on why these attributes demand action.
The phrase 'بالقدرة في الأزل والجود لازم' is key.
2
Examine the consequence: eternal creating
God's creating is eternal in the sense that He is always described by it. Maturidi says 'تكوينه أن يكون كل شيء على ما علم أن يكون' — His bringing into existence is that everything is as He knew it would be.
This avoids the problem of change in God.
3
Consider the hypothetical: if all were eternal
If the entire world were eternal, then when a temporal event occurs (which we observe), the description of God's power and action would disappear. This is absurd, so the world must be temporal.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجوه أحدها في القدم خلاء، وفي ذلك تكذيب شهادة العيان. والثاني وجود كثير من الأعيان وابتداؤها لمدد تعد، وهي من أجزاء الجملة تحتمل ما يحتمل الكل. لذلك لزم القول بالحدوث. ولم يجز أن يقال كان كامنا فظهر أو متفرقا فاجتمع لما فيه إثبات غير حكم العيان. وإذا احتمل ذلك وإن ارتفع عن الإحاطة به احتمل كون العالم من لا شيء، وإن ارتفع وجوده عن توهم البشر بدليل. والكمون لا يحتمل، لإحالة كون شيء واحد مكانا لعشرة مثله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثالث لما لا يخلو العين وصفته من صور ثم لا يخلو من مصور، كسائر ما يحس ذاته وصفته، وهو لا يقوم بنفسه ولكن بمقيم، فلا يحتمل القدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما كان كل شيء يعلم من نفسه عجزه وجهله بأحواله وما فيه صلاحه، فيكون ذلك دليل الكلية؛ وغير ذلك من الأدلة التي تقدم ذكرها. ثم وصف الصانع بالقدرة في الأزل والجود لازم، وكذلك عندنا بالصنع؛ ليكون كل على ما كان ويكون أبد الآبدين، على ارتفاع القدم عن كل كائن به؛ لأنه نوع الفناء وإحالة معنى التكوين عنه، إذ هو الكون نفسه؛ وعلى ما كان مما لا تخلو الأعيان من الحوادث التي طريقها القدرة والكرم، ثم رجعت إلى الحوادث على ما يحتمل ذلك. فمثله كل الأعيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولو كان الكل قديما لكان وصف القدرة والفعل يزول عنه في الحادث. بل كان تكوينه أن يكون كل شيء على ما علم أن يكون؛ ويريد بتكوين لم يزل به موصوفا، إذ هو يتعالى عن اعتراض الحوادث فيه، بما يصير بمعنى العالم الذي دل إحاطة الأحداث به على حدثه. فمثله صنع الصانع. والله الموفق.
Of the aspects: one of them is that in eternity there is an absence of events, and in that is a denial of the testimony of sensory observation. The second is the existence of many entities and their beginning for countable durations, and they are parts of the whole, bearing what the whole bears. Therefore it is necessary to affirm temporal origination. And it is not permissible to say that it was latent and then appeared, or scattered and then gathered, because that would affirm something other than the judgment of sensory observation. And if that is possible—even if it is beyond comprehensive grasp—then it is possible that the world came into being from nothing, even if its existence is beyond human imagination, by evidence. And latency is not possible, because it is impossible for one thing to be a place for ten like it. And there is no power except with Allah. The third: because the entity and its attribute are never free of forms, then they are never free of a form-giver, like all that which is perceived in its essence and attribute, and it does not subsist by itself but by a sustainer, so it cannot be eternal. And there is no power except with Allah. Along with the fact that everything knows of itself its incapacity and ignorance of its states and what constitutes its well-being, so that becomes evidence of the whole; and other evidence that has been mentioned previously. Then describing the Maker with power in eternity and generosity is necessary, and likewise according to us with making; so that everything is as it was and will be forever and ever, on the basis of denying eternity to every thing that comes to be by Him; because it is a kind of annihilation and annulling the meaning of bringing into existence from Him, since it is existence itself; and on what was such that entities are never free of events whose path is power and generosity, then they returned to events in the way that permits that. So every entity is like that. And there is no power except with Allah. And if all were eternal, the description of power and action would disappear from it in the temporal. Rather, His bringing into existence is that everything is as He knew it would be; and He intends by a bringing into existence with which He is continuously described, since He is exalted above the occurrence of events in Him, such that it becomes in the sense of the world whose being encompassed by events indicates its temporal origination. So the making of the Maker is like that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a cumulative argument for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world. The author lists several proofs. First proof: In eternity (al-qidam), there is an absence of events (khalā' min al-ḥawādith), but sensory observation testifies to the existence of events; thus the world cannot be eternal. Second proof: Many individual entities (a‘yān) exist and have beginnings in time; they are parts of the whole, so the whole world must likewise be contingent. The author refutes the objection that these entities might have been latent (kāmin) or scattered, showing that such explanations conflict with observable reality. Third proof: Every substance and its attribute are never free of forms, requiring a form-giver (muṣawwir); they are not self-subsistent, so they cannot be eternal. Additional arguments: Everything knows its own incapacity and ignorance, which indicates its contingency. The description of the Maker as powerful and generous in eternity necessitates that He creates; otherwise, the concept of power would become meaningless. Finally, if all were eternal, the attributes of power and action would vanish when a temporal event occurs. Thus, the world must be temporally originated, and God’s creative action is eternal. The passage concludes with 'And Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
الجود (al-jūd)
التكوين (al-takwīn)
القدم (al-qidam)
الفناء (al-fanā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's creating is a temporal event like human action, or that eternity of the world would not affect God's attributes.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if the world were eternal, God's description with power and action would be meaningless when a temporal event occurs. God's creating is eternal and unchanging.

Checkpoint
What would happen to the description of God's power and action if the whole world were eternal?
A) It would remain unchanged.
B) It would disappear when a temporal event occurs.
C) It would become stronger.
D) It would become temporal.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal attributes necessitate creation; if the world were eternal, divine power would be undermined, so the world must be temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every thing that exists is temporally originated and entirely dependent on God's eternal power and generosity, then examine one area of your life where you act as if something—a habit, a possession, a relationship—is self-sufficient and eternal. What is it, and how does this passage compel you to change your perspective and acknowledge your total need for God at every moment?
?
The passage argues that if all were eternal, God's power and action would disappear. In your own spiritual life, are there areas where you treat your own actions or achievements as eternal, forgetting that only God's power is eternal? How can you actively practice attributing all your successes and abilities to His constant creation, so that your deeds are rooted in Him and not in your own assumed permanence?
?
God's generosity necessitates creation; He creates you continuously. Do you live as if you are a self-made entity, or do you recognize that at every moment you are being brought into existence by God? What specific change in your daily routine would demonstrate that you truly believe you are being constantly 'made' by the Maker?

Ibn Shabib's Argument from Opposition and Contradiction
Ibn Shabib argues that opposition and contradiction necessitate a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Muhammad ibn Shabib's philosophical argument that things characterized by opposition and contradiction cannot be eternal and must have a Creator.

Core Idea
Perpetual opposition cannot sustain existence; an originator is necessary.

Muhammad ibn Shabib, an early theologian, presents a logical argument for the existence of a Creator. He examines things that are characterized by opposition and contradiction, showing that they cannot be eternal. This passage lays the foundation of his proof, using an analogy and two possible scenarios to establish the necessity of a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense, technical style typical of kalam.
2
Understand the analogy of the house
Ibn Shabib uses an analogy of a house that cannot be entered until someone else enters. This illustrates the impossibility of an infinite regress or circularity in existence.
The analogy is key to grasping the argument.
3
Follow the two possibilities and their invalidation
He considers two possibilities for opposing things: either they are so by nature (leading to repulsion) or they could depart from their nature by choice (leading to self-annihilation). Both are absurd, so a creator is needed.
This is the core logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد بن شبيب في ذلك بما كان معناه عندنا: إنه إذ لا يخلو القائم على ما عليه من التضاد والتناقض من أن يكون كذلك أبدا؛ فيبطل كونه من حيث لا يتوهم كون شيء من الجملة إلا أن يكون شيئاً موجودا هو فيها، فيكون مع ذلك كل كائن منها المانع لكونه، فيبطل. كمن يقول: لا يدخل أحد هذه الدار حتى يدخلها غيره إنها لا يحتمل دخول أحد فيها على وفاء الشرط أو أن كان عن تباين قد تقدم، فيبطل الوجود للتضاد، إذ حقه التنافر بما تضادا بالطبع، ولو احتمل الخروج عن طبعهما الذي فيه التضاد - والتضاد يوجب ما ذكرت - بالاختيار لجاز اختيار كل منهما الفناء له في نفسه، وإن كان هو بطبعه باق. وإذا بطل الوجهان ثبت أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن بمن أحدثه كذلك على ما فيه الاختلاف والاتفاق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم لا يجوز أن يحدث بلا محدث؛ لما لا يكون العدم به والوجود إلا واحدا، ولما لا يُعرف صورة إلا من مُصوّر، ولما تغيّر الأوقات من شتاء وصيف ونحو ذلك، ثبت أنه كان بمن أحدثه كذلك. فعُورض بما لو كان فيما كان بنفسه يمنعه عن ذلك كونه في قت دون وقت، لم لا كان كذلك فيما كان بغيره؟ فزعم أنه إذا كان بغيره تدبير كونه فله مصلحة في الدين أو الدينا، وفيها كان لا بغيره ليس ذلك، لذلك اختلف الأمران.
Muhammad ibn Shabib said regarding that, the meaning of which according to us is: Since that which subsists upon opposition and contradiction cannot be free from being so forever, so its existence is invalidated insofar as one cannot conceive of any thing from the whole except that it be an existent thing in it, so then every thing that comes to be in it would be the preventer of its existence, so it is invalidated. Like one who says: No one enters this house until another enters it; it is not possible for anyone to enter it upon the fulfillment of the condition, or if it is from a preceding divergence, then existence is invalidated due to opposition, since its due is repulsion because they are opposed by nature. And if it were possible to depart from their nature which contains opposition—and opposition necessitates what I mentioned—by choice, then it would be permissible for each of them to choose annihilation for itself, even though it is by nature permanent. And if the two aspects are invalidated, it is established that it was after it was not, by one who originated it in that state, upon what is in it of difference and agreement. There is no power except with Allah. Then it is not permissible that it come to be without a maker; because non-existence and existence are not with it except one, and because no form is known except from a form-giver, and because of the change of seasons from winter and summer and the like, it is established that it was by one who originated it in that state. So it was objected: What if, when it was by itself, it prevented it from that because it is in a time without time, why is it not so when it is by another? He claimed that when it is by another, its governance has a benefit for it in religion or the world, and in it, when it is not by another, that is not the case; therefore the two matters differ.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents Muhammad ibn Shabib's argument for the existence of a Creator. He begins by considering something that subsists upon opposition and contradiction, implying that such a thing cannot be eternal because perpetual contradiction would prevent its existence. He uses the analogy of a house that no one can enter because of a condition of prior entry, illustrating the impossibility of an infinite regress. He then considers two possibilities: either the two principles are opposed by nature, or they could depart from their nature by choice. Both lead to absurdities. Therefore, it must be that they came to be after not being, by an originator. He further argues that because things like seasons change, there must be a creator. Then an objection is raised: if something by itself prevents change at one time, why not when caused by another? He responds that when governed by another, there is a benefit in terms of religion or worldly matters, which is absent when it is by itself. Thus, the two situations differ.

Key Terms
التضاد
التناقض
الاختيار
مُصوّر
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ibn Shabib use to illustrate the impossibility of an infinite regress?
A) A house that cannot be entered until another enters.
B) A moving body.
C) The change of seasons.
D) A substance rising by nature.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib's argument shows that any system of opposing elements cannot be self-sustaining and points to an external originator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that a system of opposites cannot sustain itself. Are you trying to maintain peace in your life by balancing conflicting desires, without turning to the Originator? What would it mean for you to acknowledge that your existence is not self-sufficient?
?
Ibn Shabib points to the change of seasons as proof of a Creator. When you notice the transition from winter to summer, do you truly see it as a sign of the Originator's hand, or do you take it for granted? How can you train yourself to see these signs as immediate proofs?
?
If opposition and contradiction necessarily point to a Creator, what do the internal conflicts in your heart indicate? Are you resolving them by referring to Allah, or by trying to manage them on your own? What step will you take today to bring a conflict to Him?

Objection and Response: Time and Purpose
Ibn Shabib responds to an objection about time and governance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against Ibn Shabib's argument and his response regarding the role of time and purpose in distinguishing self-subsisting from externally governed things.

Core Idea
What is governed by another acts for a benefit, unlike what is self-subsisting.

After presenting his main argument, Ibn Shabib addresses an objection: if something by itself prevents change at one time but not another, why would it not do the same when caused by another? His response highlights that things governed externally act for a purpose, while self-subsisting things do not. This lesson examines that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. It questions why a thing behaves differently at different times if it is self-subsisting, and why that would not apply when caused by another.
The objection is brief but profound.
2
Understand Ibn Shabib's response
He answers that when a thing's existence is governed by another, it has a purpose (benefit in religion or world), but when it is self-subsisting, there is no such purpose.
The key difference is the presence of purpose.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why purpose matters. The objection assumes uniformity, but Ibn Shabib argues that governance introduces intentionality, thereby changing the behavior.
This is a sophisticated response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد بن شبيب في ذلك بما كان معناه عندنا: إنه إذ لا يخلو القائم على ما عليه من التضاد والتناقض من أن يكون كذلك أبدا؛ فيبطل كونه من حيث لا يتوهم كون شيء من الجملة إلا أن يكون شيئاً موجودا هو فيها، فيكون مع ذلك كل كائن منها المانع لكونه، فيبطل. كمن يقول: لا يدخل أحد هذه الدار حتى يدخلها غيره إنها لا يحتمل دخول أحد فيها على وفاء الشرط أو أن كان عن تباين قد تقدم، فيبطل الوجود للتضاد، إذ حقه التنافر بما تضادا بالطبع، ولو احتمل الخروج عن طبعهما الذي فيه التضاد - والتضاد يوجب ما ذكرت - بالاختيار لجاز اختيار كل منهما الفناء له في نفسه، وإن كان هو بطبعه باق. وإذا بطل الوجهان ثبت أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن بمن أحدثه كذلك على ما فيه الاختلاف والاتفاق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم لا يجوز أن يحدث بلا محدث؛ لما لا يكون العدم به والوجود إلا واحدا، ولما لا يُعرف صورة إلا من مُصوّر، ولما تغيّر الأوقات من شتاء وصيف ونحو ذلك، ثبت أنه كان بمن أحدثه كذلك. فعُورض بما لو كان فيما كان بنفسه يمنعه عن ذلك كونه في قت دون وقت، لم لا كان كذلك فيما كان بغيره؟ فزعم أنه إذا كان بغيره تدبير كونه فله مصلحة في الدين أو الدينا، وفيها كان لا بغيره ليس ذلك، لذلك اختلف الأمران.
Muhammad ibn Shabib said regarding that, the meaning of which according to us is: Since that which subsists upon opposition and contradiction cannot be free from being so forever, so its existence is invalidated insofar as one cannot conceive of any thing from the whole except that it be an existent thing in it, so then every thing that comes to be in it would be the preventer of its existence, so it is invalidated. Like one who says: No one enters this house until another enters it; it is not possible for anyone to enter it upon the fulfillment of the condition, or if it is from a preceding divergence, then existence is invalidated due to opposition, since its due is repulsion because they are opposed by nature. And if it were possible to depart from their nature which contains opposition—and opposition necessitates what I mentioned—by choice, then it would be permissible for each of them to choose annihilation for itself, even though it is by nature permanent. And if the two aspects are invalidated, it is established that it was after it was not, by one who originated it in that state, upon what is in it of difference and agreement. There is no power except with Allah. Then it is not permissible that it come to be without a maker; because non-existence and existence are not with it except one, and because no form is known except from a form-giver, and because of the change of seasons from winter and summer and the like, it is established that it was by one who originated it in that state. So it was objected: What if, when it was by itself, it prevented it from that because it is in a time without time, why is it not so when it is by another? He claimed that when it is by another, its governance has a benefit for it in religion or the world, and in it, when it is not by another, that is not the case; therefore the two matters differ.

Commentary
This passage presents Muhammad ibn Shabib's argument for the existence of a Creator. He begins by considering something that subsists upon opposition and contradiction, implying that such a thing cannot be eternal because perpetual contradiction would prevent its existence. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a denial of causation altogether.
Correction: The objection is about uniformity of behavior across different modes of existence, not a denial of causation. Ibn Shabib's response uses the concept of purpose to differentiate.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Shabib, what distinguishes something governed by another from something self-subsisting?
A) The presence of choice.
B) The possession of purpose or benefit.
C) The ability to change over time.
D) The absence of opposition.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib argues that things governed by an external agent act with purpose, while self-subsisting things do not, thus resolving the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence has a purpose beyond your own desires? What is one concrete step you can take today to align your actions with the purpose for which you were created?
?
Ibn Shabib argues that what is governed by another acts for a benefit. God governs every moment of your life. If you truly believed that every trial and blessing has a purpose from Him, how would your response to hardship and ease change starting today?
?
The objection tried to equate the self-subsisting with the created. In your own faith, have you ever treated your own plans as if they were self-subsisting—forgetting that you are entirely dependent on Allah? What will you do to remind yourself of your dependency?

Ibn Shabib's Argument from Antagonism for a Creator
Ibn Shabib argues from the impossibility of eternal antagonism to prove a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Shabib uses the impossibility of eternal antagonism to prove that conflicting entities must have been created.

Core Idea
Eternal conflict cannot exist; a creator is needed to unite opposites.

Ibn Shabib presents a logical argument that any two mutually antagonistic entities cannot have existed eternally together. Their inherent repulsion would prevent coexistence unless they were brought together by an external creator. This lesson walks through his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Learn what Ibn Shabib means by 'antagonism' and 'contradiction' and why he starts with a thing 'established upon antagonism'.
Antagonism here refers to two entities whose natures oppose each other, like fire and water.
2
First possibility: eternal coexistence
Consider why two eternal antagonistic entities cannot coexist. Their natural repulsion would prevent them from being together without a third factor.
The house-entry analogy illustrates a condition that is impossible to fulfill.
3
Second possibility: voluntary overcoming
Even if they could overcome their nature by choice, each could choose to annihilate itself, which is absurd for eternal beings.
4
Conclusion from both invalid possibilities
Since both possibilities are invalid, the entities must have come into existence after non-existence by a creator.
The final statement 'There is no power except through God' affirms reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد بن شبيب في ذلك بما كان معناه عندنا : إنه إذ لا يخلو القائم على ما عليه من التضاد والتناقض من أن يكون كذلك أبدا؛ فيبطل كونه من حيث لا يتوهم كون شيء من الجملة إلا أن يكون شيئا [موجودا] هو فيها، فيكون مع ذلك كل كائن منها المانع لكونه، فيبطل. كمن يقول: لا يدخل أحد هذه الدار حتى يدخلها غيره إنها لا يحتمل دخول أحد فيها على وفاء الشرط أو أن كان عن تباين قد تقدم، فيبطل الوجود للتضاد، إذ حقه التنافر بما تضادا بالطبع، ولو احتمل الخروج عن طبعهما الذي فيه التضاد - والتضاد يوجب ما ذكرت - بالاختيار لجاز اختيار [كل منهما] الفناء له في نفسه، وإن كان هو بطبعه باق. وإذا بطل الوجهان ثبت أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن بمن أحدثه كذلك على ما فيه الاختلاف والاتفاق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Muhammad ibn Shabib said in that regard, according to our understanding: Since that which is established upon what it is of mutual antagonism and contradiction cannot be free from being thus forever, so its existence is invalidated from the perspective where one cannot conceive of a thing from the whole except that it is a thing [existent] in it, then along with that, every existent of them would prevent its existence, thus it is invalid. Like one who says: No one enters this house until another enters it; it cannot admit the entry of anyone into it upon fulfillment of the condition, or if it were from a prior separation. Then existence is invalidated due to antagonism, for its right is repulsion because of what is mutually antagonistic by nature. And even if they could depart from their nature in which there is antagonism—and antagonism necessitates what I mentioned—by choice, then it would be permissible for each of them to choose annihilation for itself, even if it is by nature abiding. When both aspects are invalid, it is established that it came to be after it was not, by one who caused it to be thus, upon what is of difference and agreement. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents Muhammad ibn Shabib's argument. He begins by stating that any entity characterized by mutual antagonism and contradiction cannot have existed eternally in that state. If it were eternal, its existence would be impossible because the antagonism prevents any two things from coexisting without a third factor. He gives an example: if someone says no one can enter a house until another enters, then no entry can happen because the condition is impossible. Similarly, if two opposing things (like light and darkness) are eternal, their natural repulsion would prevent coexistence. Even if we consider the possibility of them voluntarily overcoming their nature, then each could choose to annihilate itself, which is absurd. So both possibilities are invalid, proving that they must have come into existence after non-existence by a creator. The conclusion is that there is no power except through God.

Key Terms
تضاد
تناقض
قائم
حدوث
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument only applies to physical objects, but it applies to any two contradictory entities.
Correction: Ibn Shabib's argument is general: any two entities with mutual antagonism, whether physical or conceptual, cannot be eternal together.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of Ibn Shabib's argument?
A) The universe is eternal.
B) Antagonistic entities must have been created.
C) Everything is self-existent.
D) The creator is invisible.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib's argument shows that anything characterized by antagonism must have a creator that brings it into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn Shabib's argument shows that self-sufficient antagonism leads to destruction—each opposite choosing annihilation. Do you have any inner conflicts (like pride vs. humility, fear vs. faith) that you think you can resolve on your own? What if your refusal to let God mediate will eventually tear you apart?
?
This passage proves that only God can bring together what is naturally opposed. What is the deepest division in your soul right now that only He can heal? Are you willing to hand over the warring parts of yourself to Him, or will you keep pretending they can coexist forever without His intervention?

Ibn Shabib's Second Proof: The Need for a Creator
Ibn Shabib gives three arguments against things coming to be without a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Shabib's three reasons why every contingent thing requires a creator.

Core Idea
Everything that comes to be needs a cause – from forms to seasons.

Ibn Shabib presents a second argument for the necessity of a creator, drawing on the nature of existence, form, and seasonal change. This proof reinforces the core Islamic belief that the world is not self-sufficient but points to its Maker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three evidences
Read the passage and note the three distinct arguments Ibn Shabib gives for why things cannot come to be without a creator.
2
Understand the first evidence
Non-existence and existence are opposites; a thing cannot bring itself from one to the other, so an external cause is needed.
This is a fundamental axiom of kalam.
3
Reflect on form and seasons
Every form implies a form-giver, and the regular change of seasons indicates a changer. These concrete examples make the proof intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يجوز أن يحدث بلا محدث؛ لما لا يكون العدم به والوجود إلا واحدا، ولما لا يُعرف صورة إلا من مُصوّر، ولما تغيّر الأوقات من شتاء وصيف ونحو ذلك، ثبت أنه كان [ بمن أحدثه ] كذلك. فعُورض بما لو كان فيما كان بنفسه يمنعه عن ذلك كونه في قت دون وقت، لم لا كان كذلك فيما كان بغيره؟ فزعم أنه إذا كان بغيره تدبير كونه [ف]له مصلحة في الدين أو الدينا، وفيها " كان لا بغيره ليس ذلك "، لذلك اختلف الأمران.
Then it is not permissible that something comes to be without a cause of coming-to-be, because non-existence and existence are [only] one with respect to it, and because no form is known except from a form-giver, and because the changes of seasons from winter to summer and so on, it is established that it came to be [by one who brought it about] similarly. Then it was objected: if that which exists by itself prevents it from [being created] at some time without time, why is it not so for that which is by another? He claimed that if it is by another, its management of its existence [has] a benefit for it in religion or world, and in it, "it is not by another" not that, therefore the two matters differ.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Shabib's second proof and an objection-response. The proof gives three reasons why something cannot come to be without a creator: (1) non-existence and existence are opposites, and something cannot bring itself from non-existence to existence; (2) every form implies a form-giver; (3) the change of seasons indicates a changer. Therefore, the world is created by a creator. An objection follows: if something can prevent itself from coming to be at a certain time (i.e., being self-determined), why can't it be so when caused by another? Ibn Shabib responds that when caused by another, the creator arranges its existence for a benefit in religion or world, whereas when self-existent, there is no such benefit, so the two cases are different.

Key Terms
محدث
صورة
مصوّر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib's proof uses three rational arguments to show that the world must have a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Shabib argues that every form requires a form-giver. Your body—your face, your hands, your very DNA—is a form of stunning intricacy. When you look in the mirror, do you truly see yourself as a created being, or do you secretly believe you are self-made? What would change in your daily gratitude if you acknowledged that your very shape is given to you moment by moment?
?
The regularity of seasons—winter and summer—points to a keeper of order. You take that for granted every day. When was the last time you thanked the One who orchestrates the sun’s tilt and the earth’s orbit? If you were to spend one minute each morning reflecting that the sunrise is an act of your Creator, how might your reliance on Him grow? This week, commit to a specific reminder (a note, an alarm) that the season outside your window is a sign of His constant care.
?
Ibn Shabib’s proof ends by saying the world came to be ‘by one who brought it about.’ You did not choose to exist—you were brought into being. Yet so often you live as if your existence, your intellect, your opportunities are your own doing. Right now, name one success you attribute entirely to your effort. Then pause and ask: could any of that have happened without the Creator who brought you from non-existence? Write down a prayer that hands that success back to Him.

Ibn Shabib's Response to the Objection about Time and Benefit
Ibn Shabib replies to an objection that a thing could determine its own time of coming to be.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against Ibn Shabib's proof and his response based on benefit.

Core Idea
Self-caused and other-caused things differ because the latter has a purpose.

In response to an objection that questions why a created thing cannot determine its own time of existence, Ibn Shabib clarifies the difference between self-existent and other-existent things. He points to the concept of benefit as the key distinction.

3
Analyze Ibn Shabib's answer
He distinguishes based on benefit: when caused, existence is arranged for a benefit in religion or world, while self-existence has no such benefit.
Benefit (maslaha) is central to divine action.

Maturidi's Response to Objection on First Creation and Time
Maturidi answers an objection regarding the timing of the first creation and the question of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical response to the objection about why the first creation was not created earlier, and why the objection is invalid.

Core Idea
There is no time before the first creation, so the question 'why not earlier?' is meaningless.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses an objection about the timing of the first creation. The objector asks: if God created for Himself without benefit, why did He not create it earlier? Maturidi argues that this question is based on a misunderstanding of time. He shows that the notion of 'first' precludes any time before it, making the question logically incoherent. This lesson explains his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that Maturidi faces: the first creation seems arbitrary if no benefit exists. The objector claims there is no benefit in the act, so why that specific time?
Notice the assumption that time is linear and before the first creation there could be a 'before'.
2
Grasp Maturidi's first response
Identify why he says there is no time to ask 'why not earlier?' because the concept of 'first creation' means there is no time before it.
3
Explore the deeper implication
Consider the alternative: if we allow infinite times before the first creation, then the question becomes infinite (why not earlier ad infinitum), making the objection invalid.
This shows the objection leads to an infinite regress.
4
See the contradiction in claiming eternity
Understand why saying the creation is eternal (i.e., it has always existed) contradicts its origination (it was created). Maturidi says this is a contradiction.
This closes the argument by showing the only alternative is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعورض بأول خلق خلقه لنفسه وليس ثمة مصلحة. فزعم أنه ليس ثمة وقت ليقال فيه: لم لا خلق قبله؟ وإنما ذلك متى يكون [ف] هو أوّل، وهو أصلح في التدبير وأولى بالحكمة. وما هو كذلك فيخرج السؤال على أنه لم لا خلق [ما هو] دونه في الحكمة وحسن التدبير؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فما ذكر من الوقت فهو [ك] ما يذكر. على أن السؤال في مثله ساقط، لأنه لا يشار إلى وقت وإلا لو كان الخلق قبل ذلك إلى ما لا يحتمل اللسان من عدد الأوقات ممكن، وفي ذلك بطلان السؤال؛ إلا عن قدمه، وذلك تناقض، لإحالة وقوع التكوين
It was objected regarding the first creation which He created for Himself while there is no benefit. He claimed that there is no time such that it could be said: "Why was it not created before it?" That is only when it is? [F] it is first, and it is more beneficial in management and more appropriate to wisdom. And if it is like that, the question becomes: "Why was not created what is? less than it in wisdom and good management?" The Shaykh (God have mercy on him) said: What is mentioned about time is like what is mentioned. However, the question in such a case is invalid, because no specific time is indicated. Otherwise, if creation before that to an infinite number of times were possible, that would invalidate the question. Except about its eternity, and that is a contradiction, because the occurrence of origination is impossible [if eternal].
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and Maturidi's response. The objection is about the first creation: if God created for Himself without benefit, why didn't He create earlier? The response is that the concept of 'first' means there is no time before it, so the question is invalid. Then an objection within that: then why not create something less wise? Maturidi's answer: the question about time is irrelevant because if we consider infinite times before, then the question of 'why not earlier' would be endless, making the question meaningless. The only alternative is to say the creation is eternal, which is contradictory because origination cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
أول الخلق
وقت
قدم
تكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi evades the question; he argues it is logically incoherent due to infinite regress.
Correction: Maturidi provides a logical refutation: the question assumes a time before the first creation, which is impossible by definition.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the question 'why was it not created earlier?' is invalid?
A) Because time is cyclical.
B) Because there is no time before the first creation.
C) Because God's wisdom is unknowable.
D) Because the creation is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the objection about timing is invalid because there is no time before the first creation, and any attempt to posit an earlier time leads to infinite regress or a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you question God's timing in your own life—'why didn't this happen earlier?'—are you aware that you are assuming a framework of time that may not even exist from God's perspective? How does Maturidi's argument challenge your impatience and force you to trust God's absolute wisdom in His choice of when to act? Write a specific situation where you will consciously surrender your timeline to God.
?
What would it mean for you to accept that some of your deepest 'why' questions about God's actions are not just unanswered but are actually invalid? Is there a trial or mystery in your faith where you need to stop asking 'why not before?' and instead say 'This is the first and best time because God decreed it'? What would that repentance look like today?
?
Maturidi shows that the objection collapses into either infinite regress or contradiction. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes entertain thoughts that lead to such dead ends—endless second-guessing of God's decisions? How can you use this logical lesson to cut off those thoughts at the root and submit to God's management (tadbir) as the most wise? Commit to one practical step to stop rehearsing such questions.

Answering 'What is God?': Maturidi's Response
Maturidi refutes Muhammad ibn Shabib's question about God's nature by affirming transcendence and knowledge through evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'What is God?' cannot be answered with a description of His essence, but rather through His evidence and names.

Core Idea
God is known through evidence and names, not through sensory likeness.

Imam Maturidi addresses a heretic's question that attempts to reduce God to a physical or comparable entity. He explains the proper way to understand the question 'What is He?' and redirects the answer to God's transcendence and knowability through rational evidence and revealed names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very direct and refutational style.
2
Identify the heretic's objection
The heretic asks 'What is He?' implying that God might be similar to something or perceptible by the senses. Understand the underlying assumption.
3
Understand Maturidi's rejection
Maturidi denies both likeness and sensory knowledge. He explains that 'what is He' actually means that His existence is proven by evidence and the testimony of the world.
This shifts the question from essence to evidence.
4
Note the final answer
The final answer to 'what is He' is His name: Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. This emphasizes that God is known through His names and attributes.
The name 'Allah' encompasses all other names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما عارض محمد بن شبيب من أسئلة الملحدين. فعارض عن الواحد الذي يعبده: ما هو؟ وقد بينا ما يجاب له. وهو زعم أن ذا يحتمل مثل ذا، وقد بينا أن لا شبيه له؛ ويحتمل ما يشار إليه؛ ولم نكن نعرفه بالحواس فنشير إليه. وما هو بمعنى يوجد قبل بالأدلة وشهادة العالم. وما هو: ما اسمه الله الرحمن الرحيم.
Then we mention what Muhammad ibn Shabib objected with from the questions of the heretics. He objected concerning the One whom he worships: 'What is He?' And we have already explained what should be answered to him. And he claimed that this could be like that, and we have explained that He has no likeness; and it could be what is pointed to; but we do not know Him by the senses so that we could point to Him. And 'what is He' means He exists prior [or: through] the evidences and the testimony of the world. And 'what is He' is: His name is Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage introduces and answers a question posed by the heretic Muhammad ibn Shabib: 'What is the One whom you worship?' The author notes that he has already provided the answer. The questioner suggests that the term 'what' could imply similarity (He is like something) or that He could be pointed to by the senses. The response asserts that God has no likeness (no similitude) and cannot be known by the senses, so He cannot be pointed to physically. Instead, the meaning of 'what is He' is that He exists, and this existence is known through the evidences and the testimony of the world. Finally, the answer to 'what is He' is that His name is Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. This emphasizes that God is known through His names and attributes, not by sensory perception.

Key Terms
ما هو
الواحد
لا شبيه له
الأدلة
شهادة العالم
الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can be described by physical or sensory characteristics
Correction: God is transcendent, has no likeness, and is not perceived by senses; He is known through rational evidence and His names.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Maturidi answer the question 'What is the One worshipped?'
A) By saying He is like other things.
B) By saying He is known through the senses.
C) By affirming that He has no likeness and is known through evidence and His name is Allah.
D) By saying the question is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
God is not known by sensory or comparative means; He is known through rational evidences and His revealed names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly imagine Allah with some physical form, like light or a throne, despite knowing He is unlike anything? This lesson says we cannot know Him by senses. What concrete step will you take today to purify your mental image of Allah from any sensory imagination?

?
The heretic assumed 'What is He?' requires a sensory answer. Have you ever treated questions about God as if they were about physical objects? Reflect on one area where you have done this, and resolve to replace that approach with the Maturidi method: affirm transcendence and use evidence and names.

The Positive Proof from Order
Maturidi argues that order proves positive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the orderly sequence of creation proves that Allah has knowledge and power, not merely that He is not ignorant or incapable.

Core Idea
Orderly creation proves positive divine attributes, not just negations.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents a fundamental argument for affirming positive attributes of Allah. He argues that the observed order and perfection in creation cannot be explained by merely negating ignorance and incapacity. Only real knowledge and power can produce such consistent and excellent acts. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi contrasts two types of proofs: those of knowledge/power vs. those of negation.
2
Identify the two types of proof
Find where Maturidi says the orderly acts are proofs of knowledge and power, not proofs of 'one who is not ignorant and not incapable' (i.e., mere negation). Understand that negation alone cannot produce action.
The example of accidents shows that negation-only does not yield coherent sequence.
3
Reflect on the insufficiency of negation
Ask yourself: Why is negation alone insufficient? Think about a hypothetical agent who is merely not ignorant and not incapable – would that guarantee orderly action? Maturidi says no.
This leads to the necessity of positive attributes like knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المتتابعة على حسن النظام والإحكام هي أدلة العلم بها والقدرة عليها، لا أنها أدلة من ليس بجاهل ولا عاجز؛ إذ غير واحد بما وصفه لا يكون منه فعل ألبتة ولا اتساق، نحو الأعراض كلها . ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنها أسماء عن صفات تسقط لسقوط الصفات. فإذ لم يحقق الصفات صيّرت الأسماء أسماء ألقاب، وإذا صارت كذا فالقول بأنه لم يزل كذا كلام لا معنى له، لإحالة اللقب في الأزل . ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم إذ لم يجب في القول بـ «سميع عليم قدير» كون كل معلوم مقدور عليه مسموع في الأزل، فمثله في القول بـ «الخالق»، لكن هو خالق الأشياء لتكون على ما هي عليه الآن، كما هو عالم بها كذلك وقادر ونحو ذلك. وإذا كان القول بـ«عالم سميع بصير» وبـ «العالم السميع البصير» واحدا فكذلك القول بـ «خالق» و «الخالق» واحد، بل «الخالق» في إيجاب قدم الخلق أحق لو كان التقدير من الملفوظ من «خالق». ألا يُرى أنه على وزن خالق يُقال: ﴿مالك يوم الدين﴾ [الفاتحة 4/1]، و﴿خالق كل شيء﴾ [الأنعام 102/6]، يدخل في ذلك كل حادث وقائم، ليس في قوله «الخالق» ذلك، ولا هو يقال عليه في العرف . ولا قوة إلا بالله . قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن الله تعالى إذ لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم عليه، بانقطاع وجوه الوصول إلى معرفته من طريق الحواس عليه أو شهادة.
Furthermore, the emergence of successive acts upon excellent order and perfection are proofs of knowledge of them and power over them, not that they are proofs of one who is not ignorant and not incapable; for someone other than the One described by these [attributes] would not produce an act at all nor a coherent sequence, like all accidents. There is no power except with Allah. On the basis that they are names for attributes that fall away with the falling away of the attributes. So when the attributes are not verified, the names become mere titles, and when they become so, then the statement that 'He has never ceased to be such' is meaningless speech, because a title in pre-eternity is impossible. There is no power except with Allah. Then, since it does not follow from the statement 'All-Hearing, All-Knowing, All-Powerful' that everything known, potential, and heard exists in pre-eternity, so likewise with the statement 'the Creator,' but He is the Creator of things so that they become as they are now, just as He is Knower of them in that way, and Powerful, and so on. And if the statement 'Knower, Hearing, Seeing' and 'the Knower, the Hearing, the Seeing' are one, so likewise the statement 'Creator' and 'the Creator' are one; rather, 'the Creator' is more deserving of necessitating the pre-eternity of creation if the estimation were from the spoken word 'Creator.' Do you not see that in the pattern of 'Creator' it is said: 'Master of the Day of Judgment' (Al-Fatiha 1:4) and 'Creator of everything' (Al-An'am 6:102), which includes every contingent and permanent thing? That is not in His saying 'the Creator,' nor is it said of Him in common usage. There is no power except with Allah. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle is that since there is no way to know Allah except through the indication of the world to Him, due to the cutting off of the means of reaching knowledge of Him through the senses or testimony...
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a positive proof for divine attributes from the observed order and perfection in creation. Al-Maturidi argues that the orderly sequence of acts demonstrates the attributes of knowledge and power in the agent, not merely the negation of opposites. Mere negation (not ignorant, not incapable) does not produce coherent action; only positive knowledge and power do. Then he addresses the nature of names: if the attributes are not real, the names become mere titles, and applying a title to God in pre-eternity is meaningless. He then draws an analogy: just as saying 'All-Hearing' does not necessitate that all sounds exist pre-eternally, saying 'Creator' does not necessitate that creation is pre-eternal. The name 'Creator' is like 'Master of the Day of Judgment' — it is a relational attribute that applies only when the object exists. Finally, he states the fundamental principle that knowledge of God comes only through the world, as direct sensory knowledge is impossible.

Key Terms
أفعال متتابعة
حسن النظام
العلم
القدرة
عاجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the orderly successive acts in creation prove?
A) That God is not ignorant and not incapable.
B) That God has knowledge and power.
C) That God is eternal.
D) That creation is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Orderly creation directly proves that Allah possesses knowledge and power, not merely that He lacks their opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen how Maturidi argues that order in creation demands that Allah possesses knowledge and power—not merely that He is free from ignorance and weakness. Where in your own life do you find yourself thinking of Allah in mere negatives (e.g., 'He is not unjust' or 'He is not weak') rather than actively affirming His positive attributes? What daily practice will you start to replace negation with positive affirmation of Allah's knowledge and power?
?
If you truly internalize that every ordered system around you—your own body's function, the seasons, the laws of physics—is a direct proof of Allah's specific attributes of knowledge and power, how does that change your sense of gratitude and dependence on Him? Will you observe one such system today and consciously declare in your heart: 'This testifies to Allah's knowledge and power'?
?
Maturidi says that a being who lacks positive attributes can produce no act at all. You claim to believe in Allah's perfection; but do you live as though He is merely a 'not'—not unjust, not limited—rather than as the One who actively knows and controls every detail of your existence? What is one decision you are facing right now where you need to affirm His specific knowledge and power over it, and submit accordingly?

Names, Attributes, and the Meaning of 'Creator'
Maturidi clarifies that divine names are relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine names are derived from attributes and why saying 'al-Khaliq' does not imply that creation is eternal.

Core Idea
Divine names are meaningful only through real attributes; 'Creator' is relational, not implying pre-eternity.

Imam al-Maturidi now addresses a common misunderstanding: if Allah is called 'Creator', does that mean creation is eternal? He explains that names like 'Creator' and 'Master of the Day of Judgment' are relational, depending on the existence of what they govern. Without real attributes, names become empty titles. This lesson clarifies the relationship between names, attributes, and pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how Maturidi moves from discussing names as derived from attributes to the example of 'Creator' and 'Master of Judgment Day'.
2
Distinguish between names and titles
Find where Maturidi says names become mere titles (أسماء ألقاب) if attributes are not real. Understand that a title applied to God in pre-eternity would be meaningless, while a name from a real attribute is meaningful even if the object is not yet created.
This refutes those who think 'Creator' implies eternal creation.
3
Analyze the analogy with 'Hearing'
Maturidi draws an analogy: saying 'All-Hearing' does not mean all sounds exist pre-eternally. Similarly, saying 'Creator' does not mean all creation exists pre-eternally. Both are relational attributes.
The names are about the attribute's perfection, not about the pre-existence of the object.
4
Understand the definite vs. indefinite form
Note the distinction between 'Khaliq' and 'al-Khaliq'. Maturidi explains that 'al-Khaliq' (the Creator) is like 'Master of Judgment Day' – it is a description that applies when the context requires, not a title implying eternal existence of the object.
The definite article here is not a claim of pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المتتابعة على حسن النظام والإحكام هي أدلة العلم بها والقدرة عليها، لا أنها أدلة من ليس بجاهل ولا عاجز؛ إذ غير واحد بما وصفه لا يكون منه فعل ألبتة ولا اتساق، نحو الأعراض كلها . ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنها أسماء عن صفات تسقط لسقوط الصفات. فإذ لم يحقق الصفات صيّرت الأسماء أسماء ألقاب، وإذا صارت كذا فالقول بأنه لم يزل كذا كلام لا معنى له، لإحالة اللقب في الأزل . ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم إذ لم يجب في القول بـ «سميع عليم قدير» كون كل معلوم مقدور عليه مسموع في الأزل، فمثله في القول بـ «الخالق»، لكن هو خالق الأشياء لتكون على ما هي عليه الآن، كما هو عالم بها كذلك وقادر ونحو ذلك. وإذا كان القول بـ«عالم سميع بصير» وبـ «العالم السميع البصير» واحدا فكذلك القول بـ «خالق» و «الخالق» واحد، بل «الخالق» في إيجاب قدم الخلق أحق لو كان التقدير من الملفوظ من «خالق». ألا يُرى أنه على وزن خالق يُقال: ﴿مالك يوم الدين﴾ [الفاتحة 4/1]، و﴿خالق كل شيء﴾ [الأنعام 102/6]، يدخل في ذلك كل حادث وقائم، ليس في قوله «الخالق» ذلك، ولا هو يقال عليه في العرف . ولا قوة إلا بالله . قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن الله تعالى إذ لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم عليه، بانقطاع وجوه الوصول إلى معرفته من طريق الحواس عليه أو شهادة.
Furthermore, the emergence of successive acts upon excellent order and perfection are proofs of knowledge of them and power over them, not that they are proofs of one who is not ignorant and not incapable; for someone other than the One described by these [attributes] would not produce an act at all nor a coherent sequence, like all accidents. There is no power except with Allah. On the basis that they are names for attributes that fall away with the falling away of the attributes. So when the attributes are not verified, the names become mere titles, and when they become so, then the statement that 'He has never ceased to be such' is meaningless speech, because a title in pre-eternity is impossible. There is no power except with Allah. Then, since it does not follow from the statement 'All-Hearing, All-Knowing, All-Powerful' that everything known, potential, and heard exists in pre-eternity, so likewise with the statement 'the Creator,' but He is the Creator of things so that they become as they are now, just as He is Knower of them in that way, and Powerful, and so on. And if the statement 'Knower, Hearing, Seeing' and 'the Knower, the Hearing, the Seeing' are one, so likewise the statement 'Creator' and 'the Creator' are one; rather, 'the Creator' is more deserving of necessitating the pre-eternity of creation if the estimation were from the spoken word 'Creator.' Do you not see that in the pattern of 'Creator' it is said: 'Master of the Day of Judgment' (Al-Fatiha 1:4) and 'Creator of everything' (Al-An'am 6:102), which includes every contingent and permanent thing? That is not in His saying 'the Creator,' nor is it said of Him in common usage. There is no power except with Allah. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle is that since there is no way to know Allah except through the indication of the world to Him, due to the cutting off of the means of reaching knowledge of Him through the senses or testimony...
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a positive proof for divine attributes from the observed order and perfection in creation. Al-Maturidi argues that the orderly sequence of acts demonstrates the attributes of knowledge and power in the agent, not merely the negation of opposites. Mere negation (not ignorant, not incapable) does not produce coherent action; only positive knowledge and power do. Then he addresses the nature of names: if the attributes are not real, the names become mere titles, and applying a title to God in pre-eternity is meaningless. He then draws an analogy: just as saying 'All-Hearing' does not necessitate that all sounds exist pre-eternally, saying 'Creator' does not necessitate that creation is pre-eternal. The name 'Creator' is like 'Master of the Day of Judgment' — it is a relational attribute that applies only when the object exists. Finally, he states the fundamental principle that knowledge of God comes only through the world, as direct sensory knowledge is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the name 'al-Khaliq' (The Creator) implies that creation is eternal, co-existing with God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that names like 'Creator' are relational: they describe God's power to create when creation exists, not that creation is eternal. The definite form 'al-Khaliq' is analogous to 'Master of the Day of Judgment'—it applies only when the object is present.

?

Proof from Temporality: God is Knowing by Essence
Maturidi proves God's essential knowledge from the temporal origination of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that God's attribute of knowledge is essential and not acquired, using the argument from the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is essential, not acquired, proven by the world's temporal origination.

Maturidi presents a concise proof for one of God's essential attributes: knowledge. He begins with the established fact that the world is temporally originated and argues that such a perfectly ordered creation must have been preceded by knowledge. However, this knowledge cannot be sensory or analogical, as those require prior objects or patterns. Therefore, the Creator must possess knowledge as an essential attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
Pay attention to the two key premises.
2
Identify the key premises
The first premise is the temporal origination of the world with its perfect order. The second premise is the impossibility of sensory or analogical knowledge without pre-existing objects or patterns.
These premises lead to the conclusion.
3
Trace the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God must be 'Knowing by essence' (ʿālim li-dhātih), meaning knowledge is an essential attribute, not acquired.
This conclusion is derived from eliminating other possibilities.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the final sentence: God's knowledge is the same whether the known is absent or present, emphasizing that His knowledge does not change.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : إذ ثبت حدث العالم ـ ومحال كونه بعد أن لم يكن على ما عليه من قيام الأوائل بالأواخر واتفاق ذلك ـ علم أنه كان عن علم به. ثم محال كون حس به يُعلم ولا محسوس أو قياس ولا عبرة، ثبت أنه عالم لذاته. وفيهما العلم لذات العالم سواء فيه غيبة المعلوم وحضرته. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Since the temporal origination of the world is established – and it is impossible for it to be after non-existence upon what it is of the subsistence of the firsts by the lasts and the agreement of that – it is known that it was by knowledge of it. Then, since it is impossible that there be a sense by which it is known while there is no sensed thing, or an analogy without a pattern, it is established that He is Knowing by His essence. And in both cases, knowledge is of the essence of the knower, whether the known is absent or present. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof for God's attribute of knowledge (ṣifat al-‘ilm). Maturidi first establishes that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith). He emphasizes that it is impossible for the world to have come into existence after non-existence in the precise manner it does – with the earlier parts supporting the later parts (e.g., causes and effects) and all parts agreeing harmoniously – without being preceded by knowledge. Therefore, the world must have been created by knowledge. Next, he argues that this knowledge cannot be a sensory knowledge, because there was no pre-existent object to sense, nor can it be analogical knowledge without a pattern. Therefore, the agent must be Knowing by essence (ʿālim li-dhātih), meaning that knowledge is an essential attribute, not something acquired. Finally, he notes that whether the known object is absent or present, the knowledge of the knower is the same; this is a key point to avoid saying that God's knowledge changes. Thus, the proof moves from the contingency of the world to the necessity of God's essential knowledge.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
علم
عالم لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge is acquired or changes based on the object.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's knowledge is essential and does not depend on the existence or absence of the known object; it is the same whether the known is present or absent.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that God's knowledge must be by essence (li-dhātih)?
A) Because the world is too complex for any other type of knowledge.
B) Because sensory knowledge requires a pre-existing object, and analogical knowledge requires a pattern, both of which are absent.
C) Because essential knowledge is simpler.
D) Because the world was created from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that God's knowledge is an essential attribute, not acquired, by showing that sensory and analogical knowledge are impossible in creation from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is essential and not acquired, what does that mean for your secret thoughts? Do you live as though God knows them by essence, not as a distant observer, but as His very nature?
?
The proof shows that creation cannot exist without knowledge. In your own life, what are you trying to build or achieve without first having the knowledge of its purpose? How does that reflect on your trust in the All-Knowing?
?
The passage ends with 'no power except with Allah.' Do you truly rely on His knowledge and power in your daily actions, or do you act as if your own acquired knowledge is sufficient?

God's Eternal Power Proved by Creation
Maturidi proves that God is powerful by essence from the existence of created things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's power is essential and not accidental, as proven by the existence of temporal things.

Core Idea
Creation itself proves that its Maker is eternally powerful.

Maturidi presents a concise logical argument for God's essential power. The question is whether God was able to create before He created. The answer reveals a fundamental truth about divine attributes: God's power is not a temporary quality but inherent to His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the proof
The proof is: 'the existence of the temporal indicates His power.' Understand why an incapable being could not create.
3
Essential vs accidental power
Maturidi emphasizes that God is powerful by His essence, not by an accidental power. This means His power is eternal and limitless.
Contrast with created beings whose power is acquired and temporary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن الله: أكان قادرًا على خلق الأشياء قبل خلقها؟ زعم أنه «نعم». دليله أن العاجز ممنوع، فدل وجود المحدث على قدرته. وإذا كان هو قادرًا بذاته، لا بما يعرض من القدرة، فهو موصوف بالقدرة على الدنيا وأمثالها مما لا يحصى.
Then he was asked about God: Was He able to create things before creating them? He claimed, 'Yes.' Its proof is that the incapable is prevented, so the existence of the temporal indicates His power. And since He is powerful by His essence, not by power that is an accident, He is described as having power over all the world and its like without limit.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This is a short argument proving God's eternal power. The questioner asks whether God was able to create things before He actually created them. The answer is yes, and the proof is that the existence of temporal created things shows that their maker must have had the power to bring them into existence, because an incapable being would be prevented from creating. Furthermore, God's power is essential, not accidental, meaning it is not something that comes and goes. Therefore His power is unlimited and extends to everything possible.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
قدرة (qudra)
محدث (muḥdath)
ذات (dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is a separate quality that He acquires or that can be removed.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God is powerful by His essence—His power is not an accidental attribute but intrinsic to who He is. Therefore, it is eternal and not subject to change.

Checkpoint
What proof does Maturidi give that God was able to create things before He created them?
A) The Qur'an says so.
B) If God were incapable, He would be prevented from creating, but creation exists.
C) All things are possible for God.
D) The question is invalid.

Key Takeaway
God's power is essential and eternal, proven by the very existence of created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says God was powerful before creating. Do you live as if God's power is fully available to you right now, even before you see results? What would change in your du'a if you truly believed His power is essential and unlimited?
?
When you observe the creation around you—your own existence, the world's order—do you take it as mere fact, or does it strike you as a direct proof of God's eternal power? What one created thing can you look at today to remind yourself that its Maker is infinitely powerful?
?
If God's power is not a temporary or acquired ability but a permanent part of His essence, how should that shape your trust in Him when you face something that seems impossible? Commit to one specific prayer today where you act on the belief that His power has no limits.

The Contingency of Rest and Motion
Muhammad ibn Shabib's opening argument: bodies always have rest or motion, both originated, so the body is originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Muhammad ibn Shabib's argument that bodies are originated because they are never free from the contingent states of rest and motion.

Core Idea
Rest and motion are inseparable from bodies, yet both are clearly contingent — proving the body's own temporality.

Muhammad ibn Shabib, a theologian of the early period, offered a compelling proof for the createdness of bodies. His argument begins with a simple observation: every body is always either at rest or in motion. Before we examine the deeper implications, let's read his opening reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Focus on the first sentence: 'bodies are never free from rest or motion'. Then read the translation.
Rest is defined as 'staying' (مقام) and motion as 'departure' (الظعن).
2
Identify the two states
Notice that the passage names two states: rest (سكون) and motion (حركة). These are always present in a body.
3
See why they are originated
The passage says both are originated (محدثان) because they differ with respect to place by the precedence of one over the other. This means they are not eternal.
Eternal things do not change priority, but rest and motion succeed each other.
4
Connect to the body's origination
Since the body is never free from these originated states, the body itself must be originated. The passage concludes 'it is established that it has occurred in one of them' and even if that originated aspect is called 'matter', it is still originated.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج محمد بن شبيب في حدث الأجسام. واحتج محمد بن شبيب في حدث الأجسام بما لا تخلو من سكون هو مقام، وحركة هي الظعن؛ وهما محدثان بما يختلف على الاثنين المكان بما تقدم أحدهما؛ ثبت أن قد حدث في أحدهما، ولو سُمّي ذلك مادة في أحد الجسمين فالقول فيها إنها حدثت. وفي حدوث غير ما قلنا، قد يُعلم بحدوث الزوال وجود الجسم في غير موضع تراه في الأول. وبهذه الضرورة التي أظهرت في الجسم من الانتقال علمنا الحركة التي لا تُحس؛ إذ وجدنا اختلاف الحال في المحسوس، وعرفنا باعتماد الشيء في المكان الأول وانتقاله إلى المكان الثاني أن المسمى اعتمادا في الحال الأول يصير حركة ونقلة في الحالة الثانية، مما لا توصف الحركة بمناسبة الجسم ولا مباينته، إذ ذلك حق الجسم. ثم زيد أحق من عمر و بها لأن توهم حركة عمرو لانعدامها عن زيد إذا وجد هو في غير المكان الأول.
Argument of Muhammad ibn Shabib on the origination of bodies. And Muhammad ibn Shabib argued for the origination of bodies by [the fact] that they are never free from rest, which is staying, and motion, which is departure; and both are originated, by what differs upon the two [as to] place by the precedence of one of them; it is established that it has occurred in one of them. And if that is called matter in one of the two bodies, the statement concerning it is that it originated. And in the occurrence of other than what we said, it may be known by the occurrence of the passing away of the existence of the body in a place other than the one you see at first. And by this necessity that is manifested in the body from transition, we know motion that is not sensed; since we found a difference of state in the sensed, and we knew by the thing's reliance in the first place and its transition to the second place that what is called reliance in the first state becomes motion and transfer in the second state, such that motion is not described as commensurate with the body nor as separate from it, for that is the right of the body. Then Zayd is more entitled than Amr to it, because the assumption of Amr's motion [exists] due to its absence from Zayd when he is found in a place other than the first.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents Muhammad ibn Shabib's argument for the origination of bodies (حدث الأجسام). The argument is based on the observation that bodies are never free from either rest (سكون) or motion (حركة). Rest is described as 'staying' (مقام) and motion as 'departure' (الظعن). Both rest and motion are originated (محدثان) because they differ with respect to place by one preceding the other. Therefore, it is established that origination occurs in one of them. If that originated aspect is called 'matter' in one of the two bodies, then it is also originated. Additionally, the occurrence of something other than what we said (i.e., the cessation of one state) can be known by the passing away (زوال) of the body's existence in its first location. Through this observable necessity of transition in a body, we infer motion that is not sensed directly. By observing a thing's reliance (اعتماد) in the first place and its transition to the second, we know that what is called 'reliance' in the first state becomes motion and transfer in the second. Motion is not described as commensurate with the body nor as separate from it, because that [relation] is proper to the body. The argument concludes with an analogy: Zayd is more entitled than Amr to something, because the assumption of Amr's motion exists due to its absence from Zayd when he is found in a place other than the first. This final part appears to illustrate the principle of distinguishing subjects.

Key Terms
سكون
حركة
مقام
الظعن
محدث
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies might be eternal because they seem permanent and rest/motion are natural to them.
Correction: The argument shows that rest and motion are not natural necessities; they are contingent states that alternate, proving the body's originatedness.

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad ibn Shabib, why are rest and motion considered originated?
A) Because they are both created by Allah directly.
B) Because they differ with respect to place by the precedence of one over the other.
C) Because they are sensed by humans.
D) Because they are inseparable from the body.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are proven originated because they are never free from rest or motion, both of which are contingent and originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is never free from rest or motion, and each state is created by Allah in that moment. How does this awareness change the way you view your own fatigue and energy? Will you consciously attribute your ability to move or be still to Allah today?
?
If every physical state you experience is originated and contingent, what does that imply about your self-reliance? What specific area of your life have you been acting as if it is permanent and under your control?
?
The alternation of rest and motion proves they are not eternal. In what ways do you treat your habits or routines as if they are 'natural' and not gifts from Allah? What would it look like to thank Allah for a single moment of rest or motion today?
