From Sensory Change to Unseen Motion
Muhammad ibn Shabib explains how we know motion from transition and concludes with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muhammad ibn Shabib uses observable transition in a body's location to infer the existence of motion that is not directly sensed, and the transformation of reliance into motion.

Core Idea
Observable change in a body's position logically necessitates motion, even if motion is not directly sensed.

After establishing the basic contingency of rest and motion, Muhammad ibn Shabib delves deeper. He argues that we can know motion even when we do not see it, by observing a body's change of place. This leads to a subtle analysis of reliance and motion, and a concluding analogy. Let's read this second part carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'وفي حدوث غير ما قلنا' to the end. Focus on the idea of passing away (زوال) and transition.
The passage includes an analogy at the end that is somewhat obscure; we will note it tentatively.
2
Understand the inference to unsensed motion
The passage says that through the necessity of transition in the body, we know motion that is not sensed. The observed difference of state in the sensed leads us to infer the unsensed.
This is an argument from sensory evidence to non-sensory reality.
3
Analyze the transformation of reliance
What is called 'reliance' (اعتماد) in the first place becomes 'motion and transfer' in the second. This shows that the same substrate changes its description.
Reliance and motion are two aspects of the same underlying reality.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The final sentence compares Zayd and Amr. It seems to illustrate that motion is attributed to the subject that actually moves, not to another. Take it as a tentative illustration.
This part needs manuscript verification; treat as secondary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجاج محمد بن شبيب في حدث الأجسام. واحتج محمد بن شبيب في حدث الأجسام بما لا تخلو من سكون هو مقام، وحركة هي الظعن؛ وهما محدثان بما يختلف على الاثنين المكان بما تقدم أحدهما؛ ثبت أن قد حدث في أحدهما، ولو سُمّي ذلك مادة في أحد الجسمين فالقول فيها إنها حدثت. وفي حدوث غير ما قلنا، قد يُعلم بحدوث الزوال وجود الجسم في غير موضع تراه في الأول. وبهذه الضرورة التي أظهرت في الجسم من الانتقال علمنا الحركة التي لا تُحس؛ إذ وجدنا اختلاف الحال في المحسوس، وعرفنا باعتماد الشيء في المكان الأول وانتقاله إلى المكان الثاني أن المسمى اعتمادا في الحال الأول يصير حركة ونقلة في الحالة الثانية، مما لا توصف الحركة بمناسبة الجسم ولا مباينته، إذ ذلك حق الجسم. ثم زيد أحق من عمر و بها لأن توهم حركة عمرو لانعدامها عن زيد إذا وجد هو في غير المكان الأول.
Argument of Muhammad ibn Shabib on the origination of bodies. And Muhammad ibn Shabib argued for the origination of bodies by [the fact] that they are never free from rest, which is staying, and motion, which is departure; and both are originated, by what differs upon the two [as to] place by the precedence of one of them; it is established that it has occurred in one of them. And if that is called matter in one of the two bodies, the statement concerning it is that it originated. And in the occurrence of other than what we said, it may be known by the occurrence of the passing away of the existence of the body in a place other than the one you see at first. And by this necessity that is manifested in the body from transition, we know motion that is not sensed; since we found a difference of state in the sensed, and we knew by the thing's reliance in the first place and its transition to the second place that what is called reliance in the first state becomes motion and transfer in the second state, such that motion is not described as commensurate with the body nor as separate from it, for that is the right of the body. Then Zayd is more entitled than Amr to it, because the assumption of Amr's motion [exists] due to its absence from Zayd when he is found in a place other than the first.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents Muhammad ibn Shabib's argument for the origination of bodies (حدث الأجسام). The argument is based on the observation that bodies are never free from either rest (سكون) or motion (حركة). Rest is described as 'staying' (مقام) and motion as 'departure' (الظعن). Both rest and motion are originated (محدثان) because they differ with respect to place by one preceding the other. Therefore, it is established that origination occurs in one of them. If that originated aspect is called 'matter' in one of the two bodies, then it is also originated. Additionally, the occurrence of something other than what we said (i.e., the cessation of one state) can be known by the passing away (زوال) of the body's existence in its first location. Through this observable necessity of transition in a body, we infer motion that is not sensed directly. By observing a thing's reliance (اعتماد) in the first place and its transition to the second, we know that what is called 'reliance' in the first state becomes motion and transfer in the second. Motion is not described as commensurate with the body nor as separate from it, because that [relation] is proper to the body. The argument concludes with an analogy: Zayd is more entitled than Amr to something, because the assumption of Amr's motion exists due to its absence from Zayd when he is found in a place other than the first. This final part appears to illustrate the principle of distinguishing subjects.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is only known through direct sensory perception of movement.
Correction: Ibn Shabib shows that motion can be inferred from the change of a body's location, even when the motion itself is not sensed.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Observable change in a body's location provides necessary proof of motion, even when motion itself is not directly perceived.

Ibn Shabib says Zayd is more entitled to motion than Amr—the one who changes is the one attributed with motion. Consider your own life: are you attributing the changes in your character to the right agent? Or do you sometimes credit yourself for what is actually a movement from Allah?
?
The passage treats 'reliance' and 'motion' as two descriptions of the same reality. How often do you consider that your current state—whether stillness or activity—is not separate from your ultimate trajectory? Proof of Tawhid from Opposition of Essences
Maturidi demonstrates that the coexistence of opposites like heat and cold cannot result from two eternal principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the natural opposition of qualities like heat and cold to argue for the existence of a single wise Creator.

Core Idea
Contrary eternal principles cannot combine; only a single wise Maker can harmonize opposites.

Maturidi presents a cosmological argument based on the observed opposition of qualities in the world. He argues that if there were two eternal principles, their inherent opposition would prevent them from cooperating to produce a harmonious universe. This leads to the conclusion that there must be one wise Manager who brings opposites together.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
Notice how Maturidi builds the argument step by step.
2
Identify the key premises
List the premises: opposites exist (e.g., heat/cold), eternal derivation is impossible, opposites naturally repel, and their combination is impossible without a harmonizer.
The argument relies on both empirical observation and rational reasoning.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow how Maturidi moves from the fact of opposition to the invalidity of two eternal principles, then to the necessity of a single wise Manager.
The argument shows that if opposites could act against their natures, they could cease to exist, which is not observed.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the only coherent account is tawhid: one wise Creator who harmonizes opposites.
This proof strengthens the Islamic doctrine of divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بما إذ ثبت تضاد الأشياء من الثقل والخفة والحرارة والبرودة ونحو ذلك. وقد ثبت فساد [كون] الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له. ثم كان من طبع المتضاد التنافر، وفي ذلك التباعد وبخاصة إذ جعل أصحاب هذا القول اثنين متباينين فامتزجا وكانا متضادين، لم يجز لهما الاجتماع بما ذكر مع ما كان اختلافهما طباعا، ولو جاز خروجهما عن الطباع الذي ذكرت لجاز أن يسخن المبرد ويبرد المسخن، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز فناؤهما ليخرجا من طبع البقاء. وإذا بطل ذا ثبت قول أهل التوحيد في مدبر عليم ألف بين ذلك ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And he argued: since the opposition of things—like heaviness and lightness, heat and cold, and so on—is established, and it is established that [the derivation] of a thing from a thing without a first [cause] is corrupt. Then it is in the nature of opposites to repel each other, and in that is separation, especially when the proponents of this view make two distinct [principles] intermix while they are opposites. Their combination is not permissible as mentioned, given that their difference is inherent. If their departure from the nature that we mentioned were permissible, then it would be permissible for a cooling thing to heat and a heating thing to cool; and if that were permissible, their annihilation would be permissible so that they depart from the nature of permanence. And if this is invalid, the statement of the people of tawhid about a wise Manager who harmonized that is established. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents a cosmological proof for tawhid (the oneness of God) based on the incompatibility of opposite essences. The argument proceeds in several steps: (1) The world contains opposite qualities like heat and cold. (2) It is false that a thing comes from a thing without a first cause (i.e., an infinite regress is invalid). (3) Opposites by nature repel each other and tend to separate. (4) If two opposite eternal principles (like light and darkness, or heat and cold) were to combine to produce the world, their opposition would prevent harmonious combination. (5) If one claims that these opposites could act against their natures (e.g., cooling things heat), then they could also cease to exist, but we observe that they persist. Therefore, the only coherent account is that there is one wise Manager (mudabbir ʻalīm) who brings opposites into harmonious existence. This underscores the necessity of a single, wise creator who overcomes the natural repulsion of opposites.

Key Terms

ثقل
خفة
حرارة
برودة
توحيد
مدبر عليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could be produced by two eternal opposite principles (e.g., light and darkness) cooperating.
Correction: Opposites by nature repel and cannot harmoniously combine. Their coexistence in an orderly world requires a single wise harmonizer, not two conflicting sources.

Checkpoint

B) Because heat and cold are the same thing
C) Because there are two opposite eternal principles
D) Because the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The opposition of qualities in the world proves that there cannot be two eternal principles; rather, there is one wise Creator who harmonizes all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the moments when your own inner contradictions—your desires for both good and evil—clash, do you rely on the single wise Manager to harmonize them, or do you try to manage on your own? What would it look like to truly submit to His arrangement today?

This argument shows that two opposing eternal forces cannot coexist. Yet in your life, you might act as if you can serve both Allah and your own whims. Reflect: where are you trying to combine two contrary loyalties? How will you commit to one Master alone starting now?
?
Māturīdī says that if opposites could act against their nature, they would cease to exist. Think of a habit or attitude in your life that goes against your true nature as a servant of Allah. If you keep acting against your fitrah, what is at stake? What step will you take today to return to harmony with your Creator?

Logical Proof for the Oneness of God
Maturidi presents a logical trilemma to prove the impossibility of multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical trilemma that proves the necessity of a single Creator.

Core Idea
Multiple gods cancel each other; only one can truly be God.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a powerful logical argument that demolishes the idea of multiple gods. By examining three logical possibilities, he shows that only one God can exist without contradiction. This argument is central to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: the argument considers three scenarios.
The argument is a trilemma.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage outlines three logical possibilities regarding the power to annihilate the other: each can, neither can, or only one can. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the flaws in each scenario
Analyze why the first two scenarios lead to incapacity and ignorance, and why the third scenario nullifies the second god.
This proves that only one God is rationally possible.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the existence of more than one God is logically impossible. Reflect on how this establishes tawhid.
This argument is known as Burhan al-Tamānuʿ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } نقول - وبالله التوفيق - إن القول بأكثر من واحد لا يخلو من أن كل واحد منهم يملك إفناء غير [ ] أو لا، أو يملك الواحد خاصة. فإن كان الأول أو الثاني لحقهما عجز، مع ما فيه من الجهل بتدبير الإهلاك بالحيل، إن لم يكن بالقوة. وإن قدر الواحد بطل غيره، لما لا يتركه يعاديه في ملكه وينازعه في ربوبيته، وله قدرة تصفية الملك
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say—and with Allah is success—that the statement about more than one [god] is free from the possibility that each of them has the power to annihilate the other or not, or that only one has that power. If it is the first or the second, they would both be affected by incapacity, along with the ignorance involved in managing destruction by devices, if not by force. And if one is able, the other is nullified, because he would not leave him to oppose him in his dominion and dispute his lordship, and he has the power to purify the dominion.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a classic logical proof for the oneness of God (tawḥīd) from the impossibility of two omnipotent beings. It presents a trilemma: if there are two gods, then either (1) each can annihilate the other, or (2) neither can annihilate the other, or (3) only one can. In cases (1) and (2), both gods would be imperfect (one would lack power to destroy, or both would be mutually checkmated), leading to incapacity (ʻajz). Additionally, if they needed to use devices to destroy each other, that would imply ignorance (jahl), which is not fit for deity. In case (3), if only one has the power to annihilate the other, then the other is not truly a god, because he would be subject to destruction. Moreover, the truly powerful god would not allow a rival to oppose him in his dominion. Therefore, the only consistent position is that there is precisely one God who has full power over all. This argument is a standard kalam proof for tawḥīd, often attributed to the method of mutual exclusion (tamānuʻ).

Key Terms
وحدانية
قدرة
عجز
ملك
ربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could coexist if they agree never to interfere with each other.
Correction: The argument shows that true divinity demands absolute sovereignty; a god would not permit a rival to exist, as it would challenge his lordship.

Checkpoint
According to the logical argument, what happens if only one of two supposed gods has the power to destroy the other?
A) They both remain gods
B) The other god is nullified because the powerful one would not tolerate a rival
C) They share power equally
D) The powerful god becomes ignorant

Key Takeaway
The existence of more than one God is logically impossible because it leads to contradiction and weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that only one God has absolute power, how would that change the way you manage your fears of other people's opinions or of losing control over your life? What action can you take today to let that truth shape your decisions?
?
The proof shows that any rival to God is nullified. In your life, what 'rivals'—such as wealth, status, relationships, or personal ambition—do you treat as if they have power equal to or greater than God? What would it mean to let this logical truth annihilate their hold on you?
?
Consider the third scenario: the one who has power to annihilate does not leave a rival. If you claim to believe in one God, are there areas of your heart where you still harbor a 'rival'—something you fear, love, or rely on as if it were independent of His dominion? What would it take for you to surrender that area completely to Him today?

Proof Against More Than One Eternal Being
Maturidi proves that there cannot be more than one God using a power-based argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical trilemma that demonstrates why there cannot be more than one eternal being.

Core Idea
If two eternal beings exist, one must be weak or the other is eliminated.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a powerful rational argument for tawhid. By considering the logical possibilities when two eternal beings are posited, Maturidi shows that each leads to a contradiction. Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's logical flow come through before interpreting.
Notice that Maturidi uses a trilemma structure.
2
Identify the three scenarios
List the three possibilities regarding annihilation power that Maturidi considers for two eternal beings.
The three are: both have power, neither has power, only one has power.
3
Consider why each scenario fails
For each scenario, reflect on the consequence Maturidi identifies (weakness, ignorance, or nullification) and why it disproves multiplicity.
This connects to the proof that only one eternal being can exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: نقول - وبالله التوفيق - إن القول بأكثر من واحد لا يخلو من أن كل واحد منهم يملك إفناء غيره أو لا، أو يملك الواحد خاصة. فإن كان الأول أو الثاني لحقهما عجز، مع ما فيه من الجهل بتدبير الإهلاك بالحيل، إن لم يكن بالقوة. وإن قدر الواحد بطل غيره، لما لا يتركه يعاديه في ملكه وينازعه في ربوبيته، وله قدرة تصفية الملك له. وبعد، فإن العاجز الجاهل أحق أن يكون عبدا مربوبا دون أن يكون ربا ملكا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur – may God have mercy on him – said: We say – and with God is success – that the statement about more than one cannot be free from that each one of them has the power to annihilate the other or not, or one alone has that power. If the first or the second, then weakness befalls them, along with what there is of ignorance in managing destruction by ruses, if not by power. And if one is capable, the other is nullified, because he would not leave him to oppose him in his dominion and contend with him in his lordship, while he has the power to purify the kingdom for himself. Moreover, the weak, ignorant one is more deserving to be a slave, subjugated, rather than a lord and king. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof against the possibility of more than one God. The author, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, presents a trilemma: if there were two eternal beings, either (1) both have the power to annihilate each other, (2) neither does, or (3) only one does. In cases (1) and (2), each is either weak (if the other can destroy it) or ignorant (if it cannot manage destruction), which contradicts perfection. In case (3), the one with power would eliminate the other, so the other is not truly eternal. Thus, the unique powerful being alone deserves lordship; any weak or ignorant being is more fit to be a servant. This argument underscores the necessity of tawhid.

Key Terms
إفناء
عجز
جهل
ربوبية
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two eternal beings could coexist peacefully without competing for power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even without conflict, logical necessities of power and weakness arise, making multiplicity impossible.

Checkpoint

A) Both are eternal.
B) The other is nullified.
C) Both are weak.
D) They coexist peacefully.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's trilemma proves that only one eternal being can exist, thus affirming tawhid.

?

Refuting the Materialist Analogy of Colors and Tastes
Maturidi refutes the materialist analogy using three arguments from mixture, color, and taste.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi refutes the materialist analogy by showing that the order and diversity in colors and tastes necessitate a wise, knowledgeable Creator.

Core Idea
Materialist analogies inadvertently prove the need for a wise Designer.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi takes a materialist analogy—that the world's order arises from the mixture of natures, like dyes—and turns it against them. He shows that if you examine the analogy carefully, it actually requires a wise and knowledgeable mixer to produce a perfect result, just as the world's perfection points to a wise Creator. He then reinforces this with additional arguments from colors and tastes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get an overview of Maturidi's refutation. Notice the flow from the dye analogy to the arguments about colors and tastes.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the three key arguments
As you read, pick out the three distinct arguments Maturidi uses: (1) Dyes do not mix by themselves; only a wise mixer produces a good color. (2) The same color can be associated with both hot and cold things, so color is not due to inherent nature. (3) The same nature and color can produce different tastes, proving management from a wise agent.
Each argument independently refutes materialism.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how Maturidi concludes that the one who originated the world is a wise manager (mudabbir hakim) and that formation must be from nothing. Ask yourself: How does each argument lead to this conclusion?
This connects the detailed arguments to the central teaching of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن تأمل تذل القول بما ضربوا له من المثل وجده يثبت قول أهل التوحيد لأن الأصباغ لأنفسها لا تمتزج؛ ثم هي لو امتزجت لأنفسها خرجت على لون مستسمج مما عُد ذلك في العقول فساد للأصباغ. فإذا مزجها حكيم عليم يعلم عواقب ذلك المزاج خرج متقناً مستحسنا. ثم كان العالم خرج متقنا، ثبت أن الذي به كان العالم عليم حكيم يعرف عواقب الأشياء فأخرجها على ذلك. وفي ذلك فساد أن تكون تلك الطبائع أو الطينة أو ما سمّوا من الأسماء لنفسها صارت بحيث يكون على ما عليه يخرج، فثبت أن الذي أنشأها كذلك مدبّر حكيم، ويجب تكوينها لا من شيء. مع ما كانت الألوان - كل لون منها ـ لا يوصف بشيء مما ذكروا من الحرارة والبرودة، إذ قد يكون في الأشياء شيء يغلب عليه لون منها وهو حار، وآخر يغلب عليه ذلك وهو بارد؛ فثبت أنه لم يكن شيء من ذلك الألوان بما ذكروا ولا ما ذكروا بها، وفي ذلك إيجاب غير الذي قالوا. وبالله التوفيق. وكذلك نجد ما فيها من الطعوم مختلفة، حتى يكون بلون واحد وطبيعة واحدة يخرج على نوع من الطعم نحو الملوحة أو الحموضة أو المرارة أو الطينة التي لا تضرب إلى شيء من ذلك. ثبت أن ذلك كان بتدبير من يملك جعل كل على ما شاء من غير أسباب. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
For he who ponders the statement (tadhillu al-qawl) regarding the example they gave finds it establishes the statement of the people of tawhid, because the dyes by themselves do not mix. Furthermore, if they mixed by themselves, they would produce a repulsive color, which would be considered in the intellects as a corruption of the dyes. But if a wise knowledgeable one who knows the consequences of that mixture mixes them, it comes out perfect and pleasing. Since the world has come out perfect, it is established that the one by whom the world is is knowledgeable and wise, who knows the consequences of things and brought them out accordingly. And in that is the corruption of the claim that those natures, or the matter, or whatever names they called—by themselves—became such that they would come out as they do. So it is established that the one who originated them is a wise manager, and their formation must be from nothing. Along with that: colors—each color—is not described by any of what they mentioned of heat and cold, for there may be in things something that a certain color predominates over it while it is hot, and another that a certain color predominates over it while it is cold. So it is established that none of those colors are due to what they mentioned, nor what they mentioned due to them, and in that is the affirmation of other than what they said. And with Allah is success. And likewise we find that the tastes in them are different, to the extent that with one color and one nature there may come out a type of taste such as saltiness, sourness, bitterness, or a clay-like taste that does not tend towards any of that. It is established that that was due to the management of one who has the power to make each as He wills without causes. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the materialist analogy of dyes. Imam al-Maturidi argues that if someone examines the analogy the materialists used, he will find it actually proves the truth of monotheism (tawhid). He gives three arguments: First, the dyes themselves do not mix by themselves. If they did, the result would be repulsive (mustasmaj), which would be considered a corruption of the dyes in the intellect. Only a wise and knowledgeable mixer who knows the consequences can produce a perfect and pleasing color. Since the world came out perfect (mutqanan), it necessarily follows that its Author (alladhi bihi kana al-ʿalam) is knowledgeable and wise. This invalidates the materialist claim that the natures themselves produce order. Second, colors are not inherently described as hot or cold; a thing can be hot while having a certain color and cold with the same color, so the natures cannot cause the colors. Third, tastes vary even with the same color and nature; one thing may be salty, another sour, etc., showing that a wise manager (mudabbir hakim) gives each its property directly, not through necessary causes. Thus, the materialist doctrine is false, and the existence of a wise creator is established.

الأصباغ
الحكيم
المدبّر
الطبائع

التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The materialist analogy of colors and tastes proves that the world's order arises from natural processes without a creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the analogy actually proves the opposite: order and diversity require a wise, knowledgeable agent, not blind nature.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Maturidi argue about the mixing of dyes?
A) Dyes mix by themselves naturally.
B) Dyes if mixed by themselves produce a perfect color.
C) Dyes do not mix by themselves and need a wise agent.
D) Dyes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation demonstrates that the materialist analogy, when examined carefully, actually proves the existence of a wise Creator and the need for creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you enjoy a meal and appreciate its colors and tastes, do you truly believe that each color and taste is directly given by Allah without any necessary cause? How would your gratitude change if you realized that same meal could have been tasteless or repulsive if Allah had willed it so?
?
Consider the dyes argument: when something beautiful or orderly emerges in your life, do you habitually attribute it to your own planning or natural processes? What would it mean to pause and say, 'This perfection is from a Wise Manager' — and live accordingly?
?
Maturidi shows that materialist explanations collapse because they cannot account for the variety of tastes from the same nature. Is there an area in your own beliefs where you rely on natural causes as if they are independent? What would it cost you to replace that default with direct reliance on Allah's wisdom and power?

Refuting the Natures Theory: The Pigment Analogy
Maturidi refutes the four-natures theory using the pigment mixing analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the theory of four eternal natures by deconstructing their own analogy of pigment mixing, demonstrating the necessity of a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Pigments don't mix themselves — a wise mixer proves a Wise Creator.

The proponents of the theory of four natures argued that the world's diversity arises from mixing heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. They supported this with an example from pigments: colors vary only by proportion, not by emergence of new colors. Imam Maturidi takes their example and turns it against them, showing that pigments require an external mixer, and thus the world requires a wise Creator. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the natures theory
Learn what the proponents claimed: four primary natures (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) are eternal, and the world's variation results from their mixing.
This sets the stage for the refutation.
2
Examine their pigment analogy
They compared the natures to pigments (white, red, black, green) that mix proportionally without creating new colors.
This analogy is central to their argument.
3
Reflect on mixing by itself
Consider what would happen if pigments mixed on their own: Maturidi says they would produce an ugly color, which reason deems corruption.
This exposes the flaw in their analogy.
4
Grasp the need for a wise mixer
When a wise, knowing agent mixes pigments, the result is perfect and beautiful. Since the world is perfect, its Creator must be Knowing and Wise.
This is the core of Maturidi's counter-argument.
5
See the broader refutation
Note additional arguments: colors are not inherently hot or cold, disproving the claimed connection, and the natures cannot be self-sufficient.
This solidifies the proof for a Creator from nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعم أصحاب الطبائع أنهنّ أربع حرّ وبَرد، ونُدُوةٌ ويُبْس. واختلف العالم باختلاف الامتزاج منها، واعتدل ما اعتدل منها باستواء المزاج منها . وعلى ذلك مجرى الشمس والقمر والنجوم، لم يزل يجرى بمثل الذي يجرى كما تُرى؛ لا أول للأشياء. وسموا حركاتها أعراضاً، وضربوا الباطلهم هذا مَثَلاً من نحو الأصباغ كالبياض والحمرة والسواد والخضرة، إنها عند الامتزاج على قدر الكثرة والقلة والرّقة والكثافة تختلف ألوانها، لا أن يكون ثمة حادث لون، وإن كان ربما يخرج على ما لا يعرف أهل هذه الألوان أن ذلك مم خرج، فمثله ما ذكروا من الطبائع. { قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فمن تأمل تذل القول بما ضربوا له من المثل وجده يثبت قول أهل التوحيد لأن الأصباغ لأنفسها لا تمتزج ؛ ثم هي لو امتزجت لأنفسها خرجت على لون مستسمج مما عُد ذلك في العقول فساد للأصباغ. فإذا مزجها حكيم عليم يعلم عواقب ذلك المزاج خرج متقناً مستحسنا . ثم كان العالم خرج متقنا ، ثبت أن الذي به كان العالم عليم حكيم يعرف عواقب الأشياء فأخرجها على ذلك. وفي ذلك فساد أن تكون تلك الطبائع أو الطينة أو ما سمّوا من الأسماء لنفسها صارت بحيث يكون على ما عليه يخرج، فثبت أن الذي أنشأها كذلك مدبّر حكيم، ويجب تكوينها لا من شيء. مع ما كانت الألوان - كل لون منها ـ لا يوصف بشيء مما ذكروا من الحرارة والبرودة، إذ قد يكون في الأشياء شيء يغلب عليه لون منها وهو حار، وآخر يغلب عليه ذلك وهو بارد؛ فثبت أنه لم يكن شيء من ذلك الألوان بما ذكروا ولا ما ذكروا بها ، وفي ذلك إيجاب غير الذي قالوا. وبالله التوفيق.
The proponents of the natures claimed that they are four: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. The world varies by variation of mixture from them, and what is balanced from them is balanced by the equality of the mixture from them. And upon that is the course of the sun, moon, and stars, they continuously run in the same manner as you see; there is no beginning for things. They called their movements accidents, and they set forth as an example for their falsehood, an example from pigments like whiteness, redness, blackness, and greenness: that they, in mixing, according to quantity and quality, thinness and thickness, their colors differ, not that there is a newly originated color, although it may sometimes emerge in a way that the people of these colors do not know from what it emerged. So their mention of the natures is like that. Said Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him: So whoever ponders the speech about the example they set forth will find it confirms the statement of the people of tawhid, because pigments by themselves do not mix; then if they mixed by themselves, they would emerge in an ugly color, which is counted in intellects as corruption of pigments. But if a wise, knowledgeable one mixes them, knowing the consequences of that mixture, it emerges perfectly and beautifully. Then since the world emerged perfectly, it is established that the One by Whom the world exists is Knowledgeable, Wise, knowing the consequences of things, and He brought them out accordingly. In that is the corruption of those natures or the clay or whatever names they have called, that they by themselves could become such that the world would emerge as it does. So it is established that the One who originated them thus is a wise manager, and their coming-into-being must be from nothing. Moreover, colors—each color of them—cannot be described by anything of what they mentioned of heat and cold, for there can be among things something whose color predominates and it is hot, and another whose color predominates and it is cold. So it is established that none of those colors are due to what they mentioned, nor what they mentioned due to them. And in that is affirming other than what they said. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the theory of four primary natures (taba'i')—heat, cold, moisture, dryness—which their proponents claimed are eternal and that all change results from their mixing. Maturidi begins by stating their claim and then examines their example of pigments: they say that colors like white, red, black, green vary only by proportion of mixture, not by emergence of a new color, analogous to how the natures produce the world. Maturidi, quoting himself as Shaykh Abu Mansur, critiques this analogy. He argues that pigments by themselves do not mix; if they did, they would produce an ugly color, deemed corruption by reason. But if a wise, knowing agent mixes them, the result is perfect and beautiful. Since the world is perfect, the agent must be Knowing and Wise, knowing consequences. Therefore, the natures cannot be self-sufficient; they require a wise manager who also creates them from nothing. Furthermore, colors are not inherently hot or cold, as the same color can appear in both hot and cold substances, disproving the claimed connection. Thus the very example meant to support the natures in fact proves a Creator beyond them.

امتزاج
أصباغ
حكيم
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidi accepts the validity of the natures theory and only argues about details.
Correction: Maturidi is actually dismantling the theory entirely, using their own example to prove the necessity of a wise Creator and the impossibility of self-sufficient natures.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about the example of pigments given by the proponents of the natures?
A) The example proves the natures are eternal.
B) The example actually proves the necessity of a wise Creator.
C) The example is irrelevant and cannot be analyzed.
D) The example shows that colors are caused by heat and cold.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the pigment analogy, which his opponents used to support the natures theory, actually proves the need for a wise Creator who brings the world into existence from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see the order in your life: your health, your provision, the moments of balance. Do you truly believe this is the product of random mixing—or the work of a Wise Mixer who knows every consequence? What would change if you lived every day aware that He is actively blending your affairs with purpose?
?
The founders of the natures theory thought they could explain the world without God. Where in your own thinking do you subtly act as if things will sort themselves out—your character, your relationships, your deen—without actively seeking the Wise Mixer's involvement? What one area can you consciously hand over to Him today?
?
Maturidi says without a wise mixer, the mixture results in ugliness and corruption. If you were to examine the 'mixture' of your daily choices—your time, your words, your intentions—would the outcome be beautiful or corrupted? What would need to change in your reliance on Allah's wisdom to make it beautiful?

The Argument from Diverse Tastes
Maturidi argues that different tastes from a single nature prove a wise Designer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity of tastes found in a single natural substance points to a wise Creator who freely assigns qualities.

Core Idea
Uniform nature cannot produce diverse tastes; a wise Designer alone can explain it.

Imam Maturidi offers a simple yet profound observation from everyday experience: substances with one color and one nature produce different tastes. This diversity cannot be due to the nature itself, so it must come from an external, wise Manager who creates each as He wills. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the key terms: طعوم (tastes), طبيعة (nature), تدبير (management), أسباب (causes).
2
Identify the observation
Maturidi observes that from a single color and nature, different tastes emerge—saltiness, sourness, bitterness, or none. This is the starting point of the argument.
The uniformity of nature is emphasized: 'one color and one nature'.
3
Understand the inference
Since the nature is uniform, it cannot account for the diversity. Therefore, the diversity must come from an external agent who manages (تدبير) and makes each as He wills (جعل كل على ما شاء). This agent is the wise Creator.
The phrase 'without causes' (من غير أسباب) negates any inherent causal necessity in the nature itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument establishes that the diversity of tastes is a sign of divine will and wisdom. It refutes those who attribute such variations to nature alone or to random chance.
The final phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' reminds us that all power is from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك نجد ما فيها من الطعوم مختلفة، حتى يكون بلون واحد وطبيعة واحدة يخرج على نوع من الطعم نحو الملوحة أو الحموضة أو المرارة أو الطينة التي لا تضرب إلى شيء من ذلك . ثبت أن ذلك كان بتدبير من يملك جعل كل على ما شاء من غير أسباب. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And likewise we find what is in them of tastes diverse, so that with one color and one nature there emerges a type of taste, such as saltiness, sourness, bitterness, or the clay that does not incline to any of that. It is established that this was by the management of One Who owns making each as He wills without causes. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a brief but powerful argument from the diversity of tastes. Maturidi observes that from a single substance (e.g., a type of clay or soil) with one color and one nature, various tastes such as saltiness, sourness, or bitterness can emerge. This variation cannot be explained by the nature itself, because the nature is uniform. Therefore, the differences must come from an external agent who freely assigns these qualities without being constrained by causes. This agent is the wise Creator who manages all things. The phrase 'without causes' negates the idea of inherent causal necessity, affirming divine will.

Correction: Maturidi argues that a uniform nature, by itself, cannot produce diverse outcomes; the diversity points to an external, intelligent agent who freely assigns qualities without being constrained by natural causes.

?
This passage ends with a declaration of complete dependence on Allah. If you truly believed that even the taste of your food is a direct gift from Him, how would that change your gratitude and your relationship with the Creator?

Natures as Substances or Accidents: Both Lead to the Creator
Maturidi demonstrates that both possibilities for natures require a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's logical argument that whether natures are substances or accidents, both possibilities necessitate the existence of an eternal, all-knowing, all-powerful Creator.

Core Idea
Whether a nature is a substance or an accident, it cannot exist without a Creator.

Imam Maturidi engages with the philosophical theory of natures (taba'i') by offering a powerful dilemma. He argues that whatever natures are, they must be either substances or accidents. By examining each possibility, he shows that both lead inexorably to the necessity of a Creator. This passage is a classic example of kalam reasoning, using rational classification to refute alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Recognize that Maturidi presents a logical fork: natures are either substances or accidents. He will examine both branches.
2
Follow the substance branch
If natures are substances, they are subject to accidents like conjunction and separation. Notice how this shows their dependence and the need for a knowing agent.
Pay attention to the phrase 'by One who knows'.
3
Follow the accident branch
If natures are accidents, they cannot exist on their own, so they require an eternal Originator.
4
Absorb the conclusion
Both branches converge on the same point: the necessity of a single, all-powerful Creator. Maturidi ends by affirming that the temporality of accidents is beyond dispute.
This is a strong refutation of the natures theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هذه الطبائع لا تخلو من أن يكون جواهر أو أعراضاً. فإن كانت جواهر صرن بالأعراض التي اعترضت فيها على ما ذكر من الاختلاف، وهي الاجتماع والافتراق، ولولاهما لكان كل جوهر من ذلك متفرقا. ودل اختلاف الجواهر مع اجتماع الأخلاط فيها على غلبة الأعراض عليها، وأنها تُصرفها من حال إلى حال. ثم كانت الأعراض لأنفسها لا تقوم ولا تقدح في الأشياء؛ ثبت أنها عملت فيها هذا العمل بمن يعلم أنها تعمل كذا. ولم يجز أن يكون يعلم أحدٌ ذلك إلا من يملك جعل تلك الجواهر يصلح لاحتمال تلك الأعراض، ومحال علم مثله إلا بمن يجعلها كذلك. وفي ذلك لزوم القول بواحد عليم قادر لا يخفى عليه شيء ولا يصعب عليه تكوين ما يريد كونه . وإن كانت أعراضاً فمحال وجودها لأنفسها وقيامها ؛ فلزم القول بموجد قديم، مع إيجاد ما فيه، وبه يدخل في حد الوجود. على أن حدث الأعراض مما لا تَمَانُع فيه . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Further, these natures cannot be free from being either substances or accidents. If they are substances, they are acted upon by accidents that occur in them, as mentioned of variation, namely, conjunction and separation; without them every substance would be scattered. And the variation of substances together with the combination of mixtures in them indicates the dominance of accidents over them, and that they turn them from state to state. Then, since accidents by themselves do not subsist nor affect things, it is established that they performed this action through One who knows that they act thus. And it is not possible that anyone knows that except One Who owns making those substances fit to bear those accidents, and knowledge of the like of it is impossible except through One who makes them thus. In that is the necessity of affirming One Knowing, Able, from Whom nothing is hidden, nor is it difficult for Him to bring into being what He wills. And if they are accidents, then their existence and subsistence by themselves is impossible; thus it is necessary to affirm an Eternal Originator, together with bringing into being what is in it, and by that it enters into the limit of existence. Moreover, the temporality of accidents is indisputable. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a logical classification argument. Maturidi considers the natures (taba'i') and forces the opponent into a dilemma: either they are substances (jawahir) or accidents (a'rad). If they are substances, they are affected by accidents like conjunction and separation, without which they would be scattered. The variation in substances due to mixtures shows that accidents dominate and change them. But accidents themselves do not subsist or act independently, so the effect must come from One who knows that they act in such a way, and only He can make the substances capable of bearing accidents. This leads to affirming a single, all-knowing, all-powerful Creator. If, on the other hand, the natures are accidents, they cannot exist or subsist by themselves; thus they need an eternal Originator who brings them and their substrate into existence. Moreover, the fact that accidents are temporally originated (hadith) is undeniable. Therefore, the natures theory collapses, and belief in a Creator is necessitated.

Key Terms
جواهر
أعراض
اجتماع
افتراق
موجد قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natures as substances could be eternal and self-sufficient.
Correction: Maturidi shows that substances are affected by accidents like conjunction and separation, proving they are contingent and require a Creator.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude if natures are accidents?
A) They are eternal.
B) They require an eternal Originator.
C) They are self-subsistent.
D) They are merely concepts.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma argument shows that whether natures are substances or accidents, both possibilities lead to the necessity of a single, all-powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the constant changes in your own body and circumstances, do you truly acknowledge that only Allah knows and controls those changes? The passage argues that accidents cannot act alone. Where in your life are you still attributing change to mere ‘nature’ or ‘time’? What would it take for you to genuinely credit every shift to Allah’s direct knowledge and power?
?
If accidents (like health, wealth, or moods) have no power of themselves, why do you sometimes fear their loss more than you fear displeasing Allah? The proof shows that only the Creator gives them effect. Will you today consciously remind yourself, ‘There is no power except by Allah,’ and let that transform your heart’s attachment to worldly states?
?
The argument demands that you affirm a single, all-knowing, all-powerful Creator. But does your daily schedule reflect this belief? Examine your last three decisions: did you act as if outcomes depend on your planning and effort alone, or did you rely on the One who brings accidents into existence? What one habit can you change to live out this affirmation more deeply?

The Necessity of a Unifier for Opposing Natures
Maturidi argues from opposition to the necessity of a unifier, establishing the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why opposing natures cannot coexist eternally and why a unifier is required.

Core Idea
Opposition leads to repulsion and dispersal; a unifier is necessary for existence.

Maturidi begins his refutation by examining the inherent conflict within the natures. He argues that their opposition would inevitably cause repulsion, leading to separation and annihilation. Therefore, they cannot be self-subsisting; a unifier must bring them together.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once through. Notice how Maturidi builds the argument from opposition to the need for a preventer.
Focus on the logical chain: opposition → repulsion → separation → dispersal → annihilation.
2
Identify the unifier's role
Identify the 'preventer' or 'unifier' (جامع) that Maturidi says is necessary. Understand that this unifier is the Creator.
3
Connect to creation
Reflect on how the argument establishes the temporality (حدوث) of the world. Ask yourself: why does the need for a unifier imply creation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه معلوم أن تلك الطبائع هي متضادة، وحق التضاد التدافع، وفي ذلك تفرق، وفي التفرق تبدد وتفان. فلم يُحتمل أن تكون أصول الأشياء لأنفسها كائنة وقائمة مع التناقض الذي ذكرت ثبت أنها إن كانت كانت بمانع عن التدافع الذي فيها التبدّد؛ وهو الجامع بينها بعد التفرق، القاهر لها، وبالجمع كان العالم؛ ثبت حدوثه. وفي ذلك فساد القول بالطبائع ، لأن كون شيء لا عن شيء ليس بأبعد في العقول من قيام الشيء مع ضده وهو ما ينقضه. ولبعد ذلك عن عقولهم صاروا إلى ما قالوا فإذا لزمهم فيما قالوا مثل الذي عنه فَروا بطل قولهم وذهب عذرهم. وبالله العصمة. وقوم قالوا بمثل ذلك، إلا أنهم زعموا أن ليس لأجناس [الطبائع ] عدد يعرفونه، وكلهم قالوا بقدم الأشياء في جميع جملتها : من مهب الشمال والجنوب والدبور والصبا ومن أعلاها وأسفلها.
And after that, it is known that those natures are opposed, and the requirement of opposition is repulsion, and in that is separation, and in separation is dispersal and annihilation. So it is not plausible that the origins of things by themselves could be and subsist with the contradiction I mentioned; it is established that if they exist, they exist by a preventer of the repulsion in which is dispersal, and He is the unifier between them after separation, the subjugator of them, and by unification the world existed; thus its temporality is established. In that is the corruption of the statement about the natures, because a thing coming from nothing is not more remote in intellects than a thing subsisting along with its opposite which annuls it. And due to the remoteness of that from their intellects, they resorted to what they said; then when they are obligated in what they said to the like of what they fled from, their statement is invalidated and their excuse vanishes. And with Allah is protection. And some people said something similar, except they claimed that there is no known number for the kinds of natures, and all of them affirmed the eternity of things in their entirety: from the blowing of the north wind, the south wind, the west wind, and the east wind, and from their highest and lowest.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the final refutation based on opposition. Maturidi states that the natures are mutually opposed (e.g., heat vs. cold, moisture vs. dryness). Opposition naturally causes repulsion, leading to separation, dispersal, and annihilation. Therefore, it is impossible for these opposed natures to be self-subsisting and eternal. If they exist, they must have a preventer that stops the repulsion—a unifier that brings them together after separation and subjugates them. This unifier is the Creator, and through His unification, the world exists. Hence the world is temporally originated (hadith). Maturidi also points out the absurdity of the opponents' position: they accept that something can come from nothing (the natures) but reject that something can coexist with its opposite—yet the latter is less absurd. Thus their own theory leads to the same conclusion they fled from, namely, the need for a Creator. Finally, he notes that some people held a similar view but without specifying the number of natures, yet all affirmed the eternity of the world in all its parts.

Key Terms
تضاد
تدافع
جامع
قاهر
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the consequence of the natures being opposed?
A) They naturally combine into a balanced whole.
B) They produce a perfect world spontaneously.
C) They would repel and separate, leading to dispersal.
D) They cancel each other out and become neutral.

Key Takeaway
Opposing natures cannot be self-subsisting; a unifier is necessary, proving that the world is created and not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's existence depends on a Unifier preventing chaos, what does this imply for the coherence and purpose of your own life? Are you living as if you can sustain yourself without Him? Commit to one practical way today to acknowledge Allah as the Unifier in a specific area of your life.
?
Reflect on areas of opposition and conflict within yourself—your desires pulling against your duties, your ambitions fighting your faith. Without a unifier, these lead to inner dispersal and ruin. How does this lesson call you to seek Allah's help to unify your heart and actions? What step will you take this week to turn back to Him as the Subjugator of your soul?
?
Maturidi argues that the world's order is not self-sufficient but requires a subjugator (قاهر). In what ways do you resist submitting to the One who has subdued all things? What would it look like for you to willingly place yourself under His authority today, especially in a matter where you have been insisting on your own way?

Refutation of the Naturists
Maturidi refutes the Naturists by showing their position leads to a greater absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Naturists by showing the absurdity of their position.

Core Idea
Claiming a thing comes from nothing is more absurd than a thing coexisting with its opposite.

After establishing the need for a unifier, Maturidi turns to refute the Naturists. He points out that they accept something coming from nothing (their natures) but reject the possibility of something coexisting with its opposite—yet the latter is less absurd. Thus their own logic undermines them.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic again, focusing on the comparison of absurdities. Notice how Maturidi uses their own reasoning against them.
Key phrase: 'كون شيء لا عن شيء ليس بأبعد في العقول من قيام الشيء مع ضده'
2
Analyze the comparison
Maturidi says that the idea of something coming from nothing is not more remote in intellects than a thing subsisting with its opposite. Ask yourself: which is more absurd? Why does this refute the Naturists?
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Naturists' position is invalid because it leads to the same absurdity they fled from. Their own theory requires a Creator.
Also note the comment about those who didn't specify the number of natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

The Dichotomy of Natures: Substances or Accidents
Maturidi presents the first arm of his refutation: natures as substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if natures are substances, their subjection to accidents proves the need for a wise Creator.

Core Idea
If natures are substances, their change demands an external knower and maker.

Maturidi begins his refutation by forcing the proponents of natures into a logical dichotomy: they must be either substances or accidents. He then demonstrates that both options lead to the existence of a Creator. In this lesson, we focus on the first branch—the case where natures are considered substances—and see how it necessitates a single, all-knowing, all-powerful being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the dichotomy and then follows the substance branch step by step.
The argument moves from 'if substances, then subject to accidents' to 'accidents need a director'.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify what is meant by substances (jawāhir) and accidents (aʿrāḍ). Understand that in kalām, substances are the fundamental particles, and accidents are non-essential properties.
Accidents like 'combination' and 'separation' are introduced as examples.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow how Maturidi shows that substances, if left to themselves, would be separate, but they combine—this indicates accidents dominate them. Since accidents don't subsist, they must be directed by an external knower.
4
Conclusion
The conclusion is that a single, all-knowing, all-powerful God is necessary. Ask yourself why the argument insists on unity and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هذه الطبائع لا تخلو من أن يكون جواهر أو أعراضاً. فإن كانت جواهر صرن بالأعراض التي اعترضت فيها على ما ذكر من الاختلاف، وهي الاجتماع والافتراق، ولولاهما لكان كل جوهر من ذلك متفرقا. ودل اختلاف الجواهر مع اجتماع الأخلاط فيها على غلبة الأعراض عليها، وأنها تُصرفها من حال إلى حال. ثم كانت الأعراض لأنفسها لا تقوم ولا تقدح في الأشياء؛ ثبت أنها عملت فيها هذا العمل بمن يعلم أنها تعمل كذا. ولم يجز أن يكون يعلم أحدٌ ذلك إلا من يملك جعل تلك الجواهر يصلح لاحتمال تلك الأعراض، ومحال علم مثله إلا بمن يجعلها كذلك. وفي ذلك لزوم القول بواحد عليم قادر لا يخفى عليه شيء ولا يصعب عليه تكوين ما يريد كونه . وإن كانت أعراضاً فمحال وجودها لأنفسها وقيامها؛ فلزم القول بموجد قديم، مع إيجاد ما فيه، وبه يدخل في حد الوجود. على أن حدث الأعراض مما لا تمانع فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد، فإنه معلوم أن تلك الطبائع هي متضادة، وحق التضاد التدافع، وفي ذلك تفرق، وفي التفرق تبدد وتفان. فلم يُحتمل أن تكون أصول الأشياء لأنفسها كائنة وقائمة مع التناقض الذي ذكرتُ ثبت أنها إن كانت كانت بمانع عن التدافع الذي فيها التبدّد؛ وهو الجامع بينها بعد التفرق، القاهر لها، وبالجمع كان العالم؛ ثبت حدوثه. وفي ذلك فساد القول بالطبائع، لأن كون شيء لا عن شيء ليس بأبعد في العقول من قيام الشيء مع ضده وهو ما ينقضه. ولبعد ذلك عن عقولهم صاروا إلى ما قالوا فإذا لزمهم فيما قالوا مثل الذي عنه فَروا بطل قولهم وذهب عذرهم. وبالله العصمة.
وقوم قالوا بمثل ذلك، إلا أنهم زعموا أن ليس لأجناس الطبائع عدد يعرفونه، وكلهم قالوا بقدم الأشياء في جميع جملتها : من مهب الشمال والجنوب والدبور والصبا ومن أعلاها وأسفلها.
Concerning that these natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) cannot be free from being either substances (jawāhir) or accidents (aʿrāḍ). If they are substances, they become subject to accidents that occur to them according to what was mentioned of difference, namely combination (ijtimāʿ) and separation (iftirāq); without these two, every substance would be separate. And the difference of substances despite the combination of elements in them indicates the dominance of accidents over them, and that they turn them from one state to another. Then, since accidents by themselves do not subsist and do not effect in things, it is established that they performed this action in them by one who knows that they act thus. And it is not possible that anyone knows that except one who can make those substances fit to accept those accidents, and knowledge like that is impossible except by one who makes them so. In that is the necessity of affirming one Knowing, Powerful, from whom nothing is hidden, and to whom the bringing into being of what He wills to be is not difficult. And if they are accidents, then their existence by themselves and their subsistence is impossible; so the affirmation of an eternal bringer-into-being is necessary, along with the bringing into being of what is in it, and through that it enters into the limit of existence. Moreover, the origination of accidents (ḥudūth al-aʿrāḍ) is something about which there is no denial. And there is no power except through God. After this, it is known that those natures are mutually opposing (mutadāddah), and the property of opposition is repulsion; in that is separation, and in separation is disintegration and annihilation. So it is not plausible that the principles of things could be, by themselves, coming-into-being and subsisting with the contradiction I mentioned; it is established that they were only [able to be] through an obstacle to the repulsion that contains disintegration—that is what brings them together after separation, the subjugator over them; and through the bringing-together the world came to be; so its temporal origination (ḥudūth) is established. In that is the corruption of the doctrine of natures (al-ṭabāʾiʿ), because the existence of a thing from non-existence is not more remote in intellects than the subsistence of a thing along with its opposite, which is what negates it. And because that [reason] is remote from their intellects, they resorted to what they said; and if they are compelled by what they said to the very thing they fled from, their statement is invalidated and their excuse is gone. And with God is protection. And some people said similarly, except that they claimed that the genera of natures have no known number, and all of them affirmed the pre-eternity (qidam) of things in their entirety: from the blowing of the north, south, west, and east winds, and their highest and lowest parts.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a complex refutation of the doctrine that the world is governed by inherent natures (ṭabāʾiʿ). Maturidi begins by stating that these natures must either be substances or accidents. If they are substances, they are subject to accidents of combination and separation, which shows that accidents dominate and change them. Since accidents have no independent subsistence, their effect must come from an external agent who knows how to make substances receptive. This leads to the necessity of a single, all-knowing, all-powerful Creator. If the natures are accidents, they cannot subsist by themselves, so an eternal originator is required. Maturidi then notes that natures are mutually opposed, which should cause disintegration; yet the world persists, implying a unifying force that compels them together—this shows the world is created. He concludes that the doctrine of natures is false because it is as unreasonable as positing the existence of a thing without a cause. Finally, he mentions a second group who believe in natures but deny that they can be enumerated; they also affirm the pre-eternity of all things, which is equally invalid.

Key Terms
طبائع (ṭabāʾiʿ)
جواهر (jawāhir)
أعراض (aʿrāḍ)
اجتماع (ijtimāʿ)
افتراق (iftirāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natures (or substances) can act independently without a Creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their changeability and subjection to accidents prove they need an external knower and enabler.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that accidents cannot be the ultimate cause of change in substances?
A) Because accidents are eternal and unchanging.
B) Because accidents do not subsist by themselves and need a substrate.
C) Because substances are more powerful than accidents.
D) Because accidents are not real.

Key Takeaway
If natures are substances, their dependence on accidents necessitates a single all-powerful Creator who knows and enables all change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that every change you experience—even the combination of elements in your own body—requires a knowing, powerful Creator. When you look at your own daily routines, where have you attributed changes to mere chance or habit rather than to the direct action of Allah? Will you consciously remind yourself today that nothing changes except by His will?
?
The argument concludes that it is impossible for anyone to know how accidents bring about effects except the One who made substances receptive. In what area of your life do you act as if you are the independent cause of your successes, forgetting that Allah is the one who enabled and knew? What can you do this week to return that feeling of power to Him alone?
?
If accidents cannot effect change by themselves, then your own inability to change a bad habit is not just weakness—it is a sign that you need the One who truly changes states. What specific habit have you been trying to change without turning to Allah? Will you today, for one minute, sincerely ask Him to make you fit to accept the change?

From Accidents and Opposition to the Creator
Maturidi completes his refutation with the accident branch and opposition argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments that natures as accidents require an eternal originator, and that the opposition among natures proves the world's temporality.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot found themselves, and opposing natures need a unifier.

Having shown that natures as substances lead to God, Maturidi now takes the second branch: natures as accidents. Since accidents cannot exist by themselves, they too require an eternal bringer-into-being. Then he presents a powerful argument from the mutual opposition of natures: if left to themselves, they would annihilate each other, but the world persists—proving a subjugating force that brought them together, which establishes the world's creation. Finally, he declares the doctrine false and mentions a variant view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the transition from the accident branch to the opposition argument.
The phrase 'وإن كانت أعراضاً' signals the second branch.
2
Understand the accident argument
Accidents cannot subsist alone, so they need an eternal being to bring them into existence along with their substrates.
This is a classic kalām argument for a Creator.
3
Understand the opposition argument
Natures are opposing; opposition leads to repulsion, separation, and annihilation. But the world's persistence shows there is a unifier.
The unifier must be external and powerful.
4
Final conclusion
The doctrine is false because it is as absurd as saying a thing comes from nothing, and the opponents are compelled to accept the very thing they fled from.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هذه الطبائع لا تخلو من أن يكون جواهر أو أعراضاً. فإن كانت جواهر صرن بالأعراض التي اعترضت فيها على ما ذكر من الاختلاف، وهي الاجتماع والافتراق، ولولاهما لكان كل جوهر من ذلك متفرقا. ودل اختلاف الجواهر مع اجتماع الأخلاط فيها على غلبة الأعراض عليها، وأنها تُصرفها من حال إلى حال. ثم كانت الأعراض لأنفسها لا تقوم ولا تقدح في الأشياء؛ ثبت أنها عملت فيها هذا العمل بمن يعلم أنها تعمل كذا. ولم يجز أن يكون يعلم أحدٌ ذلك إلا من يملك جعل تلك الجواهر يصلح لاحتمال تلك الأعراض، ومحال علم مثله إلا بمن يجعلها كذلك. وفي ذلك لزوم القول بواحد عليم قادر لا يخفى عليه شيء ولا يصعب عليه تكوين ما يريد كونه . وإن كانت أعراضاً فمحال وجودها لأنفسها وقيامها؛ فلزم القول بموجد قديم، مع إيجاد ما فيه، وبه يدخل في حد الوجود. على أن حدث الأعراض مما لا تمانع فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد، فإنه معلوم أن تلك الطبائع هي متضادة، وحق التضاد التدافع، وفي ذلك تفرق، وفي التفرق تبدد وتفان. فلم يُحتمل أن تكون أصول الأشياء لأنفسها كائنة وقائمة مع التناقض الذي ذكرتُ ثبت أنها إن كانت كانت بمانع عن التدافع الذي فيها التبدّد؛ وهو الجامع بينها بعد التفرق، القاهر لها، وبالجمع كان العالم؛ ثبت حدوثه. وفي ذلك فساد القول بالطبائع، لأن كون شيء لا عن شيء ليس بأبعد في العقول من قيام الشيء مع ضده وهو ما ينقضه. ولبعد ذلك عن عقولهم صاروا إلى ما قالوا فإذا لزمهم فيما قالوا مثل الذي عنه فَروا بطل قولهم وذهب عذرهم. وبالله العصمة.
وقوم قالوا بمثل ذلك، إلا أنهم زعموا أن ليس لأجناس الطبائع عدد يعرفونه، وكلهم قالوا بقدم الأشياء في جميع جملتها : من مهب الشمال والجنوب والدبور والصبا ومن أعلاها وأسفلها.
Concerning that these natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) cannot be free from being either substances (jawāhir) or accidents (aʿrāḍ). If they are substances, they become subject to accidents that occur to them according to what was mentioned of difference, namely combination (ijtimāʿ) and separation (iftirāq); without these two, every substance would be separate. And the difference of substances despite the combination of elements in them indicates the dominance of accidents over them, and that they turn them from one state to another. Then, since accidents by themselves do not subsist and do not effect in things, it is established that they performed this action in them by one who knows that they act thus. And it is not possible that anyone knows that except one who can make those substances fit to accept those accidents, and knowledge like that is impossible except by one who makes them so. In that is the necessity of affirming one Knowing, Powerful, from whom nothing is hidden, and to whom the bringing into being of what He wills to be is not difficult. And if they are accidents, then their existence by themselves and their subsistence is impossible; so the affirmation of an eternal bringer-into-being is necessary, along with the bringing into being of what is in it, and through that it enters into the limit of existence. Moreover, the origination of accidents (ḥudūth al-aʿrāḍ) is something about which there is no denial. And there is no power except through God. After this, it is known that those natures are mutually opposing (mutadāddah), and the property of opposition is repulsion; in that is separation, and in separation is disintegration and annihilation. So it is not plausible that the principles of things could be, by themselves, coming-into-being and subsisting with the contradiction I mentioned; it is established that they were only [able to be] through an obstacle to the repulsion that contains disintegration—that is what brings them together after separation, the subjugator over them; and through the bringing-together the world came to be; so its temporal origination (ḥudūth) is established. In that is the corruption of the doctrine of natures (al-ṭabāʾiʿ), because the existence of a thing from non-existence is not more remote in intellects than the subsistence of a thing along with its opposite, which is what negates it. And because that [reason] is remote from their intellects, they resorted to what they said; and if they are compelled by what they said to the very thing they fled from, their statement is invalidated and their excuse is gone. And with God is protection. And some people said similarly, except that they claimed that the genera of natures have no known number, and all of them affirmed the pre-eternity (qidam) of things in their entirety: from the blowing of the north, south, west, and east winds, and their highest and lowest parts.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a complex refutation of the doctrine that the world is governed by inherent natures (ṭabāʾiʿ). Maturidi begins by stating that these natures must either be substances or accidents. If they are substances, they are subject to accidents of combination and separation, which shows that accidents dominate and change them. Since accidents have no independent subsistence, their effect must come from an external agent who knows how to make substances receptive. This leads to the necessity of a single, all-knowing, all-powerful Creator. If the natures are accidents, they cannot subsist by themselves, so an eternal originator is required. Maturidi then notes that natures are mutually opposed, which should cause disintegration; yet the world persists, implying a unifying force that compels them together—this shows the world is created. He concludes that the doctrine of natures is false because it is as unreasonable as positing the existence of a thing without a cause. Finally, he mentions a second group who believe in natures but deny that they can be enumerated; they also affirm the pre-eternity of all things, which is equally invalid.

Key Terms
أعراض (aʿrāḍ)
حدث (ḥudūth)
قديم (qadīm)
التضاد (al-taḍādd)
طبائع (ṭabāʾiʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That opposing natures can coexist indefinitely by themselves without a unifier.
Correction: Maturidi argues that their natural opposition would cause annihilation; their coexistence proves an external subjugator.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi think the doctrine of natures is false?
A) Because it leads to the absurdity of a thing subsisting with its opposite.
B) Because natures are not real.
C) Because it denies the existence of accidents.
D) Because it affirms creation ex nihilo.

Key Takeaway
Whether natures are accidents or substances, and considering their opposition, the only coherent conclusion is that God is the sole Creator and the world is originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world's order and persistence despite the conflicting forces within it (e.g., fire and water, hot and cold), do you truly recognize the hand of the Subjugator (al-Qāhir) holding it together? Or do you take this stability for granted, as if it were a given? Commit today to see in every moment of peace and coherence a sign of His constant intervention.
?
Māturīdī says the doctrine of natures collapses because believing in self-sufficient causes is as absurd as believing something comes from nothing. Have you ever, even implicitly, attributed independence to natural causes—trusting in medicine, wealth, or your own efforts as if they could act without the Creator's permission? Repent from this hidden shirk.
?
The passage concludes that the opponents' excuse is gone. When you read arguments like this, does your heart submit, or does it resist? What would it mean for you to truly let go of any intellectual pride and accept that your existence and the world's existence depend entirely on God's bringing into being every moment?
