God's Creation Is for Consequences
Maturidi argues for divine action oriented toward consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God creates for the sake of consequences and not for His own benefit or mere destruction.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency means His actions are for outcomes, not personal gain.

Imam Maturidi presents a rational principle to explain the purpose of divine creation. He argues that since God is free from all needs, He cannot benefit from His creation, nor would He create for destruction alone. This leads to the conclusion that creation is for the consequences — the outcomes that befall creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the key terms: الأصل, ينتفع, الهلاك, العواقب.
2
Identify the rational principle
Notice the opening statement: 'whoever does an act from which he does not benefit, or does it for his own destruction, is blameworthy.' This is the axiom that drives the argument.
Consider why this principle is considered self-evident.
3
Apply the principle to God
Maturidi applies the principle to Allah: He cannot benefit because He is self-sufficient, and He does not create for destruction. Therefore, the only remaining purpose is the consequences.
Think about what 'consequences' might refer to (reward, punishment, etc.).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ثم الأصل أن من يفعل فعلاً لا ينتفع هو به أو يفعله لهلاكه وفنائه أنه عائب. والله سبحانه لم يكن لينتفع بما ينشئه لتعاليه عن الحاجات وغناه بنفسه عن غيره، فيبطل أن يكون فعله لينتفع به هو. ثم لو كان للهلاك لا غير لكان لا معنى لخلقه؛ فثبت أن خلق العالم وكونه للعواقب.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Then the principle is that whoever does an act from which he does not benefit, or does it for his own destruction and annihilation, is blameworthy. And Allah, exalted, is not such that He would benefit from what He creates, due to His transcendence above needs and His self-sufficiency from other than Him. So it is invalid that His act should be for His own benefit. Then, if it were for destruction only, there would be no meaning to His creation. So it is established that the creation of the world and its existence is for the consequences.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle about divine action. Imam Maturidi states as an axiom that any agent who performs an act without benefit to himself, or does it for his own destruction, is blameworthy. This is a general principle of rationality. Applied to God, since He is transcendent and free from all needs, He cannot benefit from His creation. Therefore, His act of creation cannot be for His own benefit. Nor can it be for mere destruction, as that would be meaningless. Hence, the only remaining rational purpose is that creation is for the sake of the consequences (العواقب) — i.e., the outcomes that befall creatures, such as reward and punishment.

Key Terms
الأصل
ينتفع
الهلاك
العواقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God creates for His own benefit or for mere destruction.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God's self-sufficiency precludes benefit, and destruction alone is meaningless; therefore creation must be for consequences.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Maturidi conclude that creation is for the consequences?
A) Because God benefits from creation
B) Because God is self-sufficient and cannot benefit, and destruction alone is meaningless
C) Because the world is eternal
D) Because prophets said so

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that divine creation is purposeful, aiming at consequences for creatures, not for God's self-interest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your daily worship feel like a transaction where you give God something He needs, or do you truly grasp that He is self-sufficient and everything you do is for your own consequence? What would change if you lived in that reality?
?

?
The argument rejects purposelessness. Where in your life have you been living as if God’s commands are arbitrary or meaningless? What one step will you take this week to act as if every divine command has a consequence for you?

The Wisdom of Reward and Punishment in Creation
Maturidi argues that wise creation requires accountability for those with understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that divine wisdom necessitates creating accountability through reward and punishment for beings with intellect.

Core Idea
Accountability is necessary because wisdom demands that beings with intellect understand consequences.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a profound argument linking divine wisdom to the necessity of reward and punishment. Maturidi explains that God created two categories of creatures: those without discernment, and those with intellect who can perceive consequences. For the latter, wisdom requires that they experience the outcomes of their actions through pain and pleasure, thereby establishing accountability. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's flow of thought. Note the key terms: تمييز, إدراك, العواقب, الضار والنافع, الألم واللذة.
The Arabic text is incomplete at the end; focus on the argument as presented.
2
Identify the two categories of creation
Find where Maturidi distinguishes between creatures without discernment (تمييز) and those with intellect who perceive consequences. Why are these categories treated differently?
The first group is created not for themselves; the second group is created to seek benefit.
3
Follow the argument to the necessity of pain and pleasure
Trace how Maturidi moves from the existence of intellect to the need for love of God, and finally to the creation of harm and benefit, substances capable of pain and pleasure. Understand the logical chain.
The key phrase is 'إذا لزم ما ذكرنا لزم في الحكمة...'
4
Reflect on the purpose of accountability
Consider how this argument establishes the necessity of reward and punishment in the afterlife. Ask yourself: how does experiencing pain and pleasure help beings learn consequences?
This is the foundation for Maturidi's view on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم خلق خلائق لم يجعل عندها تمييزاً ولا إدراكا لعواقب الأمر، ثبت أنه خلقهم لا لأنفسهم وخلق خلقا يعرفون ذلك ويطلبون بجميع صنيعهم فعله نفع العواقب حتى من خرج عن ذلك. إذ هو محتاج كل غير، حكيم في فعله، فلزمت محبتهم إليه لئلا يضيع نِعَم المنشئ فيهم من العقول التي يدركون بها العواقب؛ ولأنهم أو تُرِكوا وتدبيرهم لم يكونوا يرضون من أنفسهم التقلب فيما لا يؤثر نفعا ولا يعقب حمدا، ومن تعاطى منهم مثله فهو سفيه جاهل. وإذا لزم ما ذكرنا لزم في الحكمة خلق الضار والنافع، وخلق الجوهر المحتمل للألم واللذة، وإنشاء الآلام والملاذ؛ ليعلموا ما
Then He created creatures for whom He did not place discrimination nor perception of the consequences of things. It is established that He created them not for themselves. And He created a creation who know that and seek by all their actions to do the benefit of consequences, even the one who deviates from that. For He is in need of every other, wise in His act, so their love towards Him is necessary lest the blessings of the Originator in them be lost, namely the intellects by which they perceive consequences. And because if they were left and their own management, they would not be pleased with themselves to fluctuate in that which brings no benefit nor leads to praise, and whoever among them engages in that is foolish and ignorant. And if what we have mentioned is necessary, then in wisdom it is necessary to create the harmful and the beneficial, and to create the substance capable of pain and pleasure, and to bring into being pains and pleasures, so that they may know what.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage extends the argument about consequences. Imam Maturidi notes that God created some beings without discernment (e.g., animals, inanimate objects), and these are not created for their own sake. He then created beings with intellect who recognize the importance of beneficial outcomes and strive for them, even those who disobey. Since every other (other than God) is in need, and God is wise, it is necessary that these beings love God so that the blessings of intellect are not wasted. If left to their own devices, they would not be content with actions that bring no benefit or praise, and those who engage in such are foolish. Thus, wisdom dictates the creation of harm and benefit, substances capable of pain and pleasure, and the actual pains and pleasures themselves, so that these beings may learn the consequences of their actions. This implicitly establishes the necessity of reward and punishment (the afterlife).

Key Terms

إدراك
العواقب
الضار والنافع
الألم واللذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating evil and suffering contradicts divine wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi shows that evil and suffering are necessary for beings with intellect to learn consequences, thus establishing justice and accountability.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is evil
B) To test people
C) Because wisdom requires that beings learn the outcomes of their actions through experience of pain and pleasure
D) To make the world balanced

Key Takeaway
Divine wisdom requires that beings with intellect experience the consequences of their actions through pain and pleasure, establishing the necessity of reward and punishment in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The passage says that if left to your own management, you would not be pleased with fluctuation in useless actions. Look at your daily routine: how much of it is truly aimed at the afterlife? What would change if you took this accountability seriously starting today?

The Interconnection of Benefit and Harm Proves One Creator
Maturidi argues from the interconnection of benefit and harm to the unity of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the seamless interconnection of benefit and harm in the world proves that the Creator is one.

Core Idea
Interconnected benefits and harms prove a single Manager behind creation.

This lesson explores a profound proof from Imam Maturidi: the harmony and interdependence of benefit and harm in the universe. By observing how substances interact—each capable of both benefit and harm, yet arranged so that overall good prevails—Maturidi argues that only a single, wise Manager could orchestrate such coherence. You will see how this argument refutes the possibility of multiple creators and establishes the unity of the divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Maturidi's reasoning sink in before moving to commentary.
Notice the technical terms like 'jawhar' (substance) and 'mudabbir' (manager).
2
Identify the key elements of the argument
Look for how Maturidi sets up the alternation of desire/aversion, the purpose of creation, the nature of substances, and the conclusion that the Manager must be one.
Underline or note each step in your own words.
3
Understand the logical structure
Trace the cause-and-effect: if there were multiple creators, their products would conflict; but we observe coherence (ittisāq), so multiplicity is impossible.
Maturidi also includes an epistemological consequence: without a single origin, knowledge itself collapses.
4
Reflect on the example and conclusion
Maturidi gives an example of two substances in one body—benefit and harm would nullify each other unless restrained. This illustrates the need for a single Planner who restrains harm by grace.
Think about how this applies to the world around you.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختلاف ترغب إليه الأنفس وما تهرب منه، فيحذرون ويرغبون بمثله فيما امتحنوا به؛ وليعلموا النفع من الضرر الذي لولا ذلك لم يكن لخلقهم معنى، فخلقهم الله على ما خلق من لهذين. ثم بلطفه خلق كل جوهر محتملاً للنفع والضرر، يحلّ به لغيره، وأوصل منفعة كل جوهر بغيره من الجواهر التي فيها المضار، ليعلم الناظرون أن مدبر ذلك كله واحد، وأنه لو مختلف لتدافع الخلق؛ لأن جوهر الخير إذ لا يجيئ منه غير الخير ومن جوهر الشر غير الشر، لكان صنعُ كل واحد منهما في بعض صنع الآخر، وإفساده بما يقوم مع مثله. فدل الاتساق وتعلّق منافع بعض ببعض على فساد هذا. على أنا إذا لم نقل بأن الكل لواحد لم يحتمل القول منا لعدد؛ إذ لم يقدر واحد منهم على إفراد الذي منه بان [دليل] يدل عليه، ولا أعلم عليه علما يدل عليه، لم يجب بمثله حق المعرفة به والعلم بحاله؛ فيفسد العلم جميعا، لجهل الأصل الذي كل أنواع العلم وفروعه به. ما مع ينفع أحد الجوهرين [في جسم واحد و] يضر الآخر، وفي ذلك يلاقى الضار النافع، فيبطل به نفع ألبتة، لما معه المانع عنه. وفي وجود العالم وما فيه لكل منهم نفع هو الدليل الحق على أن مدبر ذلك كله واحد لحبس كل ضار عن عمله من وجه ضرره باللطف ليصل ما أراد من النفع إلى من أراد نفعه. وهكذا هذه القصة فيمن أراد ضرره ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The alternation: souls desire and flee from it, so they are warned and desire similarly in what they are tested with; and so that they know benefit from harm, without which their creation would have no meaning. So Allah created them as He created these two things. Then, by His grace, He created every substance capable of benefit and harm, affecting others, and He connected the benefit of each substance to other substances that contain harms, so that observers may know that the Manager of all that is one, and that if He were different, creation would conflict; because the substance of good, since only good comes from it, and the substance of evil only evil, then the act of each one would interfere with the act of the other and spoil it by what opposes it. So the coherence and the interconnection of benefits of some with others indicate the corruption of this [multiplicity]. Moreover, if we do not say that everything belongs to one, then we cannot maintain a claim of number; for none of them could single out what is from him by a [proof] that indicates it, nor do I know a knowledge that indicates it, so the right of knowledge of him and knowledge of his state would not be fulfilled; thus all knowledge would be corrupted due to ignorance of the origin on which all types and branches of knowledge depend. How is it that one of the two substances benefits [in one body] and the other harms, and in that the harmful meets the beneficial, so benefit is nullified entirely, because with it is the preventer. And in the existence of the world and what is in it, each of them has a benefit—this is the true proof that the Manager of all that is one, by restraining every harmful thing from its action in the aspect of its harm with grace so that the intended benefit reaches whom He intends to benefit. And such is the story concerning whom He intends harm. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a proof from design and interconnection, demonstrating the oneness of the Creator. Maturidi begins by noting the alternation in human desires and aversions, which serve to teach benefit and harm—the very purpose of creation. He states that Allah created each substance (jawhar) capable of benefiting and harming others, and interconnected their benefits so that observers realize the Manager (mudabbir) of all is one. If there were multiple managers, they would conflict because the substance of good only produces good and the substance of evil only evil, leading to mutual interference and corruption. The coherence (ittisāq) and interconnection of benefits prove the falsehood of multiple creators. Furthermore, if we do not attribute everything to a single origin, we cannot have certain knowledge of the origin itself, and all knowledge becomes corrupted. Maturidi then gives a concrete example: two substances in one body, one beneficial and one harmful, would nullify each other unless restrained. The existence of the world, where each substance has a benefit, is the true proof that the Manager is one, as He restrains harmful things by grace to allow intended benefit. He concludes that the same applies to intended harm.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
مدبر (mudabbir)
اتساق (ittisāq)
نفع (nafʿ)
ضرر (ḍarar)
لطف (luṭf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple creators could coexist without conflict if they cooperate or specialize in different domains.
Correction: Maturidi argues that since substances have inherent natures (good only does good, evil only evil), any multiplicity would lead to interference and conflict, as one's act would spoil the other's. The observed coherence and interdependence prove this cannot happen.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the interconnection of benefits of different substances prove?
A) That each substance is independent
B) That the Manager of all is one
C) That multiple creators can coexist
D) That benefit and harm are illusions

Key Takeaway
The observable harmony and interdependence of benefits in creation necessitate a single, all-wise Manager who restrains harm by grace to accomplish His purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a setback or harm, do you immediately look for the benefit that the one Manager intends, or do you blame people and circumstances? How can you train your heart to see His hand in both the pleasant and the painful?
?
If you truly believe that all benefits and harms are interconnected under one Manager, why do you sometimes attribute your successes to your own efforts and failures to bad luck? What would change if you lived with constant awareness that He alone restrains harm to deliver benefit to you?
?
In what area of your life are you trying to serve two masters—your desires and Allah's commands—hoping they won't conflict? How does Maturidi's argument about the necessary conflict of multiple managers expose the impossibility of that path, and what will you do about it today?

8 min
The Purpose of Intellect and the Wisdom of Diverse Creation
Maturidi argues that intellects are for reflection, requiring diverse substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that intellects are created for reflection and that the diversity of substances in creation is a necessary aspect of divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Intellects are for reflection, not consumption; diversity in creation is key to wisdom.

Maturidi begins by challenging a common assumption: that intellects were created for worldly purposes like eating and drinking. He points to the fact that irrational beings often surpass rational ones in these acts, and that the angels—beings of pure intellect—abstain from them. This sets the stage for his central claim: intellects are created for reflection and contemplation. From this, he argues that the Creator, in His wisdom, must create diverse substances to make the path of reflection complete, and that this diversity itself points to His self-sufficiency, power, and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of Maturidi's reasoning sink in before analysing.
Notice how the argument builds: first establishing the true purpose of intellect, then deducing the necessity of diversity.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify Maturidi's two main premises: (1) intellects are not for eating/drinking, (2) they are for reflection. Then see how he supports each: the first by comparison with creatures lacking intellect and by the example of angels; the second by the praiseworthy outcomes of reflection.
3
Trace the conclusion
From the premises, Maturidi concludes that creating different substances in wisdom is necessary. Then he adds a further argument: in the experienced world, knowing both good and evil leads to greater perfection, so creating both is more obligatory. Finally, he notes that this diversity indicates God's attributes.
The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' appears twice as a reminder of reliance on God.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how the argument shows that the existence of evil or harm is not purposeless but serves the greater purpose of complete knowledge and reflection. This counters the misconception that creation of evil contradicts divine wisdom.
This is a key point in Islamic theology regarding the problem of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما إنه معلوم أن العقول ليست تُرَكَّب للأكل والشرب، لأن ما لا عقل له في ذلك أشد قوة من ما له العقل، ولعظيم محل قوم اتقوا الأكل والشرب في القلوب وهم الملائكة، فثبت أنها خلقت للعبرة والنظر، لما فيه المحامد والمكارم. وإذا كان كذلك لزم خلق مختلف الجواهر في الحكمة ليكون طريق العبرة تاما وحق النظر وافرا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أنه معلوم في الشاهد أن من يعلم الأمرين جميعا هو أتم، بل لا يقدر أحد على اتقاء ما يضره إذا لم يعلمه؛ فعلى ذلك خلق الأمرين في الحكمة أوجب وأتم من خلق أحدهما. مع ما في ذلك من دلالة غنى الفاعل وتمام قوته وعلمه بما يليق بكل شيء أن يكون عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Moreover, it is known that intellects are not composed for eating and drinking, because what has no intellect is stronger in that than what has intellect, and because of the high station of people who abstain from eating and drinking in hearts—they are the angels. So it is established that they [intellects] were created for reflection and contemplation, because of the praiseworthy and noble qualities therein. If that is so, then the creation of different substances in wisdom is necessary so that the path of reflection is complete and the right of contemplation is abundant. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, it is known in the witnessed world that one who knows both matters is more perfect; indeed, no one can avoid what harms him if he does not know it. Therefore, creating both matters in wisdom is more obligatory and more perfect than creating only one. Along with that is an indication of the self-sufficiency of the Agent, the completeness of His power, and His knowledge of what is suitable for each thing to be upon. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the wisdom in creating diverse substances. Maturidi first establishes that intellects (‘uqūl) are not created for base purposes like eating and drinking, since irrational creatures are stronger in that regard. The high station of angels, who abstain from such acts, shows that intellects are for reflection (i‘tibār) and contemplation (naẓar) leading to praiseworthy qualities. Therefore, it is necessary in wisdom (ḥikma) to create various substances to make the path of reflection complete. Maturidi then adds a further point: in the empirical world, one who knows both good and evil is more perfect, and one cannot avoid harm without knowledge. Hence, creating both benefit and harm is more obligatory and perfect than creating only one. This also indicates the Agent’s self-sufficiency, complete power, and knowledge of what befits each thing.

Key Terms
عقول (ʿuqūl)
عبرة (ʿibra)
نظر (naẓar)
حكمة (ḥikma)
أتم (atamm)
فاعل (fāʿil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The creation of evil or harmful things is purposeless and contradicts divine wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi argues that creating both beneficial and harmful things is actually more perfect and necessary for complete knowledge and reflection, and it indicates the Creator's wisdom and power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of creating intellects?
A) Eating and drinking
B) Reflection and contemplation
C) Achieving power
D) Avoiding harm

Key Takeaway
Intellects are created for reflection, and the diversity of substances (including good and evil) is necessary for complete wisdom and points to the perfection of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to use your intellect daily, but how often do you truly reflect on the diversity of creation as a sign of God's wisdom? When was the last time you paused to contemplate a painful event as a necessary part of that wisdom?
?
Maturidi says intellects are not for eating and drinking—yet most of your mental energy goes toward material concerns. Will you commit today to redirect even one hour of your thinking toward reflection on the purpose of your existence?
?
If you only knew good and never experienced hardship, your knowledge would be incomplete and you could not avoid harm. Are you grateful for the trials in your life for the depth of awareness they give you, or do you resent them as pointless?

The Testimony of Creation and Prophetic Proofs
Maturidi presents the testimony of creation and prophetic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the proofs of the prophets and the testimony of everything in creation are sufficient to establish the existence of a wise Creator against any denial.

Core Idea
Creation itself testifies to its temporal origin and a wise Maker.

Maturidi begins by asserting that even if monotheists had only the proofs brought by the prophets, that would be enough to refute all deniers. He then points to a universal evidence: everything in creation, by its very substance, bears witness that it came into existence through a wise Originator. This passage establishes the foundational argument from contingency (ḥudūth) that underlies Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to appreciate its rhetorical style. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the contrast between the prophets' proofs and the atheists' lack of proof.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the two arguments Maturidi presents: (1) the sufficiency of prophetic proofs, and (2) the testimony of every created thing to its temporal origination.
The phrase 'ما من شيء إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بحدثه' is key.
3
Reflect on the concept of ḥudūth
Consider why temporal origination (ḥudūth) points to a creator. Maturidi says it implies a 'mudhith ḥakīm' (wise originator).
This is a basic kalam argument that everything that begins to exist requires a cause.
4
Connect to the lesson objective
See how this passage establishes that the evidence for God is clear and overwhelming, leaving no excuse for denial except obstinacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو لم يكن لما عليه أهل التوحيد سوى أدلة صدق الدعاة إليه والبراهين النيرة معهم، وهم الرسل - مما لا يوجد شيء من ذلك لأحد من منكري الصانع الواحد ـ لكان ذلك كافيا، فكيف وما من شيء إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بحدثه، وأنه حدث لمحدث حكيم؛ لولا تعنت الملحدين بما ادعوا من قدم الأعيان مما لا سبيل لهم في الرجوع إليه إلا إلى تقليد من ليس معه دليل، أو جَعَلِ سفهه ـ وهو عجزه عن الوقوف على كون شيء لا عن شيء ـ دليلاً له. ولا ريب أن كلاً منهم يعلم من نفسه جهلاً بأشياء ثم العلم بها، وعجزا عن أشياء ثم قدرته عليها، وضرورةً إلى أشياء ثم غنى عنها. فحق من هذا وصفه أن لا يثق برأيه ولا يتبع ما يرى أنه من إشارة عقله. مع ما لا يخلو أن من ردّ ذلك إلى الطبائع التي لا تعقل ما يولد منها وبها، وكذلك النجوم، أو إلى عدد من الصانعين مما كان بدء أمرهم الجهل والعمى، أو إلى تقليد أقاويل في قدم الأشياء على ما هي عليه مما يتناقض ويتضاد من أن يكون هو وأمثاله أغبياء متجاهلين فأني لهم العقل مع ثبوت هذه الأصول المتجاهلة الذين هم فروعها، أو الوقوف على حقائق الأشياء حتى يدعون في شيء حكمة أو طريقها أو سفها؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And if the people of Tawheed had nothing but the proofs of the truth of those who call to Him and the luminous proofs with them—they are the messengers—which none of the deniers of the one Maker possess, that would be sufficient. How [much more] when there is nothing but that it testifies by its very substance to its temporal origination, and that it is temporally originated by a wise Originator; were it not for the obstinacy of the atheists in claiming the eternity of entities, for which they have no way of recourse except to imitation of one who has no proof, or making his foolishness—which is his inability to comprehend that something comes from nothing—a proof for him. And there is no doubt that each of them knows of himself ignorance of things then knowledge of them, inability for things then power over them, and need for things then independence from them. So it is the right of one described as such not to trust his opinion nor follow what he sees as a sign of his intellect. Moreover, it is inevitable that whoever attributes that to natures which do not understand what is generated from and by them, and likewise the stars, or to a number of makers whose beginning was ignorance and blindness, or to imitation of sayings about the eternity of things as they are—which contradict and oppose each other—that he and his likes are foolish ignoramuses. So how can they have intellect while these ignorant principles are established, principles of which they are branches? Or how can they attain the realities of things so that they claim wisdom or its way or foolishness in anything? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive refutation of those who deny the one Creator. Maturidi begins by stating that the proofs of the prophets alone are sufficient for monotheists, and more so when everything in creation testifies to its temporality (ḥudūth) and wise Originator. He then attacks the stubbornness of atheists (mulḥidūn) who claim the eternity of entities (qidam al-aʿyān), relying only on blind imitation (taqlīd) or their own inability to conceive creation from nothing. He points out that everyone experiences personal change from ignorance to knowledge, weakness to power, need to independence—so they should not trust their own opinions over revelation. He then refutes those who attribute creation to natures (ṭabāʾiʿ), stars (nujūm), or multiple makers, noting that these views originate in ignorance and blindness. Their claims are contradictory and they themselves are foolish followers. Maturidi questions how such people can claim intellect or understanding of realities while their principles are ignorant. The passage ends with a rhetorical challenge.

Key Terms
توحيد (tawḥīd)
حدوث (ḥudūth)
محدث (muḥdith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That atheists have rational arguments for denying God.
Correction: Maturidi shows they have no proof and rely on blind imitation or foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the deniers of the one Maker lack?
A) Any proof like that of the prophets
B) Intelligence
C) The ability to see created things
D) Followers

Key Takeaway
The testimony of creation and the prophets' proofs are enough to establish the existence of the one wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says everything in creation testifies by its very essence to its temporal origin and a wise Maker. When was the last time you truly let a created thing—a tree, a star, your own heartbeat—speak to you of its Creator? Will you commit this week to pause daily and ask one object in creation: 'What do you tell me about your Maker?'
?
Maturidi states that the deniers have no proof; they merely imitate those without evidence. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah simply because you inherited them from your family or culture? Can you point to the specific evidence from creation or the prophets that grounds your faith? If not, will you seek that evidence today?
?
The proof of the prophets alone is sufficient. Yet how much time do you give to studying the lives and messages of the prophets compared to other pursuits? If you had to defend your faith to a skeptic using only the testimony of creation and the prophetic proofs, could you? Commit to learning one prophetic proof this week, and practice explaining it to someone.

The Intellectual Bankruptcy of Atheists
Maturidi refutes atheists' reliance on taqlid and personal ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique that atheists rely on blind imitation and their own foolishness, compounded by their personal experience of change from ignorance to knowledge.

Core Idea
Atheists cannot trust their own intellects because they experience change and ignorance.

Maturidi continues his refutation by exposing the weakness of the atheists' position. He points out that their claim of eternity is based either on blind imitation (taqlīd) of someone without proof, or on their own inability to conceive how something can come from nothing—a failure they mistake for a proof. He then reminds them that every person experiences personal change: from ignorance to knowledge, from inability to ability, from need to independence. This should make them distrust their own opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two sources of atheist belief mentioned.
Look for the phrases 'تقليد من ليس معه دليل' and 'جعل سفهه دليلاً'.
2
Identify the two critiques
First, Maturidi says atheists imitate someone without proof. Second, they take their own inability to understand creation from nothing as a proof.
Both are forms of irrationality.
3
Consider the personal experience argument
Maturidi points out that each atheist knows they have changed from ignorance to knowledge, etc. Why then trust their current opinion as final?
This undermines any claim to certainty about the eternity of the world.
4
Apply to your own thinking
Reflect on how this argument shows that human intellect, without guidance, is unreliable in metaphysical matters.

Source Text

?

Refutation of Nature, Stars, and Multiple Makers
Maturidi refutes nature, star, and polytheistic explanations of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of those who attribute creation to nature, stars, or multiple gods, showing that these views are self-contradictory and stem from ignorance.

Core Idea
Attributing creation to nature, stars, or multiple gods is irrational and contradictory.

Maturidi now targets specific groups: those who explain existence through natures (ṭabāʾiʿ), those who attribute it to stars (nujūm), and those who believe in multiple creators. He argues that these theories are products of ignorance and blindness from the start, and they are internally contradictory. The adherents themselves are foolish followers of such principles. He ends with a rhetorical challenge: how can they claim any understanding of realities when their foundations are so flawed?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the three groups Maturidi mentions.
The groups: nature-worshippers, star-worshippers, polytheists.
2
Understand the refutation
Maturidi says these views originate in 'ignorance and blindness' and are self-contradictory. They cannot lead to knowledge.
3
Reflect on the concluding question
The final rhetorical question ('how can they have intellect...?) exposes the absurdity of their claims.
This is a powerful closing.
4
Connect to modern parallels
Consider how naturalism and scientism today resemble the nature-worshippers' view. The critique remains relevant.

He then attacks the stubbornness of atheists (mulḥidūn) who claim the eternity of entities (qidam al-aʿyān), relying only on blind imitation (taqlīd) or their own inability to conceive creation from nothing. He points out that everyone experiences personal change from ignorance to knowledge, weakness to power, need to independence—so they should not trust their own opinions over revelation. He then refutes those who attribute creation to natures (ṭabāʾiʿ), stars (nujūm), or multiple makers, noting that these views originate in ignorance and blindness. Their claims are contradictory and they themselves are foolish followers. Maturidi questions how such people can claim intellect or understanding of realities while their principles are ignorant. The passage ends with a rhetorical challenge.

Key Terms
طبائع (ṭabāʾiʿ)
نجوم (nujūm)
تقليد (taqlīd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nature or stars can create without intelligence.
Correction: Maturidi argues that unconscious entities cannot produce purposeful order.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the origin of those who believe in multiple makers?
A) Reason and evidence
B) Ignorance and blindness
C) Divine revelation
D) Philosophical inquiry

Key Takeaway
All explanations for existence besides the one Creator are ultimately rooted in ignorance and contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see order in nature—the seasons, your own body—do you ever unconsciously slip into thinking 'nature did this' instead of 'Allah created this'? What does this passage say about the intelligence of that slip?
?
Are there beliefs about existence that you hold simply because you inherited them from family or culture, without examining their contradictions? Maturidi calls such imitation foolish. Which of your unexamined beliefs would he challenge?
?
This passage claims that those who attribute creation to anything other than the one Creator are 'foolish ignoramuses'. Does that description sit uncomfortably with you? Where in your heart do you need to root out any subtle association of partners with Allah—whether in your trust, your fear, or your gratitude?

Refuting the Dualist Denial of Creation from Nothing
Maturidi refutes dualists who deny creation from nothing due to conceptual difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the dualists' own position is equally inconceivable, and why creation from nothing is rationally defensible.

Core Idea
What is inconceivable for one cannot be proof against it for another.

Maturidi confronts a common rational objection to creation ex nihilo: that it is inconceivable. In this passage, he turns the tables, showing that the dualists' own metaphysical principles are no more conceivable, and that empirical evidence supports creation from nothing. This lesson unpacks his layered argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dualists' claim: creation from nothing escapes conceptualization. This is their reason for denial.
The passage begins by stating this directly.
2
Analyze Maturidi's counter-argument
Maturidi points out that the dualists' own principles (like light and darkness as eternal) are equally inconceivable in the imagination. If they reject one for inconceivability, they should reject their own.
He also notes that the empirical fact of humans coming from non-existence supports creation from nothing.
3
Reflect on the theological conclusion
Maturidi concludes that if the dualists knew Allah's attributes (self-sufficiency, power, transcendence), they would not be troubled by their inability to grasp the wisdom in creation. The passage ends with reliance on Allah.
This step shows that the argument is not merely intellectual but rooted in tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما على أن الذي دعا الثنوية إلى إنكار شيء من لا شيء خروجه عن التصور في العقول، أو تقديرهم في تعرف الحكمة في العقل بما عاينوا بينهم. ولو علموا أن القول بمادئ العالم على ما عندهم - في الخروج من التصور في الوهم - مثل الذي أنكروا، أو [علموا] خروج معهم من الروح والعقل والحواس أو خروج حكمهم عن التصور في الوهم لما أنكروا. ثم لو علموا أنهم شهدوا فعل الضعفاء الجهال بأنفسهم، على ما علموا بالخبر أنهم كانوا [معدومين] ثم كانوا، لعلموا أن [كون] الأشياء من غير شيء أحق أن ينسب إلى / من به جملة العالم. ثم لو علموا غناه وقدرته وتعاليه عن صفة الخلق لم تضق قلوبهم عند قصورها عن درك الحكمة في خلقه. وعلى الله نتوكل، وبه نستعين.
What is the matter that what led the dualists to deny something from nothing is its exiting from conceptualization in intellects, or their evaluation in recognizing wisdom in the intellect by what they observed among themselves. And if they knew that the statement about the principles of the world according to what they have – in exiting from conceptualization in the imagination – is like what they denied, or [they knew] the exiting with them from the spirit and intellect and senses, or the exiting of their judgment from conceptualization in the imagination, they would not have denied. Then if they knew that they witnessed the act of the weak ignorant by themselves, according to what they knew by report that they were [non-existent] then came to be, they would know that the [existence] of things from nothing is more worthy of being attributed to the One by whom the entirety of the world exists. Then if they knew His self-sufficiency, power, and transcendence beyond the attribute of creation, their hearts would not narrow at their incapacity to grasp the wisdom in His creation. And upon Allah we rely, and from Him we seek help.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists who deny creation ex nihilo because they cannot conceive it. Maturidi first identifies the cause: the idea of something coming from nothing escapes conceptualization in intellects. The dualists rely on observed empirical wisdom, but their own positing of eternal principles (like light and darkness) is equally inconceivable in the imagination. He further argues that even in our empirical world, we observe weak ignorant people who were non-existent and then came to be; this shows that existence from nothing is more fittingly attributed to the Creator of the entire world. Finally, if they had known Allah's self-sufficiency, power, and transcendence beyond creaturely attributes, their inability to comprehend the wisdom in creation would not distress them. The passage concludes with a formula of reliance on Allah.

Key Terms
الثنوية (dualists)
تصور (conceptualization)
وهم (imagination)
مادئ العالم (principles of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think creation from nothing is logically impossible because it cannot be conceived.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the alternative (eternal principles) is equally inconceivable, and empirical evidence supports creation from nothing.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do the dualists deny creation from nothing?
A) Because they have observed it is impossible
B) Because it escapes conceptualization in intellects
C) Because they rely on divine revelation
D) Because they accept the eternity of matter

Key Takeaway
Creation from nothing is not less conceivable than the dualists' own eternal principles, and empirical evidence supports it as fitting for the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth about Allah that your mind cannot fully grasp—like creation from nothing—do you instinctively doubt it, or do you accept it based on His unlimited power? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Him?
?
Have you ever judged Allah's actions or decrees by your limited imagination, thinking something is impossible or unjust? How can you train yourself to submit to His wisdom even when you see no reason?
?
Is there a specific area in your life where you rely on your own reasoning over divine revelation? Will you today repent from that and say, 'I believe as it came from Allah, even if I do not understand'?

The Finitude of the Mediator Proves the Oneness of God
Maturidi argues that the mediator's finitude proves its subordination and the oneness of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the mediator being finite proves that the true controller is one, and how this refutes dualism.

Core Idea
A finite mediator cannot be divine; it points to a single infinite designer.

Maturidi continues his refutation of dualism by focusing on the mediator, a figure that dualists claimed stands between light and darkness. He demonstrates that the mediator's finitude implies its subordination to a higher power, leading to the conclusion that the true deity is one. This lesson walks through his logical arguments from finitude, analogy, and the impossibility of self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the argument. Note the emphasis on 'finite' and 'infinite'.
2
Identify the claim about finitude
Notice that Maturidi states the mediator is finite while the other two principles are infinite. Ask yourself why finitude would be a problem for divinity.
The finite is subordinate to the infinite, like the short compared to the tall.
3
Follow the analogy of human life
Maturidi uses the example of a human's life (the 'mediator' in the body) to show that the one who designs and uses the body is not the life itself but something else. This points to a single designer.
The analogy reinforces that the true designer is one.
4
Understand the critique of mixture
He criticizes the dualist notion of mixture as an error, arguing that no human can design their own origin or fix what goes wrong. This establishes that the designer is one and distinct.
He then shows that creating mixture and separation are both acts of a single creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : جعلوا الواسط متناهيا والآخرين غير متناهيين / والمتناهي تحت غير المتناهي؛ لأنه كالمقصر عن تمام ما ليس بمتناه [و] كالقصير من الطويل . والإنسان إن كانت الحياة التي في البدن فهي مُحِسة للبدن مستعملة له فيجب أن يكون الواسط هو الذي له تدبير العالي والسافل، وهو المستعمل لهما، فيصير الإله في الحقيقة واحدا ويبطل الامتزاج وما ذكر من الخيال. ثم إشارته إلى الامتزاج - وهي حياته ـ خطأ ؛ إذ لا إنسان يعرف تدبير ابتدائه، ولا أصلح ما فسد منه، ولا دَفَع ما حل به ؛ ثبت أن المدبر واحد، وهو غير الذي ذكر، وأن الذي ذكر تحت تدبير الواحد. ثم لا فرق بين أن يحدث مزاج لم يكن، لا عن أصل هو امتزاج، وبين أن يحدث بإبانة لم تكن، لا عن أصل البينونة. ثم لا فرق بين إمكان تغيّر قد تم إلى احتمال الحوادث ـ بعد أن لم يكن كذلك ـ بقدرة قادر وبين أن تكون الحوادث به ، لا بقلب القديم إلى معنى الحديث ، إذ هما جميعا في البعد عن البصر والوهم واحد . وبالله المعونة والنجاة.
Then he said: They made the mediator finite and the other two infinite, and the finite is under the infinite, because it is like the deficient in relation to the completion of what is not finite, [and] like the short compared to the tall. And if the human is the life which is in the body, then it is sensing the body and using it, so it must be that the mediator is the one who has the design of the high and the low, and it is the user of both, so the deity becomes in reality one and the mixture and what is mentioned of fancy are invalidated. Then his reference to the mixture—which is his life—is an error; for no human knows the design of his origin, nor rectifies what is corrupted from it, nor repels what befalls it. It is established that the designer is one, and He is other than what was mentioned, and that what was mentioned is under the design of the One. Then there is no difference between a mixture occurring that was not, not from an original that is mixture, and between a separation occurring that was not, not from an original of separation. Then there is no difference between the possibility of a change that was completed to the capacity for accidents—after it was not like that—by the power of a powerful one, and between that accidents are by Him, not by turning the eternal into the meaning of the temporal, since both are equally distant from sight and imagination. And with God is assistance and deliverance.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation of dualism, focusing on the mediator (al-wasit). Maturidi notes that the dualists made the mediator finite while the other two (light and darkness) are infinite. The finite is subordinate to the infinite, like the short to the tall. He then draws an analogy: if a human is the life in the body, which senses and uses the body, then the mediator must be the one that designs (tadbīr) the high and low, making it the user of both. This implies that the true deity is one, invalidating the mixture and the fanciful talk. Next, Maturidi criticizes the reference to mixture as 'life': it is an error because no human knows the design of his own origin, cannot fix what goes wrong, nor repel afflictions. This proves that the designer is one and distinct from the supposed mediator. He then argues that there is no difference between creating a mixture from nothing and creating a separation from nothing; both require a creator. Similarly, there is no difference between attributing change and accidents to a powerful agent and attributing them directly to Him without transforming the eternal into the temporal—both possibilities are equally beyond sensory perception and imagination. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine help and deliverance.

Key Terms
متناهي
غير متناهي
مدبر
امتزاج
إبانة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mediator's finitude means it is small in size or less important.
Correction: Finitude here refers to being limited in power and scope, making it dependent on a higher infinite controller.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about the mediator being finite?
A) It is divine and eternal
B) It is under a higher controller, proving the oneness of God
C) It is a third independent entity
D) It is non-existent

Key Takeaway
The finitude of the mediator proves that it cannot be the ultimate controller, affirming that the true God is one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life are you unconsciously trusting a finite mediator—your intellect, your wealth, your social standing—to give you ultimate security, when this lesson insists that only the infinite God has true design and control?
?
If you truly believed that every finite thing is subordinate to the One Designer, how would your daily decisions about time, money, and relationships change starting tomorrow?
?
Maturidi says no human knows the design of his origin, rectifies what is corrupted, or repels afflictions. When was the last time you desperately tried to fix something beyond your control, and will you now surrender it to the sole Designer?

Refutation of the Dualist Intermediary Argument
Maturidi refutes dualist claim about the intermediary and mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that an intermediary between two eternal principles implies multiple creators.

Core Idea
Mixture and separation are equally unable to explain creation—only a single Manager suffices.

Imam Maturidi here confronts a dualist argument that posits an intermediary between two eternal principles. He systematically shows that such an intermediary cannot be finite while the others are infinite, and that the very notion of mixture fails to justify duality. This passage sharpens the Islamic understanding of tawhid by demolishing alternative metaphysical frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of Maturidi's argument.
Pay attention to the flow of the argument from the intermediary to the conclusion.
2
Identify the dualist claim
The dualists claim the intermediary is finite while the other two principles are infinite. Note how Maturidi points out the contradiction: a finite thing under an infinite is deficient.
3
Follow Maturidi's life-in-body analogy
Maturidi compares the intermediary to life in a body: it senses and uses the body, implying it manages both higher and lower realms. This leads to a single god.
This invalidates the mixture theory.
4
Understand the final indifference arguments
Maturidi argues there is no difference between mixture and separation, and no difference between change by power and accidents occurring without changing the eternal. Both are equally impossible in terms of divine action.
The conclusion is that the manager is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : جعلوا الواسط متناهيا والآخرين غير متناهيين والمتناهي تحت غير المتناهي؛ لأنه كالمقصر عن تمام ما ليس بمتناه [و]كالقصير من الطويل . والإنسان إن كان الحياة التي في البدن فهي محسة للبدن مستعملة له فيجب أن يكون الواسط هو الذي له تدبير العالي والسافل، وهو المستعمل لهما، فيصير الإله في الحقيقة واحدا ويبطل الامتزاج وما ذكر من الخيال. ثم إشارته إلى الامتزاج - وهي حياته ـ خطأ ؛ إذ لا إنسان يعرف تدبير ابتدائه، ولا أصلح ما فسد منه، ولا دفع ما حل به ؛ ثبت أن المدبر واحد، وهو غير الذي ذكر، وأن الذي ذكر تحت تدبير الواحد. ثم لا فرق بين أن يحدث مزاج لم يكن، لا عن أصل هو امتزاج، وبين أن يحدث بإبانة لم تكن، لا عن أصل البينونة. ثم لا فرق بين إمكان تغير قد تم إلى احتمال الحوادث ـ بعد أن لم يكن كذلك ـ بقدرة قادر وبين أن تكون الحوادث به، لا بقلب القديم إلى معنى الحديث، إذ هما جميعا في البعد عن البصر والوهم واحد . وبالله المعونة والنجاة.
Then he said: They made the intermediary finite and the others infinite, and the finite is under the infinite; because it is like the deficient compared to the completeness of what is not finite, and like the short compared to the tall. And if man is the life that is in the body, then it is that which senses the body and uses it, so it must be that the intermediary is the one who has the management of the higher and the lower, and he is the one who uses them both, so the god in reality becomes one, and the mixture and what was mentioned of illusion are invalidated. Then his indication of mixture—that being his life—is error; for no man knows the management of its beginning, nor does he rectify what has become corrupted from it, nor does he repel what has befallen it. So it is established that the manager is one, and he is other than the one mentioned, and that the one mentioned is under the management of the One. Then there is no difference between a mixture coming to be that was not, without an origin that is mixture, and its coming to be by a separation that was not, without an origin of separation. Then there is no difference between the possibility of a change that has been completed to the capacity for accidents—after not being so—by the power of a powerful one, and between accidents being by it, not by changing the eternal into the meaning of the temporal; for both are one in remoteness from sight and illusion. And with God is assistance and salvation.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a dualist argument that posits an intermediary between two eternal principles. Maturidi begins by pointing out that dualists claim the intermediary is finite while the other two (light and darkness?) are infinite. But a finite thing under an infinite is deficient and subordinate. Then he considers the possibility that the intermediary is like the life in a body: it senses and uses the body. If so, the intermediary must be the one that manages both the higher and lower realms, implying a single manager—God—and invalidating the mixture theory. He then argues that no human knows the management of the beginning of things or can fix corruption, so the true manager must be one. He concludes by showing that whether something comes into existence by mixture or by separation, both are equally impossible in terms of divine action, so the dualist's distinction fails. The argument affirms that God's power alone brings about events, not any change in His eternal attributes.

Key Terms
الواسط
الامتزاج
المدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of mixture or composition in the world necessitates two creators.
Correction: Maturidi shows that mixture does not imply duality because mixture and separation are indifferent and both point to a single manager.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what does the fact that no human knows the management of his own beginning or can rectify his corruption prove?
A) The world has multiple managers
B) The manager is one and not the being described by dualists
C) The intermediary is eternal
D) Mixture is a valid explanation for creation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that the supposed intermediary is finite and subordinate, and that mixture and separation are equally unable to explain creation, pointing to a single Manager.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When confusion or disorder enters your life, do you instinctively attribute it to multiple causes rather than entrusting it to the One Manager? What conscious steps can you take today to hand over every affair to Him alone?
?
The passage humbles us: 'no man knows the management of its beginning.' How does this truth challenge your desire to control outcomes? What is one area you need to surrender control of right now?
?
If mixture and separation are both equally in need of a single Creator, what excuse do you have for attributing any blessing or trial to luck, nature, or your own effort instead of to Allah? How can you weave this lesson into your daily shahada?
